Four Meanings of Mufrad and the Logical Sense
Commentator distinguishes four meanings of 'mufrad' and specifies the intended sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intended meaning of 'mufrad' in a logical text and distinguish it from three other possible meanings.

Core Idea
Mufrad in logic is broader than a single word: it includes any non-sentence expression, even compound terms.

In logic and other disciplines, terms can have multiple meanings. The word 'mufrad' is a prime example. Before diving into the five universals and definitions, the commentator clarifies exactly what 'mufrad' means in this book, so that you don't confuse it with its grammatical or numerical senses. Let's walk through the four meanings and pin down the one that governs the rest of this chapter.

Guided Path
1
List the four meanings of 'mufrad'
Read the Arabic passage and identify the four distinct uses of 'mufrad' mentioned: (1) opposite of dual/plural (numerical singular), (2) opposite of annexed (grammatical status), (3) opposite of compound (in the sense of morphological composition), (4) opposite of a complete sentence (syntactic completeness).
2
Understand the context of each meaning
For each meaning, recall which science or context it belongs to: (1) grammar (number), (2) grammar (annexation), (3) morphology/word-structure, (4) logic/syntax (sentence vs. non-sentence). Note that meanings (1)-(3) are more specific, while (4) is the technical logical sense.
The commentator marks meaning (3) as coming later ('in the discussions of wording').
3
Identify the intended meaning based on evidence
The commentator explicitly states: 'And what is meant by mufradat here is this last meaning'. He also gives evidence: the author opposes the first article (on mufradat) to the second article (on propositions). Therefore, mufradat must be everything that is not a proposition. This excludes meanings (1)-(3) because they would not cover all non-propositional expressions.
4
Apply the intended meaning to include the five universals and definitions
Once you accept meaning (4), note that it includes 'restrictive compounds' such as 'the rational animal'. The five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are expressed as restrictive compounds (e.g., 'animal' is a genus, 'rational' is a differentia). Likewise, definitions (hadd and rasm) are restrictive compounds. Thus, they all belong under 'mufradat' in this article.
This shows why the first article covers topics that might seem to include phrases, not just single words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يطلق «المفرد» ويراد به ما يقابل المثنى والمجموع - أعني الواحد - وقد يطلق ويراد به ما يقابل المضاف فيقال: هذا مفرد أي ليس بمضاف - وقد يطلق على ما يقابل المركب - وسيأتي في مباحث الألفاظ - وقد يطلق على ما يقابل الجملة، فيقال: هذا مفرد أي ليس بجملة، وهو بهذا المعنى الأخير يتناول المركبات التقييدية أيضاً. والمراد بالمفردات هاهنا هو هذا المعنى الأخير، فيندرج فيها الكليات الخمس والتعريفات أيضاً لأنها مركبات تقييدية، والدليل على ذلك أنه قد جعل المفردات في مقابلة القضايا حيث قال: «المقالة الثانية في القضايا» (شريف).
The term 'mufrad' (singular) may be used to mean what is opposed to dual and plural—that is, the one. And it may be used to mean what is opposed to the annexed (mudaf), so it is said: this is mufrad, i.e., not annexed. And it may be used for what is opposed to the compound (murakkab)—and it will come in the discussions of wording—and it may be used for what is opposed to the sentence (jumla), so it is said: this is mufrad, i.e., not a sentence. And by this last meaning, it includes restrictive compounds as well. And what is meant by 'mufradat' here is this last meaning, so the five universals and also definitions are included under it because they are restrictive compounds. The evidence for that is that he placed 'mufradat' in opposition to 'qadaya' where he said: 'The second article is on propositions' (Sharif).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the term 'mufrad' (singular) in the context of the book. The commentator lists four possible meanings: (1) as opposed to dual and plural, meaning the numerical singular; (2) as opposed to the annexed (in Arabic grammar, mufrad vs. mudaf); (3) as opposed to compound (in the sense of composite words or phrases); (4) as opposed to a complete sentence (jumla). The intended meaning in this book is the fourth: mufrad means any expression that is not a complete sentence. This includes not only single words but also restrictive compounds like 'al-insan al-katib' (the writing man). Therefore, the first article on mufradat includes the five universals (kulliyat khams) and definitions (ta'rifat) because they are restrictive compounds. The evidence is that the author himself opposes the first article (on mufradat) to the second article (on propositions). So mufradat here means everything that is not a proposition.

Key Takeaway
The logical sense of 'mufrad' is 'any expression that is not a complete sentence', which includes both single words and restrictive compounds like the five universals and definitions.

Essential Accidents: The Sixfold Classification and Refinement
Classification and definition of essential accident with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify accidents into six types and identify which are essential according to the author's refined definition.

Core Idea
An essential accident attaches for the essence or for an equal — not via anything more general.

This lesson unpacks how accidents are categorized based on their mode of attachment to a subject. The author presents a standard sixfold classification, then refines it by excluding attachment via a more general category from the definition of essential accident. Understanding this refinement is key to precise definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the classification framework
Notice the three initial examples: wonder attaches to man for his essence, voluntary movement via a part (animality), laughter via an external equal (wonder). The general classification follows: attachment is either for the essence, for a part, or for an external thing (which can be equal, more general, more specific, or disjoint).
2
Identify the three essential categories
The author states that the first three (essence, part, equal) are called 'essential accidents' because they trace back to the essence. Understand that attachment for the essence and for a part are clear; attachment via an external equal is also essential if the equal is not more general.
3
Examine the critique of later scholars
The later scholars included attachment via a more general part as essential. The author rejects this, asserting that only the three above are essential. Compare the two positions.
4
Understand the intermediary condition
Essential accidents have no existential intermediary between them and the subject, but knowledge of their existence may require a proof. This is a metaphysical point about dependence.
5
Apply the refined definition
Test given accidents: e.g., 'voluntary movement' attaches via animality (part) – essential; 'laughter' via wonder (equal) – essential; 'mortality' via being an animal (more general) – non-essential according to the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالتعجب اللاحق لذات الإنسان - أو تلحق الشيء الجزئه - كالحركة بالإرادة اللاحقة للإنسان بواسطة أنه حيوان - أو تلحقه بواسطة أمر خارج عنه مساو له - كالضحك العارض للإنسان بواسطة التعجب. العرض الذاتي وغير الذاتي] والتفسير هناك أن العوارض ست : لأن ما يعرض للشيء إما أن يكون عروضه لذاته أو لجزئه أو لأمر خارج عنه، والأمر الخارج عن المعروض إما مساو له أو أعم منه أو أخص منه أو مبائن له؛ فالثلاثة الأول العارض لذات المعروض والعارض لجزئه والعارض للمساوي - وهي تسمّى «أعراضاً ذاتية لاستنادها إلى ذات المعروض. تلحق الشيء للأمر الذي هو - أي ذلك الأمر - هو ، أي ذلك الشيء، وحاصله تلحق الشيء لذاته (شريف). (1) فان قلت : العارض للشيء مايكون محمولاً عليه خارجاً عنه، والتعجب ليس محمولاً على الإنسان ؟ أجيب بأنهم يتسامحون في العبارات كثيراً فيذكرون مبدء المحمول - كالتعجب والنطق والضحك والكتابة وغيرها - ويريدون بها المحمولات المشتقة منها. واعلم أن العوارض التي تلحق الأشياء لذواتها لا يكون بينها وبين تلك الأشياء واسطة في ثبوتها لها بحسب نفس الأمر، وأما العلم بثبوتها لها بحسب نفس الأمر ، فربما يحتاج إلى برهان (شريف). (٢ طريقة المتأخرين أنهم يجعلون اللاحق بواسطة الجزء الأعم من الأعراض الذاتية التي يبحث عنها في العلوم، وليست بصحيحة، بل الحق أن الأعراض الذاتية ما يلحق الشيء لذاته أو لما يساويه، سواء كان جزء له أو خارجاً عنه (شريف).
Like wonder attaching to the essence of man—or it attaches to the part of the thing—like voluntary movement attaching to man by virtue of his being an animal—or it attaches by virtue of an external thing equal to it—like laughter that occurs to man by virtue of wonder. The essential and non-essential accident. And the detail there is that the accidents are six: because what attaches to a thing is either its attachment is for its essence, or for its part, or for an external thing. The external thing relative to the subject is either equal to it, or more general, or more specific, or disjoint from it. So the first three: the accident for the essence of the subject, the accident for its part, and the accident for the equal—these are called 'essential accidents' because they are based on the essence of the subject. They attach to the thing for the matter that is—i.e., that matter—it is, i.e., that thing; the summary is that they attach to the thing for its essence (al-Sharif). (1) If you say: the accident of a thing is what is predicated of it external to it, and wonder is not predicated of man? The answer is that they are very lenient in expressions; they mention the source of the predicate—like wonder, speech, laughter, writing, etc.—and intend by them the derived predicates. And know that the accidents that attach to things for their essences have no intermediary between them and those things in their establishment according to the matter itself; but knowledge of their establishment according to the matter itself may require a proof (al-Sharif). (2) The method of the later scholars is that they make the accident that attaches via the more general part among the essential accidents that are investigated in the sciences, but this is not correct; rather, the truth is that essential accidents are those that attach to the thing for its essence or for what is equal to it, whether it is a part of it or external to it (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of accidents (aʿrāḍ) and defines which are 'essential' (dhātiyya). It begins by giving examples of how an accident may attach to a thing: for itself (like wonder attaching to man), via a part (like voluntary movement attaching to man via his being an animal), or via an external equal (like laughter attaching to man via wonder). Then a classification is given: accidents are six types because the attachment is either for the essence, for a part, or for an external thing. The external thing can be equal, more general, more specific, or disjoint. The first three (essence, part, equal) are called essential accidents because they trace back to the essence of the subject. The commentator al-Sharif adds a note: an objection is raised that wonder is not predicated of man, but the answer is that they use the source term loosely to mean the derived relation. Then he clarifies that essential accidents have no intermediary in their existence, though proving their existence may require a proof. Finally, a critique is given: the later scholars include accidents that attach via a more general part as essential, but the author asserts the truth is that only those attaching for the essence or for what is equal are essential, whether the equal is part of the thing or external. This refines the definition and excludes the 'via a more general' case.

Key Terms
عارض
عرض ذاتي
عرض غير ذاتي

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered an essential accident according to the author's final position in this passage?
A) An accident attaching to a thing for its essence, like wonder to man.
B) An accident attaching to a thing via a part of it, like voluntary movement via animality.
C) An accident attaching to a thing via an external thing more general than it, like laughter via wonder (assuming wonder is more general).
D) An accident attaching to a thing via an external thing equal to it, like laughter via wonder (assuming equality).

Key Takeaway
Essential accidents are those that attach to a thing for its essence or for what is equal to it; attachment via a more general is not essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing Particular and Universal Terms in Logic
Defines particular and universal terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a term is a real particular (juz'i haqiqi) or a universal (kulli) based on its capacity for predication of many.

Core Idea
A term's logical classification depends on whether it can be predicated of many or only one.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational division of terms based on their meaning: either particularized to one individual or fit for predication of many. This distinction is crucial for all subsequent logical analysis, as universals are the building blocks of definitions and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for particularity
Find where the passage states that if the meaning is one and particularized (mutashakhkhas) so that it cannot be said of many, it is a real particular. Look for the phrase 'lam yasluh li-an yugala 'ala kathirin'.
The example given is 'Zayd', a specific person.
2
Recognize the term 'juz'i haqiqi'
Note that logicians call this a real particular. Grammarians call it a proper noun (alam). Understand that it applies to exactly one individual.
3
Identify the criterion for universality
If the meaning is not particularized and can be predicated of many, it is a universal (kulli). The many are its individuals (afrad).
4
Apply the criterion to examples
Test whether 'insan' (human) or 'shams' (sun) can be predicated of many. 'Insan' applies to all humans (external individuals). 'Shams' applies to multiple mental concepts (since there is only one sun externally but can be conceived multiply).
Mental individuals count as individuals.
5
Check for common misunderstanding
Do not restrict 'individuals' to external existence only. The passage includes mental individuals, as shown by the sun example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الألفاظ
أن يكون معناه واحداً أو كثيراً، فإن كان الأول - أي إن كان معناه واحداً - فإما أن يتشخص ذلك المعنى - أي لم يصلح لأن يكون مقولاً على كثيرين - أو لم يتشخص - أي يصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين : فإن تشخص ذلك المعنى ولم يصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين كـ«زيد» يسمّى «عَلَماً» في عرف النحاة، لأنه علامة دالة على شخص معين و جزئياً حقيقياً» في عرف المنطقيين. وإن لم يتشخص وصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين فهو «الكلي» والكثيرون أفراده. فلا يخلو إما أن يكون حصوله في أفراده الذهنية والخارجية على السوية، أولا ؛ فإن تساوت الأفراد الذهنية والخارجية في حصوله وصدقه عليها يسمى «متواطئاً» لأنّ أفراده متوافقة في معناه، من «التواطؤ» وهو التوافق كالإنسان والشمس، فإن الإنسان له أفراد في الخارج وصدقه عليها بالسوية، والشمس لها أفراد في الذهن وصدقها عليها أيضاً بالسوية. وإن لم تتساو الأفراد، بل كان حصوله في بعضها أولى أو أقدم أو أشد من البعض الآخر يسمّى «مشككاً» والتشكيك على ثلاثة أوجه : التشكيك بالأولوية : وهو اختلاف الأفراد في الأولوية وعدمها،كالوجود، فإنّه في الواجب أتم وأثبت وأقوى منه في الممكن. والتشكيك بالتقدم والتأخر : هو أن يكون حصول معناه في بعض الأفراد متقدماً على حصوله في البعض الآخر، كالوجود أيضاً، فإنّ حصوله في الواجب قبل حصوله في الممكن. والتشكيك بالشدّة والضعف : وهو أن يكون حصول معناه في بعضها أشد من حصوله في البعض الآخر، كالوجود أيضاً، فإنه في الواجب أشدّ من الممكن، لأن آثار الوجود في وجود الواجب أكثر، كما أن أثر البياض - وهو تفريق البصر - في بياض الثلج أكثر مما هو في بياض العاج. وإنما سمى «مشككاً» لأنّ أفراده مشتركة في أصل معناه ومختلفة بأحد الوجوه الثلاثة، فالناظر إليه إن نظر إلى جهة الاشتراك خيله أنه متواطئ التوافق أفراده فيه، وإن نظر إلى جهة الاختلاف أوهمه أنه مشترك، كأنه لفظ له معان مختلفة - كالعين - فالناظر فيه يتشكك هل هو متواطئ أو مشترك، فلهذا سمي بهذا الاسم.
Topics of words. Its meaning is either one or many. If the first - i.e., its meaning is one - then either that meaning is particularized - i.e., it is not fit to be predicated of many - or not particularized - i.e., it is fit to be predicated of many. If that meaning is particularized and not fit to be predicated of many, like 'Zayd', it is called a 'proper noun' in the terminology of grammarians, because it is a sign indicating a specific person, and a 'real particular' in the terminology of logicians. And if it is not particularized and is fit to be predicated of many, then it is the 'universal', and the many are its individuals. Then it is either such that its occurrence in its mental and external individuals is equal, or not. If the mental and external individuals are equal in its occurrence and truth of predication upon them, it is called 'univocal', because its individuals agree in its meaning, from 'al-tawatu' meaning agreement, like 'human' and 'sun'. For 'human' has individuals in the external world and its predication upon them is equal, and 'sun' has individuals in the mind and its predication upon them is also equal. And if the individuals are not equal, but rather its occurrence in some of them is prior, or earlier, or stronger than in others, it is called 'equivocal' (mushakkik). And equivocity is of three types: equivocity by priority: which is the difference of individuals in priority and its absence, like existence, for it is more perfect, more established, and stronger in the Necessary than in the contingent. Equivocity by anteriority and posteriority: which is that the occurrence of its meaning in some individuals is prior to its occurrence in others, like existence as well, for its occurrence in the Necessary is before its occurrence in the contingent. Equivocity by intensity and weakness: which is that the occurrence of its meaning in some is more intense than its occurrence in others, like existence as well, for it is more intense in the Necessary than in the contingent, because the effects of existence in the existence of the Necessary are more, just as the effect of whiteness - which is the dispersion of sight - in the whiteness of snow is more than in the whiteness of ivory. And it is only called 'mushakkik' because its individuals share in the root of its meaning and differ in one of the three aspects. So the observer, if he looks at the aspect of sharing, imagines it to be univocal with its individuals agreeing in it, and if he looks at the aspect of difference, it suggests to him that it is ambiguous (mushtarak), like a word with different meanings - like 'ayn' - so the observer doubts whether it is univocal or ambiguous, hence it is named with this name.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the meanings of words as they relate to logical analysis. The author begins by dividing the meaning of a word: either it is one or many. If the meaning is one, it may be particularized (i.e., applies only to a single individual) or not. The particularized is called 'alam' (proper noun) by grammarians and 'juz'i haqiqi' (real particular) by logicians; an example is 'Zayd', which denotes one specific person. If the meaning is not particularized and can be predicated of many, it is called 'kulli' (universal), and the many are its individuals. The author then explains that every universal is either such that its occurrence in its individuals (both mental and external) is equal (sawiyya) or not. If equal, it is 'mutawati' (univocal), because its individuals share equally in its meaning; example: 'insan' (human) applies equally to all individual humans in the external world, and 'shams' (sun) applies equally to all mental individuals (i.e., instances of the concept). If not equal, that is, the meaning applies to some individuals with priority (awlawiyya), or anteriority (taqaddum wa ta'akhkhur), or intensity (shidda wa du'f), then it is called 'mushakkik' (equivocal or analogical). The author gives three types: (1) priority: where the meaning is more perfect in some individuals, like existence (wujud) is more perfect in the Necessary Being (God) than in contingent beings; (2) anteriority/posteriority: where existence is prior in the Necessary than in the contingent; (3) intensity/weakness: where existence is stronger in the Necessary, because its effects are more manifest, analogous to whiteness being more intense in snow than in ivory. Finally, the author explains the name 'mushakkik' (from shakk = doubt): because the observer, seeing that the individuals share in the root meaning, may think it is univocal, but noticing the difference, may suspect it is ambiguous (like a homonym). Thus, the term indicates a state of doubt between univocity and ambiguity.

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي (juz'i haqiqi): A particular that applies to only one individual, like 'Zayd'; it cannot be predicated of many. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard in post-Avicennan logic.]
كلي (kulli): A universal that can be predicated of many individuals, like 'human'. [Note: Stable term across Islamic logic.]
متواطئ (mutawati'): A universal whose meaning applies equally to all its individuals, like 'human' to all humans. [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
مشكك (mushakkik): A universal whose meaning applies to individuals with priority, anteriority, or intensity, causing doubt whether it is univocal or ambiguous. [Note: Stable; sometimes translated as 'analogical' in English.]
التشكيك بالأولوية (al-tashkik bi al-awlawiyya): Type of equivocity where the meaning is more perfect or prior in some individuals, e.g., existence in God vs. contingent. [Note: Specific classification within tashkik; found in standard logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a real particular (juz'i haqiqi)?
A) It can be predicated of many individuals.
B) Its meaning is particularized so it cannot be predicated of many.
C) It is a proper noun only.
D) It is synonymous with universal.

Key Takeaway
The first step in logical analysis is to determine whether a term is a real particular (one individual) or a universal (many individuals), based on its capacity for predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as a real particular or a universal: 'Allah', 'al-insan', 'hadha al-rajul', 'al-wujūd'. Explain your reasoning based on the passage's criteria.

Univocal and Equivocal (Mushakkik) Universals: Three Types of Tashkik
Divides universal into univocal and equivocal with three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between univocal (mutawati') and equivocal (mushakkik) universals, and identify which of the three types of tashkik (priority, anteriority, intensity) applies to a given example.

Core Idea
A universal's meaning may apply equally or unequally to its individuals, creating the distinction between univocity and equivocity with three modalities.

Having established universals, the author now divides them based on equality of occurrence in their individuals. If equal, it is univocal (mutawati'); if unequal, it is equivocal (mushakkik) with three types: priority, anteriority/posteriority, and intensity/weakness. The paradigmatic example is 'existence' (wujud) as applied to God and contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion for univocity
If the universal's occurrence (husul) in its mental and external individuals is equal (ala sawiyya), it is mutawati'. Example: 'insan' applies equally to all humans.
Equality means the meaning is predicated without any difference in priority, anteriority, or intensity.
2
Understand the criterion for equivocity
If not equal, i.e., the meaning is more perfect, prior, or stronger in some individuals, it is mushakkik.
3
Learn the three types of tashkik
(a) Priority (awlawiyya): meaning is more perfect/established in some. (b) Anteriority/posteriority: meaning occurs earlier in some. (c) Intensity/weakness: meaning is stronger in some. All three are illustrated with existence (wujud) as applied to God and contingent beings.
The passage also gives the example of whiteness in snow vs. ivory for intensity.
4
Understand why it is called mushakkik
The name comes from doubt (shakk): the observer may doubt whether it is univocal (due to shared root meaning) or ambiguous (mushtarak) due to the difference. It is not a homonym but a single meaning with varying strength.
5
Apply to Islamic theological examples
Consider terms like 'alim' (knowing) applied to God and humans. Does it have priority, anteriority, or intensity? Reflect on divine attributes.
This helps in understanding tashbih and tanzih.

مباحث الألفاظ
أن يكون معناه واحداً أو كثيراً، فإن كان الأول - أي إن كان معناه واحداً - فإما أن يتشخص ذلك المعنى - أي لم يصلح لأن يكون مقولاً على كثيرين - أو لم يتشخص - أي يصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين : فإن تشخص ذلك المعنى ولم يصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين كـ«زيد» يسمّى «عَلَماً» في عرف النحاة، لأنه علامة دالة على شخص معين و جزئياً حقيقياً» في عرف المنطقيين. وإن لم يتشخص وصلح لأن يقال على كثيرين فهو «الكلي» والكثيرون أفراده. فلا يخلو إما أن يكون حصوله في أفراده الذهنية والخارجية على السوية، أولا ؛ فإن تساوت الأفراد الذهنية والخارجية في حصوله وصدقه عليها يسمى «متواطئاً» لأنّ أفراده متوافقة في معناه، من «التواطؤ» وهو التوافق كالإنسان والشمس، فإن الإنسان له أفراد في الخارج وصدقه عليها بالسوية، والشمس لها أفراد في الذهن وصدقها عليها أيضاً بالسوية. وإن لم تتساو الأفراد، بل كان حصوله في بعضها أولى أو أقدم أو أشد من البعض الآخر يسمّى «مشككاً» والتشكيك على ثلاثة أوجه : التشكيك بالأولوية : وهو اختلاف الأفراد في الأولوية وعدمها،كالوجود، فإنّه في الواجب أتم وأثبت وأقوى منه في الممكن. والتشكيك بالتقدم والتأخر : هو أن يكون حصول معناه في بعض الأفراد متقدماً على حصوله في البعض الآخر، كالوجود أيضاً، فإنّ حصوله في الواجب قبل حصوله في الممكن. والتشكيك بالشدّة والضعف : وهو أن يكون حصول معناه في بعضها أشد من حصوله في البعض الآخر، كالوجود أيضاً، فإنه في الواجب أشدّ من الممكن، لأن آثار الوجود في وجود الواجب أكثر، كما أن أثر البياض - وهو تفريق البصر - في بياض الثلج أكثر مما هو في بياض العاج. وإنما سمى «مشككاً» لأنّ أفراده مشتركة في أصل معناه ومختلفة بأحد الوجوه الثلاثة، فالناظر إليه إن نظر إلى جهة الاشتراك خيله أنه متواطئ التوافق أفراده فيه، وإن نظر إلى جهة الاختلاف أوهمه أنه مشترك، كأنه لفظ له معان مختلفة - كالعين - فالناظر فيه يتشكك هل هو متواطئ أو مشترك، فلهذا سمي بهذا الاسم.
Topics of words. Its meaning is either one or many. If the first - i.e., its meaning is one - then either that meaning is particularized - i.e., it is not fit to be predicated of many - or not particularized - i.e., it is fit to be predicated of many. If that meaning is particularized and not fit to be predicated of many, like 'Zayd', it is called a 'proper noun' in the terminology of grammarians, because it is a sign indicating a specific person, and a 'real particular' in the terminology of logicians. And if it is not particularized and is fit to be predicated of many, then it is the 'universal', and the many are its individuals. Then it is either such that its occurrence in its mental and external individuals is equal, or not. If the mental and external individuals are equal in its occurrence and truth of predication upon them, it is called 'univocal', because its individuals agree in its meaning, from 'al-tawatu' meaning agreement, like 'human' and 'sun'. For 'human' has individuals in the external world and its predication upon them is equal, and 'sun' has individuals in the mind and its predication upon them is also equal. And if the individuals are not equal, but rather its occurrence in some of them is prior, or earlier, or stronger than in others, it is called 'equivocal' (mushakkik). And equivocity is of three types: equivocity by priority: which is the difference of individuals in priority and its absence, like existence, for it is more perfect, more established, and stronger in the Necessary than in the contingent. Equivocity by anteriority and posteriority: which is that the occurrence of its meaning in some individuals is prior to its occurrence in others, like existence as well, for its occurrence in the Necessary is before its occurrence in the contingent. Equivocity by intensity and weakness: which is that the occurrence of its meaning in some is more intense than its occurrence in others, like existence as well, for it is more intense in the Necessary than in the contingent, because the effects of existence in the existence of the Necessary are more, just as the effect of whiteness - which is the dispersion of sight - in the whiteness of snow is more than in the whiteness of ivory. And it is only called 'mushakkik' because its individuals share in the root of its meaning and differ in one of the three aspects. So the observer, if he looks at the aspect of sharing, imagines it to be univocal with its individuals agreeing in it, and if he looks at the aspect of difference, it suggests to him that it is ambiguous (mushtarak), like a word with different meanings - like 'ayn' - so the observer doubts whether it is univocal or ambiguous, hence it is named with this name.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the meanings of words as they relate to logical analysis. The author begins by dividing the meaning of a word: either it is one or many. If the meaning is one, it may be particularized (i.e., applies only to a single individual) or not. The particularized is called 'alam' (proper noun) by grammarians and 'juz'i haqiqi' (real particular) by logicians; an example is 'Zayd', which denotes one specific person. If the meaning is not particularized and can be predicated of many, it is called 'kulli' (universal), and the many are its individuals. The author then explains that every universal is either such that its occurrence in its individuals (both mental and external) is equal (sawiyya) or not. If equal, it is 'mutawati' (univocal), because its individuals share equally in its meaning; example: 'insan' (human) applies equally to all individual humans in the external world, and 'shams' (sun) applies equally to all mental individuals (i.e., instances of the concept). If not equal, that is, the meaning applies to some individuals with priority (awlawiyya), or anteriority (taqaddum wa ta'akhkhur), or intensity (shidda wa du'f), then it is called 'mushakkik' (equivocal or analogical). The author gives three types: (1) priority: where the meaning is more perfect in some individuals, like existence (wujud) is more perfect in the Necessary Being (God) than in contingent beings; (2) anteriority/posteriority: where existence is prior in the Necessary than in the contingent; (3) intensity/weakness: where existence is stronger in the Necessary, because its effects are more manifest, analogous to whiteness being more intense in snow than in ivory. Finally, the author explains the name 'mushakkik' (from shakk = doubt): because the observer, seeing that the individuals share in the root meaning, may think it is univocal, but noticing the difference, may suspect it is ambiguous (like a homonym). Thus, the term indicates a state of doubt between univocity and ambiguity.

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي (juz'i haqiqi): A particular that applies to only one individual, like 'Zayd'; it cannot be predicated of many. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard in post-Avicennan logic.]
كلي (kulli): A universal that can be predicated of many individuals, like 'human'. [Note: Stable term across Islamic logic.]
متواطئ (mutawati'): A universal whose meaning applies equally to all its individuals, like 'human' to all humans. [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
مشكك (mushakkik): A universal whose meaning applies to individuals with priority, anteriority, or intensity, causing doubt whether it is univocal or ambiguous. [Note: Stable; sometimes translated as 'analogical' in English.]
التشكيك بالأولوية (al-tashkik bi al-awlawiyya): Type of equivocity where the meaning is more perfect or prior in some individuals, e.g., existence in God vs. contingent. [Note: Specific classification within tashkik; found in standard logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Intensity
D) Univocity

Key Takeaway
The classification of universals into univocal and equivocal (mushakkik) with three types of tashkik is essential for accurate predication in theology and jurisprudence, preventing misunderstandings about the nature of divine attributes.

Synonymy and Heteronymy: Conceptual Unity vs. Co-Reference
Qutb al-Din defines synonymy and heteronymy, refutes a mistaken claim of synonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true synonyms from false synonyms by applying the criterion of conceptual unity (mafhum) rather than co-reference (dhat).

Core Idea
Synonymy requires identity of concept, not just identity of essence.

In this passage, Qutb al-Din defines two key relations between words: synonymy (mutaradif) and heteronymy (mutabayin). He then refutes a common mistake: thinking that words that apply to the same object are synonyms. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, where the meanings of terms determine legal and theological conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definitions
From the matn: synonymy is agreement in meaning, heteronymy is difference. Note that the definition is relational: a word is synonym or heteronym only with respect to another word.
The matn is the first sentence: 'Every word with respect to another word is a synonym if they agree in meaning, and a heteronym if they differ.'
2
Understand the analogy
The author uses the analogy of riders on a mount: meaning is the mount, words are riders. If two words share one meaning, they are like two riders on one mount (synonyms). If meanings differ, each word rides its own mount (heteronyms).
Example of synonyms: 'al-layth' and 'al-asad' (both mean lion). Example of heteronyms: 'al-insan' (man) and 'al-faras' (horse).
3
Analyze the false synonymy examples
Pairs like 'al-natiq' (speaker) and 'al-fasih' (eloquent) are not synonyms because even though they can refer to the same person, they have different meanings. Unpack why each fails the conceptual unity test.
The key is that synonymy requires identity of mafhum, not just application to the same dhat.
4
Apply the criterion to problematic cases
The passage discusses partial overlap (e.g., 'al-hayawan' and 'al-abyad') and coextensive terms (e.g., 'al-insan' and 'al-katib bi-al-imkan'). Even coextensive terms are not necessarily synonyms. The error stems from falsely assuming that universal affirmative propositions convert.
This step sharpens your ability to detect false synonymy in any Islamic text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤ - المترادفان والمتبائنان]
قال : وكلّ لفظ فهو بالنسبة إلى لفظ آخر «مرادف» لـه إن توافقا في المعنى، و «مباين» له إن اختلفا فيه.
أقول : مامر من تقسيم اللفظ كان بالقياس إلى نفسه وبالنظر إلى نفس معناه، وهذا تقسيم للفظ بالقياس إلى غيره من الألفاظ : فاللفظ إذا نسبناه إلى لفظ آخر فلا يخلو إما أن يتوافقا في المعنى - أي المعنيين، وحينئذ يجب أن تجعل التاء للنقل من الوصفية إلى الاسمية، كما في «الذبيحة» ونظائرها. أو يجعل لفظ الحقيقة في الأصل جارية على موصوف مؤنث غير مذكور، كما في قولك : مررت بقبيلة بني فلان وجاز أن يؤخذ من حق اللازم، بمعنى الثابتة، فلا إشكال في التاء (شريف).
۱) هذا إشارة إلى المعنى الأول وقوله «معلوم الدلالة» إشارة إلى المعنى الثاني (شريف) (۲) فعلى هذا يكون المجاز مصدراً ميمياً استعمل بمعنى اسم الفاعل ثم نقل إلى اللفظ المذكور، وقد يوجه بأن المتكلم جاز في هذا اللفظ عن معناه الأصلي إلى معنى آخر، فهو محل الجواز (شريف).
يكون معناهما واحداً - أويتخالفا في المعنى – أي يكون لأحدهما معنى وللآخر معنى آخر فإن كانا متوافقين فهو مرادف له واللفظان «مترادفان» أخذاً من الترادف الذي هو ركوب أحد خلف آخر، كأن المعنى مركوب واللفظان راکبان عليه فيكونان مترادفين كـ «الليث» و «الأسد». وإن كانا مختلفين فهو مباين له واللفظان «متباينان» لأن المباينة المفارقة، ومتى اختلف المعنى لم يكن المركوب واحداً، فيتحقق المفارقة بين اللفظين للتفرقة بين المركوبين كـ « الإنسان» و «الفرس». ومن الناس من ظن أن مثل الناطق» و«الفصيح» ومثل «السيف» و«الصارم» من الألفاظ المترادفة لصدقهما على ذات واحدة، وهو فاسد لأنّ الترادف هو الاتحاد في المفهوم، لا الاتحاد في الذات؛ نعم الاتحاد في الذات من لوازم الاتحاد في المفهوم بدون العكس. (۱) فيه تحقير لهم بناء على ظهور فساد ظنّهم ، فإن الناطق» موصوف بـ«الفصيح» فالفصاحة صفة للنطق، فهما مختلفان في المعنى وإن صدقا على ذات واحدة مع صدق الناطق على ذات أخرى بدون الفصيح ؛ وكذا «السيف» فإنه موصوف بـ«الصارم» والصارم - بمعنى القاطع - صفة له، مع أن السيف أعم منه، فيبعد ظنّ الترادف في هذين المثالين. وأبعد منهما توهم (ن : ظن) الترادف فيما بين شيئين بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه كالحيوان والأبيض وأما ظن الترادف بين الموصوف والصفة المساوية لـه كـ«الإنسان» و «الكاتب بالإمكان فهو وإن كان باطلاً أيضاً إلا أنه ليس بذلك البعد بالكلية، وكان منشأ الظن في المتساويين توهم انعكاس الموجبة كلية كنفسها، فلما وجدوا أن كل مترادفين متحدان في الذات، تخيلوا أن كل متحدين في الذات مترادفان، وإذا بطل الظنّ في المتساويين كان بطلانه في غيره أظهر (شريف).
[14 - The Two Synonyms and the Two Heteronyms] He said: Every word with respect to another word is a synonym for it if they agree in meaning, and a heteronym for it if they differ in meaning. I say: The previous division of the word was in relation to itself and in consideration of its own meaning, and this is a division of the word in relation to other words: when we relate a word to another word, it is either that they agree in meaning—i.e., the two meanings—and then one must make the ta for transfer from adjectival to nominal, as in 'al-dhabiha' and its like. Or one makes the word al-haqiqa originally flowing upon a feminine described not mentioned, as in your saying: 'I passed by the tribe of so-and-so' and it is permissible to take from the right of the necessary, meaning the fixed, so there is no problem with the ta (Sharif). (1) This refers to the first meaning, and his saying 'known indication' refers to the second meaning (Sharif). (2) On this basis, the majaz is a verbal noun used in the sense of the active participle, then transferred to the mentioned word, and it may be directed that the speaker traversed in this word from its original meaning to another meaning, so it is the place of permissibility (Sharif). Then he said: Their meaning is one—or they differ in meaning—, i.e., one has a meaning and the other has another meaning. If they are in agreement, then it is a synonym for it, and the two words are 'mutaradifan' taken from al-taraduf which is riding one behind another, as if the meaning is ridden and the two words are riders upon it, so they are synonyms, like 'al-layth' and 'al-asad'. And if they are different, then it is a heteronym for it, and the two words are 'mutabayinan' because al-mubayana is separation. Whenever the meaning differs, the ridden is not one, so separation occurs between the two words due to the distinction between the two riders, like 'al-insan' and 'al-faras'. And among people there are those who think that examples like 'al-natiq' and 'al-fasih' and like 'al-sayf' and 'al-sarim' are synonymous words because they apply to one essence, but that is corrupt because synonymy is unity in concept, not unity in essence; yes, unity in essence is a necessary consequence of unity in concept, but not vice versa. (1) In it is disparagement of them based on the apparent corruption of their assumption. Indeed, 'al-natiq' is described by 'al-fasih', so eloquence is an attribute of speech, so they differ in meaning even if they apply to one essence, with 'al-natiq' applying to another essence without 'al-fasih'. Likewise 'al-sayf', for it is described by 'al-sarim', and 'al-sarim'—meaning 'the cutter'—is an attribute of it, while 'al-sayf' is more general than it, so the assumption of synonymy in these two examples is far-fetched. Even more far-fetched is the illusion of synonymy between two things between which there is generality and specificity from one aspect, like 'al-hayawan' and 'al-abyad'. As for the assumption of synonymy between the described and the attribute equal to it, like 'al-insan' and 'al-katib bi-al-imkan', although it is also false, yet it is not that far-fetched at all. The source of the assumption in the coextensive cases was the illusion of the conversion of the universal affirmative, for when they found that every pair of synonyms are united in essence, they imagined that every pair united in essence are synonyms. And if the assumption is false in the coextensive cases, its falsehood in others is more apparent (Sharif).
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a definition and expansion on the concepts of synonymy (mutaradif) and heteronymy (mutabayin) as they apply to words in relation to other words. The author, Qutb al-Din, first quotes a matn that divides words relative to other words: a word is a synonym if it agrees in meaning with another, and a heteronym if it differs. He then comments that this division is by relation to other words, as opposed to the previous division which considered the word in itself. He then explains the two possibilities: agreement or difference in meaning. In the midst of this, he inserts a linguistic note about the letter 'ta' in words like 'al-dhabiha', indicating that it transfers from an adjectival to a nominal form. This is followed by a marginal note (from Sharif) clarifying that this note refers to the first meaning and the phrase 'known indication'. This digression, though seemingly unrelated, is part of the commentary's expansion on the nature of words. Returning to the main topic, he continues explaining that if two words agree in meaning, they are called 'mutaradifan', taken from the analogy of riders mounting a mount; the meaning is the mount and the words are riders. Examples given are 'al-layth' and 'al-asad' (both meaning 'lion'). If they differ, they are 'mutabayanin' because of separation; the meaning being different implies different mounts, so separation between words occurs, as in 'al-insan' (man) and 'al-faras' (horse). The passage then critiques a false common belief: that words like 'al-natiq' (the speaker) and 'al-fasih' (the eloquent) or 'al-sayf' (the sword) and 'al-sarim' (the sharp one) are synonyms because they apply to the same object. The author refutes this, emphasizing that synonymy requires unity in concept (mafhum), not merely in essence (dhat). While unity in essence is a consequence of unity in concept, the reverse is not true; thus, co-reference does not imply synonymy. The marginal note elaborates that 'al-natiq' is described by 'al-fasih', so they have different meanings even if they apply to the same individual. Similarly, 'al-sayf' is broader than 'al-sarim', so they are not synonyms. Even more far-fetched is assuming synonymy between terms with partial overlap like 'al-hayawan' (animal) and 'al-abyad' (white). The note also addresses the case of a subject and its coextensive attribute, like 'al-insan' and 'al-katib bi-al-imkan' (man and potentially writer), which, though false, is less far-fetched. The source of the error in coextensive cases is the mistaken belief that universal affirmative propositions convert universally; people observed that all synonym pairs share essence, so they erroneously concluded that all pairs sharing essence are synonyms. The refutation thereby clarifies the precise condition for synonymy: conceptual unity, not mere existential overlap.

Key Terms
مرادف
مباين
المفهوم
الذات

Key Takeaway
Synonymy requires identity of concept (mafhum), not merely co-reference in essence (dhat); co-reference is a necessary but not sufficient condition.

Kulli and Juz'i: The Foundations of Conceptual Classification
Author defines kulli and juz'i concepts and explains what is meant by 'ma'ani' in this context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given concept is kulli (universal) or juz'i (particular) based on the criterion of preventing sharing in conception.

Core Idea
A concept's universality or particularity is determined by its very conception, not by the number of words.

This lesson introduces the foundational distinction between universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) concepts. This distinction underpins all subsequent discussions in logic about definitions and syllogisms. The author also clarifies what is meant by 'ma'ani' in this context, ensuring we are not misled by later usages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
The author defines 'particular' and 'universal' as properties of concepts. Note the Arabic terms: جزئي (juz'ī) and كلي (kullī).
2
Extract the criterion for particularity
A concept is juz'ī if its very conception prevents multiple instances from sharing it. Example: the concept of a specific person (e.g., Zayd) cannot be applied to anyone else.
The text says: 'إن منع نفس تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه'.
3
Extract the criterion for universality
A concept is kullī if its conception does not prevent sharing. Example: the concept of 'human' can apply to many individuals.
The text says: 'و إن لم يمنع'.
4
Trace the accidental transfer of names
The word indicating a kullī concept is also called kullī, but only derivatively (bi al-ʿaraḍ). The true kullī is the concept itself.
The text says: 'واللفظ الدال عليهما يسمّى كلياً و جزئياً بالعرض'.
5
Clarify the meaning of 'ma'ani' and 'singular meaning'
The author specifies that by 'ma'ani' he means mental forms (ṣuwar dhihniyya) signified by words. Singular meaning (maʿnā mufrad) is what is expressed by a single word, not necessarily a simple concept.
The commentary (Sharif) clarifies that 'singular meaning' does not mean simple with no parts.

كل مفهوم فهو «جزئيّ» إن منع نفس تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه و «كلّيّ» إن لم يمنع واللفظ الدال عليهما يسمّى «كلياً» و «جزئيّاً» بالعرض. أقول : المعاني هي الصور الذهنية من حيث أنها وضع بإزائها الألفاظ، فإن عبر عنها بألفاظ مفردة فهي المعاني المفردة. (شريف) يعني ليس المراد هاهنا من المعنى المفرد ما يكون بسيطاً لاجزء له، ومن المعنى
Every concept is 'particular' if its very conception prevents the occurrence of sharing in it, and 'universal' if it does not prevent, and the word indicating them is called 'universal' and 'particular' by accident. I say: the meanings (ma'ani) are the mental forms insofar as words are placed to signify them; if they are expressed by singular words, they are singular meanings. (Sharif) He means that what is intended here by 'singular meaning' is not what is simple having no part, nor from the meaning...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage begins the second chapter on singular meanings. It first defines the key terms 'particular' (juz'i) and 'universal' (kulli) as properties of concepts (mafhum). A concept is particular if conceptualizing it alone prevents multiple individuals from sharing it (e.g., Zayd - a specific person), and universal if its conceptualization does not prevent sharing (e.g., human - applicable to many). The word that indicates these concepts is called 'universal' or 'particular' derivatively (bi al-ʿarad). The author then explains that 'meanings' (ma'ani) here are the mental forms (suwar dhihniyya) insofar as words are conventionally assigned to them. When these meanings are expressed by singular (mufrad) words, they are called singular meanings. The commentator (Sharif) clarifies that 'singular meaning' in this context does not mean something simple with no parts; rather, it means a concept that is signified by a single word, regardless of whether the concept itself is composite or simple.

Key Terms
مفهوم (mafhūm)
جزئي (juz'ī)
كلي (kullī)
معنى (ma'nā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for a concept to be 'particular' (juz'i)?
A) It is signified by a single word.
B) Its very conception prevents multiple individuals from sharing it.
C) It has no parts.
D) It is a mental form.

Key Takeaway
A concept is particular if its conception prevents sharing, universal if it does not; and 'meanings' here are mental forms signified by words, with 'singular meaning' referring to what is expressed by a single word, regardless of complexity.

The Division of Concepts: Particular vs Universal
Author defines concept and divides it into particular and universal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a particular concept from a universal concept using the criterion of the mere conception (nafs al-tasawwur) and explain why this distinction is foundational for definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Particularity is defined by intellectual prevention, not external impossibility.

This passage from the Shamsiyya and its commentary establishes the foundational division of all concepts in logic: particular and universal. The division is not based on actual existence but on whether the mere conception of the concept prevents the intellect from supposing it true of many individuals. Understanding this distinction is essential for later discussions on predication, definition, and the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides every concept (mafhūm) into particular (juz'ī) and universal (kullī). Find the exact definitions: particular is that whose very conception prevents sharing; universal is that whose conception does not prevent sharing.
The prevention must be intellectual, not external.
2
Understand the criterion: 'nafs al-tasawwur'
The criterion is the mere conception considered in itself. It is not whether sharing is actually possible in external reality, but whether the intellect, by merely conceiving the meaning, is prevented from applying it to many.
This excludes cases where external proof prevents sharing, like Wajib al-Wujud.
3
Apply to examples
The passage gives 'this human' (dhat Zayd) as particular: the intellect cannot suppose it true of another. 'Human' is universal: the intellect can readily suppose it of many.
Even if only one human exists, 'human' is still universal because the conception does not prevent sharing.
4
Recognize the exception: Wajib al-Wujud
Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Existence) is universal in conception because the mere concept does not prevent sharing; sharing is impossible only by external proof. This illustrates that the criterion is purely about the conception, not external reality.
This prevents Wajib al-Wujud from being incorrectly classified as particular.
5
Clarify the textual mistake
The commentary notes that some copies erroneously added 'its meaning' to 'the very conception', which is redundant because the concept is the meaning. This emphasizes precision in logical definitions.
The qualification 'nafs al-tasawwur' alone suffices.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس . فالمركبة؛ والكلام هاهنا إنما هو في المعاني المفردة كما ستعرف.
(1) فكل مفهوم - وهو الحاصل في العقل - إما جزئي أو كلي، لأنه إما أن يكون نفس تصوّره - أي من حيث أنه متصور - مانعاً من وقوع الشركة فيه - أي من اشتراكه بين كثيرين وصدقه عليها - أو لا يكون، فإن منع نفس تصوّره عن الشركة فهو الجزئي، كهذا الإنسان، فإنّ الهذية إذا حصل مفهومها عند العقل امتنع العقل بمجرد تصوره عن صدقه على متعدد. وإن لم يمنع الشركة من حيث أنه متصوّر فهو الكلي، كالإنسان.
المركب ما يكون مركبا وله جزء، بل المراد من المعنى المفرد ما يكون لفظه مفرداً، ومن المعنى المركب ما يكون لفظه مركباً، فالإفراد والتركيب صفتان للألفاظ أصالة، ويوصف المعاني بهما تبعاً؛ فيقال: المعنى المفرد ما يستفاد من اللفظ المفرد، والمعنى المركب ما يستفاد من اللفظ المركب، وبعبارة أخرى المعنى المركب ما يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه، والمعنى المفرد ما لا يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه سواء كان هناك للمعنى واللفظ جزء، أو لا يكون لشيء منهما جزء، أو يكون لأحدهما جزء دون الآخر (شريف).
(1) ملخص الكلام أن ما حصل في العقل فهو بمجرد حصوله فيه إن امتنع في العقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين فهو الجزئي، كذات زيد، فإنه إذا حصل عند العقل استحال أن يفرض صدقه على كثيرين، وإلا - أي وإن لم يمتنع بمجرد حصوله فيه فرض صدقه على كثيرين - فهو الكلي، فالكلية إمكان فرض الاشتراك، والجزئية استحالته (شريف).
(2) لما كان ظاهر العبارة يدل على أن المانع من الشركة هو نفس تصوره نبه على أن المراد منع ذلك المفهوم من حيث أنه متصور (شريف).
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
فإن مفهومه إذا حصل عند العقل لم يمنع من صدقه على كثيرين. وقد وقع في بعض النسخ «نفس تصور معناه» وهو سهو، وإلا لكان للمعنى معنى، لأن المفهوم هو المعنى.
وإنما قيد بـ «نفس التصور» لأن من الكليات ما يمنع الشركة بالنظر إلى الخارج، كواجب الوجود، فإن الشركة فيه ممتنعة بالدليل الخارجي، لكن إذا جرّد العقل النظر إلى مفهومه لم يمتنع من صدقه على كثيرين، فإن مجرد تصوره لو كان مانعاً من الشركة لم يفتقر في إثبات الوحدانية إلى دليل آخر، وكالكليات الفرضية مثل اللا شيء.
(1) منشأ هذا السهو أن القوم قد يصفون اللفظ بالكلي والجزئي وإن كان بالعرض، فيقولون: اللفظ إما أن يمنع نفس تصور معناه من وقوع الشركة فيه فهو الجزئي، أو لا يمنع فهو الكلي (شريف).
(2) يريد أنه لو قيل: «كل مفهوم إما أن يمنع من الشركة» لفهم أن المقصود منعه من اشتراكه بين كثيرين في نفس الأمر - أي امتناع اشتراكه بين كثيرين في نفس الأمر - فيلزم أن يكون مفهوم واجب الوجود داخلاً في حد الجزئي، فلما قيد بالتصور علم أن المراد منعه في العقل من الاشتراك، أي يمنع العقل من أن يجعله مشتركا ويمتنع منه ذلك فلا يمكن للعقل فرض اشتراكه، فلا يلزم دخول مفهوم واجب الوجود في حد الجزئي. وأما التقييد بـ «النفس» فلئلا يتوهم دخول مفهوم واجب الوجود فيه إذا لاحظه العقل مع ملاحظة برهان التوحيد، فإن العقل حينئذ لا يمكنه فرض اشتراكه، لكن هذا الامتناع لم يحصل بمجرد تصوره وحصوله في العقل، بل به وملاحظة ذلك البرهان، وأما بمجرد تصوره وحصوله في العقل فيمكن للعقل فرض اشتراكه (شريف).
(3) هي التي لا يمكن صدقها في نفس الأمر على شيء من الأشياء الخارجية
The five universals. As for compound [meanings], the discussion here is only about simple meanings, as you will know. (1) Every concept — which is what is obtained in the intellect — is either particular or universal, because either its very conception — i.e., insofar as it is conceived — prevents the occurrence of sharing in it — i.e., sharing among many and being true of them — or it does not. If its very conception prevents sharing, then it is the particular, like 'this human', for when its concept is obtained in the intellect, the intellect by mere conception is prevented from being true of multiple [individuals]. And if it does not prevent sharing insofar as it is conceived, then it is the universal, like 'human'. Compound is what is compound and has a part; rather what is meant by simple meaning is that whose expression is simple, and by compound meaning is that whose expression is compound. For simplicity and composition are attributes of expressions originally, and meanings are described by them derivatively. So it is said: simple meaning is that which is understood from a simple expression, and compound meaning is that which is understood from a compound expression. In other words, compound meaning is that whose part is understood from part of its expression, and simple meaning is that whose part is not understood from part of its expression, whether there is a part for the meaning and the expression, or there is no part for either, or there is a part for one without the other (Sharif). (1) Summary of the speech: What is obtained in the intellect is, by its mere obtainment, if it is impossible in the intellect to suppose it true of many, then it is the particular, like the essence of Zayd, for when it is obtained in the intellect, it is impossible to suppose it true of many; otherwise — i.e., if by its mere obtainment it is not impossible to suppose it true of many — then it is the universal. So universality is the possibility of supposing sharing, and particularity is its impossibility (Sharif). (2) Since the apparent wording indicates that the preventer of sharing is its very conception, he alerted that what is meant is the prevention of that concept insofar as it is conceived (Sharif). Commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: For its concept, when obtained in the intellect, does not prevent being true of many. And it has occurred in some copies [the phrase] 'the very conception of its meaning' and that is a mistake, otherwise the meaning would have a meaning, for the concept is the meaning. And it is only qualified by 'the very conception' because among universals are those that prevent sharing with respect to the external, like the Necessary Existence, for sharing in it is impossible by an external proof, but if the intellect isolates consideration of its concept, it is not prevented from being true of many. For if its mere conception were a preventer of sharing, then there would be no need for another proof to establish oneness. And likewise hypothetical universals, like 'nothing'. (1) The origin of this mistake is that the people sometimes describe the expression as universal or particular, even if accidentally, so they say: the expression either prevents the very conception of its meaning from the occurrence of sharing in it, so it is particular, or it does not prevent, so it is universal (Sharif). (2) He intends that if it were said: 'Every concept either prevents from sharing', it would be understood that what is meant is its prevention from sharing among many in reality — i.e., the impossibility of its sharing among many in reality — so the concept of Necessary Existence would enter into the definition of particular. So when qualified by 'conception', it is known that what is meant is prevention in the intellect from sharing, i.e., it prevents the intellect from making it shared and the intellect is prevented from that, so it cannot suppose its sharing, and thus the concept of Necessary Existence does not enter into the definition of particular. As for the qualification by 'self' [النفس], it is to prevent misunderstanding that the concept of Necessary Existence enters into it if the intellect considers it along with consideration of the proof of Oneness, for then the intellect cannot suppose its sharing, but this prevention is not obtained by mere conception and obtainment in the intellect, but rather by it and consideration of that proof. As for by mere conception and obtainment in the intellect, it is possible for the intellect to suppose its sharing (Sharif). (3) They are those that cannot be true in reality of any external thing.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental division of concepts (مفهوم) into particular (جزئي) and universal (كلي). The author begins by stating that every concept obtained in the intellect is either particular or universal. The criterion is whether the mere conception of that concept prevents it from being shared among many individuals—that is, from being predicated of multiple things. If the very conception prevents sharing, it is particular, like the concept 'this human' (إنسان معين) which by its very nature cannot be truly said of another. If it does not prevent sharing, it is universal, like 'human' (إنسان) which can be predicated of many. The commentary clarifies that the prevention must be from the concept itself, not from external factors. It gives the example of 'necessary existence' (واجب الوجود) which is impossible to share by external proof, but its mere conception does not prevent sharing; therefore it remains universal. The qualification بـ'نفس التصور' (by the very conception) is crucial to exclude cases where sharing is impossible due to external proof. The passage also explains the mistake in some copies that added 'معناه' (its meaning) to 'نفس تصور' which would be redundant. Furthermore, it distinguishes between universal and particular as attributes of expressions (لفظ) derivatively, noting that some scholars describe expressions as universal or particular based on whether the conception of their meaning prevents sharing. The commentary emphasizes that the prevention is intellectual (عقلي), not external, and that the condition is satisfied if the intellect, by merely conceiving the concept, cannot suppose it shared. This ensures that concepts like 'necessary existence' do not incorrectly become particular.

مفهوم
جزئي
كلي
نفس التصور
واجب الوجود

The Five Universals

Defining the Universal by Mental Possibility
Author defines universal and particular by mental possibility and discusses hypothetical universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define universal and particular concepts according to the logicians' criterion of mental possibility of sharing, and distinguish them from definitions based on actual predication.

Core Idea
A universal needs only mental possibility of sharing, not actual individuals.

The logicians define the universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) not by what exists in reality, but by how the mind considers the concept. A universal is a concept that does not prevent the mind from assuming it could apply to multiple individuals, even if it never does. This subtle but crucial distinction allows us to include concepts like 'Necessary Existent' and hypothetical universals like 'non-thing' within the category of universals. In this lesson, we will unpack this definition and see why the logicians insisted on the mental perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions in the passage
Locate the commentator's statement: 'The [logicians] considered in the division into universal and particular the state of concepts in the mind, i.e., their prevention of the mind from assuming their sharing and their non-prevention from it.' This gives the criterion: prevention or non-prevention of mental sharing.
Note that 'non-prevention' means universal; 'prevention' means particular.
2
Understand the hypothetical universals
The commentator introduces concepts like 'non-thing', 'non-possible', 'non-existent'. These can never be true of anything in actual fact, yet the mind can still conceive of them as possibly shared. Ask yourself: why does the mind not prevent sharing for these? Because their contradictories blanket all reality, but the mind can ignore that and still think 'non-thing' could apply to multiple things in imagination.
This shows that actual impossibility of instantiation does not destroy universality.
3
Contrast with a wrong definition
If the definition required actual predication, these hypothetical universals would be particulars (since they have no actual individuals). The commentator warns that this would make the definition of universal not comprehensive and the definition of particular not exclusive.
This is a classic test: any definition of universal must include these.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Take the concept 'al-wajib' (the Necessary Existent). In Islamic theology, there is only one Necessary Existent (God). Yet we can conceive of 'wajib' as a concept that could apply to multiple individuals if there were more than one Necessary. The mind does not prevent this sharing, so it is a universal despite having only one actual individual.
This shows the utility in Kalam discussions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس. واللا إمكان واللا وجود - فإنّها يمتنع أن تصدق على شيء من الأشياء في الخارج، لكن لا بالنظر إلى مجرد تصورها. ومن هاهنا يعلم أن أفراد الكلي لا يجب أن يكون الكلي صادقاً والذهنية، كـ «اللاشيء» فإنّ كلّ ما يفرض في الخارج فهو شيء في الخارج ضرورة، وكل ما يفرض في الذهن فهو شيء في الذهن ضرورة، فلا يصدق في نفس الأمر على شيء منهما أنه لاشيء، وكـ اللاممكن» بالإمكان العام، فإن كل مفهوم يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر أنه ممكن عام، فيمتنع صدق نقيضه في نفس الأمر على مفهوم من المفهومات، و كـ «اللاموجود» فإن كل ما هو في الخارج يصدق عليه أنه موجود فيه، وكل ما هو في الذهن يصدق عليه أنه موجود في الذهن فلا يمكن صدق نقيضه في نفس الأمر على شيء أصلاً، لكن هذه الكليات الفرضية مع امتناع صدقها على شيء لا يمتنع العقل بمجرد حصولها فيه عن فرض الاشتراك بل يمكنه فرض اشتراكها بمجرد حصولها فيه مع قطع النظر عن شمول نقائضها لجميع الأشياء، وإنما اعتبر القوم في التقسيم إلى الكلي والجزئي حال المفهومات في العقل، أعني امتناعها عن فرض العقل لاشتراكها وعدم امتناعها عنه، فجعلوا أمثال مفهوم الواجب ونقائض المفهومات الشاملة لجميع الأشياء الذهنية والخارجية المحققة والمقدرة داخلة في الكليات دون الجزئيات ولم يعتبروا حال المفهومات في أنفسها أعني امتناعها عن الاشتراك في نفس الأمر وعدم امتناعها عنه فيه – ولم يجعلوا تلك المذكورات داخلة في الجزئيات، بناء على أن مقصودهم هو التوصل ببعض المفهومات إلى بعض، وذلك إنما هو باعتبار حصولها في الذهن، فاعتبار أحوالها الذهنية هو المناسب لماهو غرضهم (شريف). (١) أي ومن أجل أن مفهوم الواجب الوجود ومفهومات «اللاشيء» و«اللا ممكن» و «اللاموجود» كليات يعلم أن أفراد الكلّي التي يتحقق بها كليته لا يجب أن يصدق الكلي عليها في نفس الأمر، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع صدقه عليها في نفس الأمر، فإن مفهوم الواجب الوجود» يمتنع صدقه في نفس الأمر على أكثر عليها، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع أن يصدق الكلي عليه في الخارج، إذا لم يمنع العقل من صدقه عليه بمجرد تصوّره، فلو لم يعتبر «نفس التصوّر» في تعريف الكلّي و الجزئي لدخل تلك الكليات في تعريف الجزئي - فلايكون مانعاً - وخرجت عن تعريف الكلي - فلا يكون جامعاً. (۲ و بيان التسمية بالكلّي و الجزئي أنّ الكلّي جزء للجزئي غالباً كالإنسان - فإنّه جزء لزيد - والحيوان - فإنّه جزء للإنسان - والجسم - فإنه جزء للحيوان - فيكون الجزئي كلاً، والكلي جزء له، وكلية الشيء إنما تكون بالنسبة إلى الجزئي، فيكون ذلك الشيء منسوباً إلى الكل، من واحد، و الكليات الفرضيّة يمتنع صدقها في نفس الأمر على شيء واحد كليته منه. - فضلاً عما هو أكثر. فالمعتبر في أفراد الكلّي إمكان فرض صدقه عليها، إذ بهذا المقدار تتحقق وكون تلك الأفراد له محققة في نفس الأمر غير لازم لكليته. نعم ما كان فرداً للكلّي في نفس الأمر فلابد أن يصدق عليه ذلك الكلّي في نفس الأمر، أو أمكن صدقه عليه فيها.
The five universals. And non-possibility and non-existence - for they are prevented from being true of any thing in external reality, but not by consideration of mere conceptualization of them. From here it is known that the individuals of the universal do not require that the universal be true of them in external reality and mental [individuals], such as 'non-thing': for everything that is assumed in external reality is necessarily a thing in external reality, and everything assumed in the mind is necessarily a thing in the mind, so it is not true in actual fact of either of them that it is a non-thing; and like 'non-possible' by general possibility: for every concept is true of it in actual fact that it is generally possible, so it is impossible for its negation to be true in actual fact of any concept; and like 'non-existent': for everything that is in external reality is true of it that it exists there, and everything in the mind is true of it that it exists in the mind, so it is not possible for its negation to be true in actual fact of anything at all. But these hypothetical universals, despite the impossibility of their being true of anything, the mind, by the mere occurrence of them in it, is not prevented from assuming sharing; rather it is possible for it to assume sharing of them by their mere occurrence in it, disregarding the coverage of their contradictories of all things. The [logicians] considered in the division into universal and particular the state of concepts in the mind, i.e., their prevention of the mind from assuming their sharing and their non-prevention from it. So they made examples like the concept of the Necessary [Existent] and the contradictories of concepts that encompass all mental and external, actual and hypothetical things, included in universals, not in particulars; and they did not consider the state of concepts in themselves, i.e., their prevention from sharing in actual fact and their non-prevention from it; and they did not make those mentioned included in particulars, based on the fact that their purpose is to arrive from some concepts to others, and that is only by consideration of their occurrence in the mind, so considering their mental states is appropriate to what is their objective (Sharif). (1) That is, because the concept of the Necessary Existent and the concepts of 'non-thing', 'non-possible', and 'non-existent' are universals, it is known that the individuals of the universal by which its universality is realized do not require that the universal be true of them in actual fact; rather among its individuals are those whose truth in actual fact is impossible, for the concept of the Necessary Existent is impossible to be true in actual fact of most things. Rather among its individuals are those for which it is impossible for the universal to be true of them in external reality, since the mind does not prevent its being true of them by its mere conceptualization. Thus if 'mere conceptualization' were not considered in the definition of the universal and particular, those universals would enter into the definition of the particular—so it would not be a preventer—and they would exit from the definition of the universal—so it would not be comprehensive. (2) And the explanation of the naming 'universal' and 'particular' is that the universal is a part of the particular usually, like 'human'—for it is a part of Zayd—and 'animal'—for it is a part of 'human'—and 'body'—for it is a part of 'animal'. So the particular is like a whole, and the universal is a part of it. And the universality of a thing is only relative to the particular, so that thing is attributed to the whole. And the hypothetical universals are impossible to be true in actual fact of any one thing, let alone more. So what is considered in the individuals of the universal is the possibility of supposing it true of them, for by this extent the individuals are realized as belonging to it, and their being actual individuals of it is not necessary for its universality. Indeed, that which is an individual of the universal in actual fact must have that universal true of it in actual fact, or possible to be true of it therein.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) by al-Sharif al-Jurjani on the 'Risala al-Shamsiyya' in logic, specifically on the section about the five universals. The commentator clarifies the definition of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) by emphasizing the criterion used by logicians: the mental possibility of sharing, not actual predication in external reality. He begins by discussing hypothetical universals such as 'non-thing', 'non-possible', and 'non-existent'—concepts that cannot be truly predicated of any individual in external reality or even in the mind, because their contradictories are always true. For example, everything in external reality is a 'thing', so 'non-thing' cannot be true of it. Nevertheless, even though these concepts cannot be instantiated, the mind can still conceive of them and assume that they could be shared among multiple individuals. The logicians, therefore, defined universal as a concept that does not prevent the mind from assuming its sharing (ishtirak), regardless of whether it actually applies to anything. This is why concepts like 'Necessary Existent' and the negations of universal concepts are considered universals, not particulars, even though they may have no actual instances. The commentator then explains that if 'mere conceptualization' (mujarrad tasawwur) were not the criterion, these hypothetical universals would wrongly be classified as particulars. He then elaborates on the naming: the universal is called 'kulli' because it is a part of the particular, which is like a whole containing it. For instance, 'human' is a part of Zayd's essence, 'animal' is a part of 'human', and 'body' is a part of 'animal'. The universality is always relative to the particular; a thing is universal only with respect to the particulars under it. Finally, he reiterates that the condition for something to be universal is only the possibility of its applying to multiple individuals in the mind, not actual existence of such individuals. This correction is important to avoid misunderstanding the scope of universals.

الجزئي
الكليات الفرضية
نفس الأمر

The universal is defined by the mind's ability to assume sharing, not by actual instantiation; this includes hypothetical universals and the concept of the Necessary Existent.

The Naming of Universal and Particular: Part-Whole Relationship
Author explains why universal is called kulli and particular juz'i using part-whole relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the universal (kulli) is so named, using the part-whole relationship with the particular, and apply this understanding to logical hierarchies.

Core Idea
The universal is a part of the particular, like 'human' is part of Zayd.

The commentator al-Sharif al-Jurjani provides an etymological explanation for the terms 'kulli' (universal) and 'juz'i' (particular). The universal is called 'kulli' because it is a part (juz') of the particular, which is like a whole (kull). For example, 'human' is a part of the essence of the particular individual Zayd. This insight helps us understand how universals function as building blocks of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the naming explanation in the passage
Find where the commentator says: 'And the explanation of the naming universal and particular is that the universal is a part of the particular usually.' This is after the discussion of hypothetical universals.
The passage uses the example: 'human is a part of Zayd, animal is a part of human, body is a part of animal.'
2
Analyze the part-whole hierarchy
For any particular individual like Zayd, the concepts 'human', 'animal', 'body' are more general universals that are part of Zayd's definition. Each universal is a component of the particular's essence. So the particular is like a whole composed of universals.
The universal is 'kulli' because it is a part (juz') of the particular, so it is attributed to the whole (kull).
3
Contrast with hypothetical universals
Hypothetical universals like 'non-thing' cannot be parts of any real particular because they have no instances. Yet they are still universals based on mental possibility, but in terms of naming, they are not actually parts of any existing particular.
The naming explanation applies generically; the part-whole relation is typical but not necessary for universality.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In Islamic theology, the universal 'rasul' (messenger) is a part of the definition of particular messengers like Muhammad (pbuh). Similarly, 'nabi' (prophet) is a universal that is part of each prophet's definition.
This helps in understanding how definitions in fiqh and kalam are constructed by combining universals.

الكليات الخمس. واللا إمكان واللا وجود - فإنّها يمتنع أن تصدق على شيء من الأشياء في الخارج، لكن لا بالنظر إلى مجرد تصورها. ومن هاهنا يعلم أن أفراد الكلي لا يجب أن يكون الكلي صادقاً والذهنية، كـ «اللاشيء» فإنّ كلّ ما يفرض في الخارج فهو شيء في الخارج ضرورة، وكل ما يفرض في الذهن فهو شيء في الذهن ضرورة، فلا يصدق في نفس الأمر على شيء منهما أنه لاشيء، وكـ اللاممكن» بالإمكان العام، فإن كل مفهوم يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر أنه ممكن عام، فيمتنع صدق نقيضه في نفس الأمر على مفهوم من المفهومات، و كـ «اللاموجود» فإن كل ما هو في الخارج يصدق عليه أنه موجود فيه، وكل ما هو في الذهن يصدق عليه أنه موجود في الذهن فلا يمكن صدق نقيضه في نفس الأمر على شيء أصلاً، لكن هذه الكليات الفرضية مع امتناع صدقها على شيء لا يمتنع العقل بمجرد حصولها فيه عن فرض الاشتراك بل يمكنه فرض اشتراكها بمجرد حصولها فيه مع قطع النظر عن شمول نقائضها لجميع الأشياء، وإنما اعتبر القوم في التقسيم إلى الكلي والجزئي حال المفهومات في العقل، أعني امتناعها عن فرض العقل لاشتراكها وعدم امتناعها عنه، فجعلوا أمثال مفهوم الواجب ونقائض المفهومات الشاملة لجميع الأشياء الذهنية والخارجية المحققة والمقدرة داخلة في الكليات دون الجزئيات ولم يعتبروا حال المفهومات في أنفسها أعني امتناعها عن الاشتراك في نفس الأمر وعدم امتناعها عنه فيه – ولم يجعلوا تلك المذكورات داخلة في الجزئيات، بناء على أن مقصودهم هو التوصل ببعض المفهومات إلى بعض، وذلك إنما هو باعتبار حصولها في الذهن، فاعتبار أحوالها الذهنية هو المناسب لماهو غرضهم (شريف). (١) أي ومن أجل أن مفهوم الواجب الوجود ومفهومات «اللاشيء» و«اللا ممكن» و «اللاموجود» كليات يعلم أن أفراد الكلّي التي يتحقق بها كليته لا يجب أن يصدق الكلي عليها في نفس الأمر، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع صدقه عليها في نفس الأمر، فإن مفهوم الواجب الوجود» يمتنع صدقه في نفس الأمر على أكثر عليها، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع أن يصدق الكلي عليه في الخارج، إذا لم يمنع العقل من صدقه عليه بمجرد تصوّره، فلو لم يعتبر «نفس التصوّر» في تعريف الكلّي و الجزئي لدخل تلك الكليات في تعريف الجزئي - فلايكون مانعاً - وخرجت عن تعريف الكلي - فلا يكون جامعاً. The five universals. And non-possibility and non-existence - for they are prevented from being true of any thing in external reality, but not by consideration of mere conceptualization of them. From here it is known that the individuals of the universal do not require that the universal be true of them in external reality and mental [individuals], such as 'non-thing': for everything that is assumed in external reality is necessarily a thing in external reality, and everything assumed in the mind is necessarily a thing in the mind, so it is not true in actual fact of either of them that it is a non-thing; and like 'non-possible' by general possibility: for every concept is true of it in actual fact that it is generally possible, so it is impossible for its negation to be true in actual fact of any concept; and like 'non-existent': for everything that is in external reality is true of it that it exists there, and everything in the mind is true of it that it exists in the mind, so it is not possible for its negation to be true in actual fact of anything at all.
