The True Species: Essence-Complete Universal
Matn and commentary define true species as a universal that is the whole essence of its particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a universal is a true species by checking if it constitutes the complete essence of its particulars and is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'.

Core Idea
A true species gives the full essence, not just an accidental description.

In this passage, al-Qutbi defines one of the five universals: the true species. Understanding this is essential for constructing precise definitions in Islamic sciences. Let's dissect its key features: being the complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya) and being internal to its particulars, and always answering the question 'what is it?'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'al-naw' al-haqiqi' in the Arabic. The passage begins 'من أقسام الكلي النوع الحقيقي' – 'From the divisions of the universal: the true species'. Note that the entire paragraph is about defining this term.
2
Extract the two conditions
The matn says the universal is either the 'complete essence' (تمام ماهية) of its particulars and 'internal to them' (داخلاً فيها), or external to them. The first is the true species. So the two conditions are: (1) it must be the whole essence of its particulars, and (2) it must be inside them (i.e., part of their quiddity).
3
Test the examples given
The passage gives two examples: 'human' (إنسان) with many individuals, and 'sun' (شمس) with a single individual. Both are true species because they satisfy the conditions: each is the complete essence of its particulars and answers 'what is it?'.
A true species can have one or many individuals – the number does not affect the definition.
4
Distinguish from other universals
Contrast true species with other universals like genus (جنس) which is only part of the essence, or accident (عرض) which is external. A true species is the whole essence – it is the definiendum itself.
This distinction is critical for constructing proper definitions (hadd) in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أقسام الكلي النوع الحقيقي] قال : والكلّيّ إما أن يكون تمام ماهيّة ماتحته من الجزئيات وداخلاً فيها أو خارجاً عنها، والأول هو النوع الحقيقي» سواء كان متعدد الأشخاص- و هو المقول في جواب «ما هو بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً كالإنسان أو غير متعدّد الأشخاص - وهو المقول في جواب «ما هو » بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كالشمس – فهو إذن كلّيّ مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب «ماهو». أقول : إنك قد عرفت أن الغرض من وضع هذه المقالة معرفة كيفية اقتناص المجهولات التصورية من المعلومات التصورية ، و هي لا تقتنص شيء» وذلك الشيء يكون متناولاً لذلك الجزئي ولغيره، فالكلية والجزئية الإضافية مفهومان متضايفان لا يتعقل أحدهما الأمع الآخر، كالأبوة والبنوة؛ وأما الجزئية الحقيقية فهي تقابل الكلية تقابل الملكة والعدم، فإنّ الجزئية منع فرض الاشتراك بأن يصدق على كثيرين، والكلية عدم المنع. فالأولى أن يذكر وجه التسمية في الكلّي والجزئي الإضافي ثم يقال : وإنما سمي الجزئي الحقيقي أيضاً جزئياً لأنه أخص من الجزئي الإضافي، فأطلق اسم العام على الخاص وقيد بالحقيقي كما سنذكره (شريف).
[From the divisions of the universal: the true species] He said: The universal is either the complete essence of what is under it from the particulars and internal to them, or external to them. The first is the true species, whether it has multiple individuals—and it is that which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' according to both commonality and specificity together, like 'human'—or it has a single individual—and it is that which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' according to pure specificity, like 'sun'. So it is a universal predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. I say: You have already known that the purpose of composing this discourse is to know how to acquire unknown conceptual things from known conceptual things, and they are not acquired...' and that thing encompasses that particular and others. For relative universality and particularity are two correlative concepts; one cannot be understood without the other, like fatherhood and sonship. As for absolute particularity, it is opposed to universality as privation and possession: particularity is the prevention of supposing sharing by being true of many, and universality is the absence of prevention. So it is more appropriate to mention the reason for naming in the relative universal and particular, and then it is said: The absolute particular is also called particular because it is more specific than the relative particular, so the name of the general is applied to the specific and restricted by 'absolute' as we will mention (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from the Qutbi commentary defines 'true species' (al-naw' al-haqiqi) as one of the divisions of the universal. The matn states: The universal is either the complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya) of its particulars and internal to them, or external to them. The first is the true species. This applies whether the species has many individuals (like 'human') or only one (like 'sun'). In both cases, it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa). The commentator then elaborates on the purpose of logic: to acquire unknown concepts from known ones. He reiterates the distinction between relative and absolute universality/particularity, emphasizing that relative concepts are correlative (like fatherhood and sonship), while absolute particularity is the privation of universality—i.e., the prevention of being predicated of many. He suggests that the naming of 'particular' for the absolute sense is derived from the relative sense because it is more specific, hence the name of the general is applied to the specific with the qualifier 'absolute'. This passage clarifies that a true species is a universal that gives the full answer to 'what is it?' for its individuals, and it can be either common or unique.

Key Terms
النوع الحقيقي
تمام الماهية
المقول في جواب ما هو

D) A universal that prevents sharing among individuals.

Key Takeaway
A true species is a universal that constitutes the complete essence of its particulars, whether they are many or one, and is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'.

Relative vs Absolute: Understanding Universality and Particularity
Commentary explains relative vs absolute universality/particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between relative universality/particularity and absolute universality/particularity and explain the logical relationship between them.

Core Idea
Relative universal and particular are correlatives; absolute particular is simply the privation of universality.

The commentator expands on the distinction between relative and absolute senses of universality and particularity, clarifying the logic behind the terminology. This is crucial for understanding how concepts like 'human' and 'Zayd' relate in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction made in the passage
The passage introduces 'al-kulliyya wa al-juz'iyya al-idafiyya' (relative universality and particularity) and contrasts them with 'al-juz'iyya al-haqiqiyya' (absolute particularity). The relative concepts are described as 'mutadayifan' (correlative), meaning they cannot be understood without each other.
Correlatives are like father and son: one implies the other.
2
Understand the nature of absolute particularity
Absolute particularity is defined as 'man' al-iftirad al-ishtirak' (prevention of supposing sharing by being true of many). It is the privation ('adam) of universality. In contrast, universality is 'adam al-man' (absence of prevention). So absolute particularity is the negation of universality in the sense of not being applicable to many.
This follows the pattern of 'malaka wa 'adam' (possession and privation) – similar to sight and blindness.
3
Trace the naming relationship
The commentator explains why the absolute particular is called 'particular': because it is more specific (akhas) than the relative particular. The general name (particular) is applied to the specific (absolute particular) with a qualifier (haqiqi). This is a common linguistic phenomenon in Arabic, called 'itlaq ism al-'amm 'ala al-khass'.
This helps avoid confusion between the two senses of 'particular'.
4
Apply to examples
Take 'human' (insan) as a relative universal: it applies to many individuals and is understood in relation to those individuals. The individual Zayd is an absolute particular: it cannot be predicated of others. As a relative particular, Zayd is one of the many individuals under human. The absolute particular is a stricter sense: it is the complete prevention of being true of many.
The same entity can be considered under both relative and absolute perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أقسام الكلي النوع الحقيقي] قال : والكلّيّ إما أن يكون تمام ماهيّة ماتحته من الجزئيات وداخلاً فيها أو خارجاً عنها، والأول هو النوع الحقيقي» سواء كان متعدد الأشخاص- و هو المقول في جواب «ما هو بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً كالإنسان أو غير متعدّد الأشخاص - وهو المقول في جواب «ما هو » بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كالشمس – فهو إذن كلّيّ مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب «ماهو». أقول : إنك قد عرفت أن الغرض من وضع هذه المقالة معرفة كيفية اقتناص المجهولات التصورية من المعلومات التصورية ، و هي لا تقتنص شيء» وذلك الشيء يكون متناولاً لذلك الجزئي ولغيره، فالكلية والجزئية الإضافية مفهومان متضايفان لا يتعقل أحدهما الأمع الآخر، كالأبوة والبنوة؛ وأما الجزئية الحقيقية فهي تقابل الكلية تقابل الملكة والعدم، فإنّ الجزئية منع فرض الاشتراك بأن يصدق على كثيرين، والكلية عدم المنع. فالأولى أن يذكر وجه التسمية في الكلّي والجزئي الإضافي ثم يقال : وإنما سمي الجزئي الحقيقي أيضاً جزئياً لأنه أخص من الجزئي الإضافي، فأطلق اسم العام على الخاص وقيد بالحقيقي كما سنذكره (شريف).
[From the divisions of the universal: the true species] He said: The universal is either the complete essence of what is under it from the particulars and internal to them, or external to them. The first is the true species, whether it has multiple individuals—and it is that which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' according to both commonality and specificity together, like 'human'—or it has a single individual—and it is that which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' according to pure specificity, like 'sun'. So it is a universal predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. I say: You have already known that the purpose of composing this discourse is to know how to acquire unknown conceptual things from known conceptual things, and they are not acquired...' and that thing encompasses that particular and others. For relative universality and particularity are two correlative concepts; one cannot be understood without the other, like fatherhood and sonship. As for absolute particularity, it is opposed to universality as privation and possession: particularity is the prevention of supposing sharing by being true of many, and universality is the absence of prevention. So it is more appropriate to mention the reason for naming in the relative universal and particular, and then it is said: The absolute particular is also called particular because it is more specific than the relative particular, so the name of the general is applied to the specific and restricted by 'absolute' as we will mention (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from the Qutbi commentary defines 'true species' (al-naw' al-haqiqi) as one of the divisions of the universal. The matn states: The universal is either the complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya) of its particulars and internal to them, or external to them. The first is the true species. This applies whether the species has many individuals (like 'human') or only one (like 'sun'). In both cases, it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa). The commentator then elaborates on the purpose of logic: to acquire unknown concepts from known ones. He reiterates the distinction between relative and absolute universality/particularity, emphasizing that relative concepts are correlative (like fatherhood and sonship), while absolute particularity is the privation of universality—i.e., the prevention of being predicated of many. He suggests that the naming of 'particular' for the absolute sense is derived from the relative sense because it is more specific, hence the name of the general is applied to the specific with the qualifier 'absolute'. This passage clarifies that a true species is a universal that gives the full answer to 'what is it?' for its individuals, and it can be either common or unique.

Relative Universal and Particular as Correlatives
Author defines relative universal/particular and distinguishes essential from accidental universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relative universal and particular in a given definition and explain why they are correlatives.

Core Idea
Universality and particularity are relational: one cannot exist without the other.

In logic, 'universal' and 'particular' are not absolute labels; they depend on what they are compared to. This passage defines the relative universal and particular as correlatives, meaning each implies the other. Understanding this relationship is foundational for working with definitions, categories, and analogies in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
The passage defines two concepts: the relative universal (الكلي الإضافي) and the relative particular (الجزئي الإضافي). Each is defined in terms of the other: 'that which is attributed to the universal is universal, and that which is attributed to the particular is particular.'
Notice that the definition is circular in form but intentionally so—they are correlatives.
2
Understand the relational nature
A thing is not inherently universal or particular; it becomes so by relation. For example, 'animal' is universal relative to 'human', but 'human' is universal relative to 'Zayd'. The same term can shift roles depending on the comparison.
This is why the passage says 'the particularity of a thing is only in relation to the universal'.
3
Apply the definition to a real example
Take the five universals: 'species' is universal relative to 'individuals' but particular relative to 'genus'. Confirm that each concept fits the definition: what is attributed to the universal (species) is itself universal (genus-wise), and what is attributed to the particular (individual) is itself particular.
This relational view is essential for constructing proper definitions (hadd) and analogies (qiyas) in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنسوب إلى الكلّ كلّي، وكذلك جزئية الشيء إنما هي بالنسبة إلى الكلي، فيكون منسوباً إلى الجزء، والمنسوب إلى الجزء جزئي. واعلم أن الكلية والجزئية إنما تعتبران بالذات في المعاني، وأما في الألفاظ فقد تسمّى كلّيّة وجزئية بالعرض، تسمية الدالّ باسم المدلول.
And that which is attributed to the universal is universal, and likewise the particularity of a thing is only in relation to the universal, so it becomes attributed to the particular, and that which is attributed to the particular is particular. Know that universality and particularity are considered essentially in meanings, but in terms they may be called universal and particular accidentally, naming the signifier by the name of the signified.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage defines the relative universal and particular. It first states that anything attributed to the universal is itself universal, and the particularity of a thing is only relative to the universal, so that which is attributed to the particular is particular. This establishes the relational nature of these concepts. Then the author draws an important distinction: universality and particularity are intrinsic to meanings (concepts), but when applied to words, they are only accidental, because words are called universal or particular by virtue of the meanings they signify. This is like calling the signifier by the name of the signified. The key idea is that universality and particularity are correlative concepts ('mutadhayifan') that cannot be understood without each other.

Key Terms
كلّي (kulli): A universal; that which is attributed to the whole (the universal) is itself universal in the relative sense. [Note: This is relative universal, not the logical universal such as genus. In later texts, 'kulli' usually refers to the five universals.]
جزئي (juz'i): A particular; that which is attributed to the part (the particular) is particular in the relative sense. [Note: Relative particular as opposed to the true particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi).]
بالذات (bi al-dhat): Essentially; universality and particularity are essentially considered in meanings (concepts). [Note: Stable across tradition.]
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally; words are called universal only accidentally because of the meaning they signify. [Note: Common distinction in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A concept that applies to many individuals essentially
B) A concept that is attributed to the universal itself
C) A word that is universal accidentally
D) A genus that has species

Key Takeaway
Universality and particularity are correlatives: each is defined in relation to the other, and they are essential in meanings but accidental in words.

The True Species: Complete Essence of Particulars
Author defines true species as complete essence of particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true species and distinguish it from other universals based on whether it is the complete essence of its particulars.

Core Idea
The true species is the universal that fully constitutes the essence of its particulars, whether many or one.

This passage defines one of the five universals: the true species. Unlike genus or accident, the true species is the complete essence of the things under it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division of the universal
The author divides the universal into two: either it is the complete essence of its particulars (entering into them) or it is outside them (accident). Focus on the first type.
2
Recognize the true species
The first type is the true species. It is a universal that fully constitutes the quiddity of its particulars.
3
Note the two subdivisions
The true species can have multiple individuals (e.g., 'human') or a single individual (e.g., 'sun'). Both are true species as long as they answer 'what is it?'.
4
Understand the criterion
The true species is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa). This distinguishes it from accidents, which answer 'what kind?' or 'how much?'.
5
Distinguish from logical species
The true species (al-naw' al-haqiqi) is a real essence, while the logical species (al-naw' al-mantiqi) is a subjective concept. This passage focuses on the real one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والكلّيّ إما أن يكون تمام ماهيّة ماتحته من الجزئيات وداخلاً فيها أو خارجاً عنها، والأول هو النوع الحقيقي» سواء كان متعدد الأشخاص- و هو المقول في جواب «ما هو بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً كالإنسان أو غير متعدّد الأشخاص - وهو المقول في جواب «ما هو » بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كالشمس – فهو إذن كلّيّ مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب «ماهو».
He said: The universal is either the complete essence of what is under it among the particulars, either entering into them or being outside them. The first is the true species, whether it has multiple individuals – and it is what is said in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of commonality and specificity together, like 'human', or it does not have multiple individuals – and it is what is said in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of pure specificity, like 'sun'. So it is a universal predicated of one or many agree in realities in answer to 'what is it?'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage defines the true species (al-naw' al-haqiqi). The author begins by dividing the universal: it is either the complete essence of its particulars, either entering into them (as a constituent) or being outside them (as an accident). The first type is the true species. It has two subdivisions: (1) a species with multiple individuals, e.g., 'human', which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' with both commonality (shared by many) and specificity (distinguishing them); (2) a species with only one individual, e.g., 'sun', which is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' with pure specificity. In both cases, the true species is a universal that can be said of one or many things that share the same reality in answer to the question 'what is it?'.

Key Terms

النوع الحقيقي

Key Takeaway
The true species is a universal that fully constitutes the essence of its particulars, answering 'what is it?' for one or many individuals.

Defining Species (al-Naw') as the First Universal
Author defines species as the universal that is the essence of its particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of species (al-naw') as the universal that is the complete essence of its particulars, distinguish the two meanings of dhātī (essential), and apply the definition to classify a given universal as species or not.

Core Idea
Species is the universal that is the very essence of its particulars.

The five universals are fundamental to Islamic logic. Here, the author defines the first universal: species (al-naw'). He explains that a universal relates to its particulars either as part of their essence (essential) or external (accidental). Understanding species as the essence itself is critical for proper definition and classification in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations between a universal and its particulars
Look at the first sentence: the universal is either internal to the essence of its particulars (dhātī) or external ('araḍī). This is the basic division. Find the Arabic terms: الذاتي (essential) and العرضي (accidental).
The author then gives a second, broader meaning of dhātī: not external, which includes the essence itself.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'essential' (dhātī)
The passage says dhātī can mean either 'internal to the essence' (the parts) or 'not external' (including the whole essence). Note which is more common according to the sharh.
The author's use of 'ربما' (rubba mā) indicates the first meaning is more common.
3
Recognize species as the universal that is the very essence
The author states: 'The first—i.e., the universal that is the very essence of its particulars—is species.' Example: 'human' is the essence of Zayd and 'Amr. Individuals differ only by accidental features external to the essence.
Species is the complete essence; it is not just a part (like genus).
4
Apply the 'What is it?' test to verify species
The passage explains that species answers 'What is it?' (ماهو) both commonly (when asked about a group) and specifically (when asked about one individual). This is because the question seeks the complete essence. Use this test to check if a universal is a species.
For example, 'human' answers 'What is Zayd?' (specific) and 'What are Zayd and 'Amr?' (common).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس .۱۳۱ ماهيتها وداخلاً فيها أو خارجاً عنها، والداخل يسمى ذاتياً، والخارج عرضيّاً. وربما يقال : «الذاتي» على ماليس بخارج، وهذا أعم من الأول. والأول - أي الكلّي الذي يكون نفس ماهية ماتحته من الجزئيات - هو «النوع» كالإنسان، فإنّه نفس ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرها من جزئياته، وهي لاتزيد على الإنسان إلا بعوارض مشخصة خارجة عنه بها يمتاز شخص عن شخص آخر. ثم النوع لا يخلو إما أن يكون متعدّد الأشخاص في الخارج أولا يكون، فإن كان متعدد الأشخاص في الخارج فهو المقول في جواب «ماهو» بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً لأنّ السؤال بـ«ما هو» عن الشيء إنما هو لطلب تمام ماهيته وحقيقته، فإن كان السؤال سؤالاً عن شيء واحد كان طالباً لتمام الماهية المختصة به، وإن جمع بين شيئين أو أشياء في (١) أي عن الماهية، فيتناول الذاتي بهذا المعنى الماهيّة، لأنها ليست خارجة عن نفسها، ويتناول أجزاءها المنقسمة إلى الجنس والفصل. وأما الذاتي بالمعنى الأول - أي الداخل في الماهية – فيختص بالأجزاء. وفي قوله : «ربما» إشارة إلى أن إطلاق الذاتي على المعنى الأول أشهر (شریف). (٢) يعني أن أفراد الإنسان لا تشتمل إلا على الإنسانية وعوارض مشخصة موجبة للمنع عن قبول فرض الاشتراك، وليست تلك العوارض معتبرة في ماهية تلك الأفراد، بل في كونها أشخاصاً معينة ممتازة بعضها عن بعض، فيكون الإنسانية تمام ماهيّة كلّ فرد من تلك الأفراد (شريف).
The five universals. [Page 131] Their essence (mahiyya) is either internal to [the particulars] or external to them. The internal is called essential (dhātī), and the external accidental ('araḍī). Sometimes it is said that 'essential' applies to what is not external, and this is more general than the first. The first—i.e., the universal that is the very essence of its particulars—is 'species' (naw'), such as 'human', for it is the very essence of Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and other individuals of it, and they have nothing over and above 'human' except individuating accidents external to it by which one person is distinguished from another. Then the species is either multiple in external existence or not. If it is multiple in external existence, then it is said in answer to 'What is it?' (ma huwa) both commonly and specifically, because the question 'What is it?' about a thing seeks its complete essence and reality. If the question is about one thing, it seeks the complete essence specific to it, and if it combines two or more things... (1) i.e., regarding the essence (mahiyya), so 'essential' in this sense includes the essence itself because it is not external to itself, and includes its parts divided into genus and differentia. But the essential in the first sense—i.e., internal to the essence—is specific to the parts. In his saying 'perhaps' (rubba mā) is an indication that the usage of 'essential' for the first meaning is more common (Sharīf). (2) Meaning that the individuals of 'human' comprise only humanity and individuating accidents that prevent the possibility of sharing, and those accidents are not considered in the essence of those individuals, but in their being distinct individuals distinguished from each other. Thus humanity is the complete essence of each individual of those individuals (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the five universals: species (al-naw'). It begins by stating the two ways a universal can relate to its particulars: either internal (essential) or external (accidental). The internal is called dhātī (essential), the external 'araḍī (accidental). A second, broader meaning of dhātī is also mentioned: that which is not external, which includes the essence itself and its parts. The first meaning (internal to the essence) is the usual one. The first universal that is the very essence of its particulars is species, exemplified by 'human'. Human is the essence of Zayd, 'Amr, etc., and the individuals differ only by individuating accidents (a'raḍ mushakhkhiṣa) external to the essence. Then the passage discusses whether a species has multiple individuals or a single one. If it has multiple individuals, it is used in answer to 'What is it?' (ma huwa) in two ways: commonly (when asking about a group) and specifically (when asking about one individual), because the question seeks the complete essence. The commentary (Sharīf) explains that the broader meaning of dhātī includes the essence itself and its parts (genus and differentia), while the narrower meaning (internal) is limited to the parts. The author's phrase 'perhaps' (rubba mā) indicates that the narrower usage is more common. The second commentary clarifies that individuals of human consist only of humanity and individuating accidents that prevent sharing (i.e., make each individual distinct); those accidents are not part of the essence but are what make individuals distinct. Thus humanity is the complete essence of each individual.

العرضي
الماهية

C) It is any universal that can be predicated of multiple things.

Key Takeaway
Species is the universal that is the complete essence of its particulars, and it answers the question 'What is it?' both commonly and specifically.

Defining Species (al-Naw') and Its Predication in Answer to 'What Is It?'
Author defines species as a universal predicated of one or many in answer to 'what is it?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define species (al-naw') as a universal that is the complete quiddity of its particulars and determine how it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' for both single-individual and multi-individual species.

Core Idea
Species is the universal that gives the complete quiddity of its individuals, answering 'what is it?' whether they are one or many.

The five universals are the building blocks of every definition. Central among them is species (al-naw'), which captures the very essence of the things it describes. This lesson unpacks the precise definition of species: it is the universal that forms the complete quiddity (māhiyya) of its particulars. We will see how it functions when answering the question 'what is it?' for a single individual (like the sun) or for many individuals (like humans). Understanding this will sharpen your grasp of definitional logic used across the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential from accidental
The passage first distinguishes what is inside the quiddity (essential, dhātī) from what is outside (accidental, 'araḍī). Essential is either the whole quiddity (species) or a part (genus/differentia). Recognise that species is the first kind of essential universal.
Essential here includes the broader sense: anything not outside the quiddity.
2
Identify species as the quiddity of its particulars
The author defines species as the universal that is the very quiddity of its particulars. Example: 'human' (insān) is the quiddity of Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr. Their individual differences come from external accidents (like height or colour).
Do not confuse species with genus; genus is a part of the quiddity, not the whole.
3
Analyse predication in answer to 'what is it?' for multi-individual species
When a species has multiple individuals (like human), asking 'what is it?' about one individual (e.g., Zayd) elicits the species name as the complete quiddity specific to him. Asking about more than one (e.g., Zayd and 'Amr) also elicits the same species name because it captures the common quiddity. Thus the species is predicated in both particular and common modes.
The question 'what is it?' always seeks the complete quiddity.
4
Analyse predication for single-individual species
If a species has only one individual (like the sun), it is predicated solely in particularity. Since no other individual exists, the question 'what is it?' can only refer to that one, so the answer is the species name itself (the sun). There is no common predication.
The definition must cover both cases.
5
Extract the logical definition of species
The passage concludes with the formal definition: species is a universal predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. Here 'universal' is the genus, and 'predicated of one or many in answer to what is it' is the differentia. This ensures both single-individual and multi-individual species are included.
Check that the definition truly captures all instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس . ماهيتها وداخلاً فيها أو خارجاً عنها، والداخل يسمى ذاتياً، والخارج عرضيّاً. وربما يقال : «الذاتي» على ماليس بخارج، وهذا أعم من الأول. والأول - أي الكلّي الذي يكون نفس ماهية ماتحته من الجزئيات - هو «النوع» كالإنسان، فإنّه نفس ماهية زيد وعمرو وبكر وغيرها من جزئياته، وهي لاتزيد على الإنسان إلا بعوارض مشخصة خارجة عنه بها يمتاز شخص عن شخص آخر. ثم النوع لا يخلو إما أن يكون متعدّد الأشخاص في الخارج أولا يكون، فإن كان متعدد الأشخاص في الخارج فهو المقول في جواب «ماهو» بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً لأنّ السؤال بـ«ما هو» عن الشيء إنما هو لطلب تمام ماهيته وحقيقته، فإن كان السؤال سؤالاً عن شيء واحد كان طالباً لتمام الماهية المختصة به، وإن جمع بين شيئين أو أشياء في السؤال كان طالباً لتمام ماهيتها، وتمام ماهية الأشياء إنما يكون بتمام الماهية المشتركة بينها ؛ ولما كان النوع متعدّد الأشخاص – كالإنسان – كان هو تمام ماهية كل واحد من أفراده. فإذا سئل عن زيد مثلاً بـ «ما هو ؟ » كان المقول في الجواب «هو الإنسان» لأنه تمام الماهية المختصة به، وإن سئل عن زيد وعمرو بمـا هما ؟ كان الجواب «الإنسان» أيضاً، لأنّه كمال ماهيّتهما المشتركة بينهما، فلاجرم يكون مقولاً في جواب مـا هـو بحسب الخصوصية والشركة معاً. وإن لم يكن متعدد الأشخاص، بل ينحصر نوعه في شخص واحد - كالشمس - كان مقولا في جواب ما هو بحسب الخصوصية المحضة، لأن السائل بـ«ما هو عن ذلك الشخص لا يطلب إلا تمام الماهية المختصة به، إذ لافرد آخر له في الخارج حتى يجمع بينه وبين ذلك الشخص في السؤال، حتى يكون طالباً لتمام الماهية المشتركة. وإذا علمت أن النوع إن تعددت أشخاصه في الخارج كان مقولاً على كثيرين في جواب ما هو كالإنسان، وإن لم تتعدد كان مقولاً على واحد في جواب ما هو ، فهو إذن كلّي مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو ». فـ «الكلّي» جنس، وقولنا : مقول على واحد ليدخل في الحد النوع الغير المتعدد الأشخاص. وقولنا : «أو على كثيرين ليدخل في الحد النوع المتعدد الأشخاص،
The five universals. Their quiddity: that which is inside it or outside it; the inside is called essential, the outside accidental. And sometimes 'essential' is said of that which is not outside, and this is more general than the first. The first—i.e., the universal that is the very quiddity of the particulars under it—is the 'species', like 'human', for it is the very quiddity of Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and other particulars of it, and they do not exceed 'human' except by individuating accidents external to it by which one person is distinguished from another. Then the species is either one that has multiple individuals in the external world or not. If it has multiple individuals in the external world, then it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of both commonality and particularity together, because the question 'what is it?' about a thing is only to seek its complete quiddity and reality. If the question is about one thing, it seeks the complete quiddity specific to it; and if it combines two or more things in the question, it seeks the complete quiddity of them; and the complete quiddity of things is only by the complete common quiddity among them. Since the species has multiple individuals—like 'human'—it is the complete quiddity of each one of its individuals. So if one asks about Zayd, for example, 'What is he?', the answer is 'He is human', because it is the complete quiddity specific to him; and if one asks about Zayd and 'Amr, 'What are they?', the answer is 'human' as well, because it is the complete quiddity common between them. Hence it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of particularity and commonality together. And if it does not have multiple individuals, but its species is confined to one individual—like the sun—then it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in terms of pure particularity, because the questioner asking 'what is it?' about that person does not seek except the complete quiddity specific to him, since there is no other individual for it in the external world so that he would combine it with that person in the question, such that he would be seeking the complete common quiddity. And if you know that the species, if its individuals are multiple in the external world, is predicated of many in answer to 'what is it?' like 'human', and if not multiple, is predicated of one in answer to 'what is it?', then it is a universal predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. So 'universal' is the genus, and our saying 'predicated of one' is to include in the definition the species whose individuals are not multiple; and our saying 'or of many' is to include in the definition the species whose individuals are multiple.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines 'species' (al-naw') as one of the five universals. It begins by distinguishing essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) predicates: what is inside the quiddity is essential, what is outside is accidental. Then it introduces the first kind of essential universal, which is the very quiddity of its particulars—this is species. Example: 'insan' (human) is the quiddity of Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr. Their individual differences come from external accidents. The passage then explains how species is predicated in answer to 'ma huwa' (what is it?). It notes that the question 'what is it?' seeks the complete quiddity. If the species has multiple individuals (like insan), it answers both when asked about one individual (e.g., Zayd) and when asked about multiple (e.g., Zayd and 'Amr). So it combines particularity and commonality. If the species has only one individual (like the sun), it answers only with particularity, because there is no other to share. Finally, the passage gives the logical definition: species is a universal predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'. The genus in this definition is 'universal'; the differentia is 'predicated of one or many in answer to what is it'. This ensures both single-individual species and multi-individual species are included.

C) With both particularity and commonality together.
D) It is not predicated in answer to 'what is it?'.

Key Takeaway
Species is the universal that constitutes the complete quiddity of its particulars and is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' for one or many individuals, whether unique or multiple.

The Five Universals
beginner
10 min
Definition of Species (Naw') and Analysis of Its Definition
Defines the species as a universal predicate in answer to 'what is it?' and analyzes each part of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and the four conditions in the definition of species and explain what each condition excludes.

Core Idea
The species is defined by five conditions that precisely distinguish it from the other universals.

In classical logic, the species (al-naw') is a universal predicable that answers the question 'What is it?'. The passage below from Qutbi's logic course defines the species and carefully explains each part of its definition. Let us dissect this definition step by step to understand how it distinguishes the species from the other four universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and overall definition
The passage defines al-naw' (species). Read the full definition: 'a universal said of one or of many agreeing in realities in answer to What is it?'.
2
Extract the genus (al-jins) of the definition
The genus of species is 'universal' (kulli). This is the broadest category that includes all the five predicables.
3
Analyze the condition 'said of one or of many'
This condition ensures that species can have a single individual (like the sun) or multiple individuals (like humans). It includes both cases.
4
Analyze the condition 'agreeing in realities'
This excludes the genus, because genus is said of many differing in realities (e.g., 'animal' is said of humans and horses, which differ in reality). It also excludes remote differentiae and properties of genera.
5
Analyze the condition 'in answer to What is it?'
This excludes the remaining three: differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and general accident ('arad 'amm), because they answer 'Which thing is it?' or are not complete quiddities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال كان طالباً لتمام ماهيتها، وتمام ماهية الأشياء إنما يكون بتمام الماهية المشتركة بينها ؛ ولما كان النوع متعدّد الأشخاص – كالإنسان – كان هو تمام ماهية كل واحد من أفراده.
فإذا سئل عن زيد مثلاً بـ «ما هو ؟ » كان المقول في الجواب «هو الإنسان» لأنه تمام الماهية المختصة به، وإن سئل عن زيد وعمرو بـمـا هما ؟ كان الجواب «الإنسان» أيضاً، لأنّه كمال ماهيّتهما المشتركة بينهما، فلاجرم يكون مقولاً في جواب مـا هـو بحسب الخصوصية والشركة معاً.
وإن لم يكن متعدد الأشخاص، بل ينحصر نوعه في شخص واحد - كالشمس - كان مقولا في جواب ما هو بحسب الخصوصية المحضة، لأن السائل بـ«ما هو عن ذلك الشخص لا يطلب إلا تمام الماهية المختصة به، إذ لافرد آخر له في الخارج حتى يجمع بينه وبين ذلك الشخص في السؤال، حتى يكون طالباً لتمام الماهية المشتركة.
وإذا علمت أن النوع إن تعددت أشخاصه في الخارج كان مقولاً على كثيرين في جواب ما هو كالإنسان، وإن لم تتعدد كان مقولاً على واحد في جواب ما هو ، فهو إذن كلّي مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو ».
فـ «الكلّي» جنس، وقولنا : مقول على واحد ليدخل في الحد النوع الغير المتعدد الأشخاص.
وقولنا : «أو على كثيرين ليدخل في الحد النوع المتعدد الأشخاص، وقولنا : «متفقين بالحقائق» ليخرج الجنس)، فإنه مقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق، وقولنا : «في جواب ماهو» يخرج الثلاثة الباقية، أعني الفصل والخاصة والعرض العام، لأنها لاتقال في جواب «ما هو ؟.
(۱) هذا القيد يخرج الجنس مطلقاً - كماذكره - ويخرج العرض العام أيضاً مطلقا، ويخرج الفصول البعيدة - كالحسّاس والنامي وقابل الأبعاد - و يخرج أيضاً خواص الأجناس كالماشي، فإنه وإن كان عرضاً عاماً بالقياس إلى الإنسان مثلاً، لكنه خاصة بالقياس إلى الحيوان.
وأما القيد الأخير - أعني في جواب ما هو » - فإنه يخرج الفصول مطلقاً - قريبة كانت أو بعيدة - ويخرج الخواص أيضاً مطلقاً – سواء كانت خواص الأنواع أو الأجناس - فكان إسناد إخراج الفصول والخواص إلى القيد الأخير أولى.
وأما إخراج العرض العام، فقد قيل : إسناده إلى الأول أولى، وإنما أسند إلى الثاني رعاية لإدراجه مع الخاصة المشاركة إياه في العرضية في سلك الإخراج بقيد واحد (شريف).
(٢) أما العرض العام فلا يقال في جواب «ماهو»، لأنه ليس تمام ماهية لما هو عرض عام له، ولا في جواب «أي شيء هو » لأنه ليس مميزاً لما هو عرض عام له. وأما الفصل والخاصة فلا يقالان في جواب «ما هو» لأنهما ليسا تمام ماهية لما كانا فصلاً وخاصة له، ويقالان في جواب أيّ شيء هو » لأنهما يميزانه؛ فالفصل يقال في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره والخاصة في جواب «أي شيء هو في عرضه». وأما النوع والجنس فيقالان في جواب ماهو. أما النوع فلانه تمام الماهية المشتركة بين الأفراد المتفقة الحقيقة. وأما الجنس فلانه تمام الماهية المشتركة بين الأفراد المختلفة الحقيقة، وسيرد عليك تفاصيل هذه المعاني (شريف).
The question was a seeker of the completion of its quiddity, and the completion of the quiddity of things is only by the completion of the quiddity common among them. And since the species is multiple in individuals – like 'human' – it is the completion of the quiddity of each one of its individuals. So if Zaid, for example, is asked with 'What is it?', what is said in the answer is 'He is human' because it is the completion of the quiddity specific to him. And if Zaid and 'Amr are asked with 'What are they?', the answer is also 'human', because it is the perfection of their quiddity shared between them. Therefore, it is inevitably said in answer to 'What is it?' according to both specificity and sharing together. And if it is not multiple in individuals, but its species is confined to a single individual – like the sun – it is said in answer to 'What is it?' according to pure specificity, because the questioner by 'What is it?' about that person does not seek except the completion of the quiddity specific to him, since there is no other individual for it in external existence so that he could combine between it and that person in the question, such that he would be a seeker of the completion of the common quiddity. And since you know that the species – if its individuals are multiple externally – is said of many in answer to 'What is it?' like 'human', and if not multiple, it is said of one in answer to 'What is it?', it is therefore a universal said of one or of many agreeing in realities in answer to 'What is it?'. So 'universal' is the genus. And our saying 'said of one' is to include in the definition the species not multiple in individuals. And our saying 'or of many' is to include in the definition the species multiple in individuals. And our saying 'agreeing in realities' is to exclude genus, for it is said of many differing in realities. And our saying 'in answer to 'What is it?'' excludes the remaining three, namely differentia, property, and general accident, because they are not said in answer to 'What is it?'. (1) This condition excludes genus absolutely – as he mentioned – and also excludes general accident absolutely, and excludes remote differentiae – like sentient, growing, and capable of dimensions – and also excludes properties of genera like walking, for although it is a general accident relative to man, for example, it is a property relative to animal. As for the last condition – namely 'in answer to 'What is it?'' – it excludes differentiae absolutely, whether proximate or remote, and also excludes properties absolutely, whether properties of species or genera. So attributing the exclusion of differentiae and properties to the last condition is more appropriate. As for the exclusion of general accident, it has been said: attributing it to the first condition is more appropriate, but it was attributed to the second out of consideration for including it together with property, which shares with it in accidentally, in the category of exclusion by one condition (Sharif). (2) As for general accident, it is not said in answer to 'What is it?' because it is not the complete quiddity of that of which it is a general accident, nor in answer to 'Which thing is it?' because it does not distinguish that of which it is a general accident. As for differentia and property, they are not said in answer to 'What is it?' because they are not the complete quiddity of that of which they are differentia and property, and they are said in answer to 'Which thing is it?' because they distinguish it. The differentia is said in answer to 'Which thing is it? with respect to its essence, and the property is said in answer to 'Which thing is it? with respect to its accident. As for species and genus, they are said in answer to 'What is it?'. As for species, because it is the complete quiddity common among individuals agreeing in reality. As for genus, because it is the complete quiddity common among individuals differing in reality. And details of these meanings will come to you (Sharif).
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the species (naw') as one of the five universal predicables and analyzes each part of its definition. The author begins by explaining why the species is the complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya) of its individuals: the question 'What is it?' seeks the complete essence, and for multiple individuals (e.g., Zaid and 'Amr), the species 'human' is the common complete essence. For a species with a single individual (e.g., the sun), it is the specific complete essence. So the species is a universal that can be predicated of one or many individuals that agree in reality, in answer to 'What is it?'. The definition is then broken down: 'universal' is the genus; 'said of one' covers species with a single individual; 'said of many' covers species with multiple individuals; 'agreeing in realities' excludes the genus (which covers differing realities); and 'in answer to 'What is it?'' excludes the other four predicables: differentia, property, and general accident. The commentary also discusses finer points: the condition 'agreeing in realities' also excludes remote differentiae and properties of genera. The last condition ('in answer to 'What is it?'') is better suited to exclude differentiae and properties. The general accident is excluded by the first condition. The translation and commentary use Islamic examples: Zaid and 'Amr as individuals of the species 'human', and the sun as a species with one individual.

Defining Species with Two Conditions
Author explains the definition of species with two restrictions to exclude other universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions of 'agreeing in realities' and 'answering 'what is it?'' to distinguish species from genus, differencia, property, and general accident.

Core Idea
Species is defined by two conditions that together separate it from the other four universals.

In this lesson, we examine the precise definition of species (al-naw') as one of the five universals. The author provides two conditions—'agreeing in realities' and 'in answer to 'what is it?''—and then shows how each condition excludes certain other universals. Understanding these exclusions is crucial for correctly classifying any universal predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions in the definition
Locate the two conditions that make up the definition of species: 'agreeing in realities' (muttafiqin bi-l-haqa'iqi) and 'in answer to what is it?' (fi jawab ma huwa). These appear in the first two lines of the Arabic passage.
The definition is: 'the universal said of many agreeing in realities in answer to what is it?'
2
Determine which universal each condition excludes
The first condition excludes genus because genus is said of many differing in realities. The second condition excludes differencia, property, and general accident because they are not said in answer to 'what is it?'.
The commentary notes that the first condition also excludes general accident and remote differencias, while the second excludes all differencias and properties.
3
Analyze the rationale for each exclusion
For each universal, ask: Is it said of many agreeing in realities? Is it said in answer to 'what is it?'? Species and genus answer 'what is it?' because they give the complete quiddity. Differencia and property answer 'which thing is it?' (distinguishing), not 'what is it?'. General accident answers neither.
Use the table in the commentary: species and genus → 'what is it?'; differencia and property → 'which thing is it?'; general accident → neither.
4
Apply the definition to a concrete example
Take 'man' as species. Predicate 'animal' is genus (said of many differing realities: man, horse). 'Rational' is differencia (answers 'which thing is it in its essence?'). 'Laughing' is property (answers 'which thing is it in its accident?'). 'Walking' is general accident when said of man (not essential, not distinguishing).
Walk through each predicate and check if it satisfies both conditions for species.

وأما القيد الأخير - أعني في جواب ما هو » - فإنه يخرج الفصول مطلقاً - قريبة كانت أو بعيدة - ويخرج الخواص أيضاً مطلقاً – سواء كانت خواص الأنواع أو الأجناس - فكان إسناد إخراج الفصول والخواص إلى القيد الأخير أولى.
وأما إخراج العرض العام، فقد قيل : إسناده إلى الأول أولى، وإنما أسند إلى الثاني رعاية لإدراجه مع الخاصة المشاركة إياه في العرضية في سلك الإخراج بقيد واحد (شريف). (٢) أما العرض العام فلا يقال في جواب «ماهو»، لأنه ليس تمام ماهية لما هو عرض عام له، ولا في جواب «أي شيء هو » لأنه ليس مميزاً لما هو عرض عام له. وأما الفصل والخاصة فلا يقالان في جواب «ما هو» لأنهما ليسا تمام ماهية لما كانا فصلاً وخاصة له، ويقالان في جواب أيّ شيء هو » لأنهما يميزانه؛ فالفصل يقال في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره والخاصة في جواب «أي شيء هو في عرضه». وأما النوع والجنس فيقالان في جواب ماهو. أما النوع فلانه تمام الماهية المشتركة بين الأفراد المتفقة الحقيقة. وأما الجنس فلانه تمام الماهية المشتركة بين الأفراد المختلفة الحقيقة، وسيرد عليك تفاصيل هذه المعاني (شريف).
The five universals. And our statement: 'agreeing in realities' excludes genus, for it is said of many differing in realities. And our statement: 'in answer to 'what is it?' excludes the remaining three, namely differencia, property, and general accident, because they are not said in answer to 'what is it?' (1) This condition excludes genus absolutely – as he mentioned – and also excludes general accident absolutely, and excludes remote differencias – like sensitive, growing, and receptive of dimensions – and also excludes properties of genera like walking; for although it is a general accident in relation to man for example, it is a property in relation to animal. As for the last condition – namely 'in answer to what is it' – it excludes differencias absolutely, whether near or remote, and also excludes properties absolutely, whether properties of species or genera. Thus attributing the exclusion of differencias and properties to the last condition is more appropriate. As for the exclusion of general accident, it was said: its attribution to the first condition is more appropriate, but it was attributed to the second to include it with the property sharing with it in accidentally in the line of exclusion by one condition (Sharif). (2) As for general accident, it is not said in answer to 'what is it' because it is not the complete quiddity of that for which it is a general accident, nor in answer to 'which thing is it' because it does not distinguish that for which it is a general accident. As for differencia and property, they are not said in answer to 'what is it' because they are not the complete quiddity of that for which they are differencia and property, and they are said in answer to 'which thing is it' because they distinguish it: the differencia is said in answer to 'which thing is it in its essence', and the property in answer to 'which thing is it in its accident'. As for species and genus, they are said in answer to 'what is it'. Species because it is the complete quiddity common among individuals agreeing in reality. Genus because it is the complete quiddity common among individuals differing in reality. And details of these meanings will come to you (Sharif).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the definition of species (al-naw') as 'that which is said of many agreeing in reality in answer to 'what is it?' The author then shows how each condition excludes certain universals. The first condition 'agreeing in realities' excludes genus because genus is said of many differing in realities (e.g., animal said of man and horse). The second condition 'in answer to what is it' excludes the remaining three: differencia (al-fasl), property (al-khassa), and general accident (al-'ard al-'amm). The commentary then notes that the first condition also excludes general accident absolutely and remote differencias and properties of genera. The second condition excludes differencias and properties absolutely. The author argues that attributing the exclusion of differencias and properties to the second condition is more appropriate. Then it is mentioned that some said attributing the exclusion of general accident to the first condition is better, but it was attributed to the second to include it with property in a single condition. Then the author explains why each universal is or is not said in answer to 'what is it' or 'which thing is it'. Species and genus are said in answer to 'what is it' because they give the complete quiddity. Differencia and property are said in answer to 'which thing is it' because they distinguish. General accident is said in neither. This passage uses examples from Islamic philosophy and logic, such as 'walking' as a property of animal and a general accident of man.

Critique and Refinement of the Definition of Species
Author critiques the definition of species and discusses inclusion of non-existent concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in the standard definition of species and reconstruct the corrected definition that includes non-existent quiddities.

Core Idea
The definition of species must include non-existent quiddities, or the classification of universals collapses.

The definition of species as 'said of one and many differing in kind' has a hidden dilemma: it either includes a redundancy or excludes non-existent concepts like the phoenix. In this passage, the commentator Sharif exposes this flaw and proposes a refined definition that preserves the comprehensiveness of the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma in the original definition
The original definition of species includes 'said of one and many differing in kind'. The commentator points out that 'the many' (الكثيرين) is ambiguous. Determine the two possible interpretations and their consequences.
The passage states: 'إما اشتمال التعريف على أمر مستدرج، وإما أن لا يكون التعريف جامعاً'.
2
Analyze the redundancy problem
If 'many' is taken absolutely (including mental existents), then 'said of one' becomes redundant because a species with few external individuals (e.g., the sun) is still said of many in the mind. This makes the definition include an unnecessary element.
The commentator argues that 'said of one' is then زائداً حشواً (redundant).
3
Analyze the exclusion problem
If 'many' is restricted to externally existing individuals, then species with no external existence (e.g., the phoenix) are excluded from the definition, making it non-comprehensive (غير جامع).
The passage asks: 'كالعنقاء' as an example of a non-existent species.
4
Examine the commentator's proposed correction
The commentator (Sharif) proposes deleting both 'of one' and 'the universal', leaving only 'said of many'. The new definition becomes: 'The species is what is said of many that agree in reality in answer to "what is it?"'. This solves both problems by relying solely on 'said of many' which inherently indicates universality without restricting to actual existence.
The passage says: 'والصواب أن يحذف من التعريف قوله «على واحد»، بل لفظ «الكلّي» أيضاً، فإنّ «المقول على كثيرين» يغني عنه'.
5
Apply the corrected definition to a non-existent concept
Consider the concept 'phoenix' (العنقاء). According to the corrected definition, is it a species? Yes, because it is said of many (in the mind) and they agree in reality (the quiddity of phoenix) in answer to 'what is it?'. This shows the definition includes non-existent quiddities while excluding accidental universals.
The commentator emphasizes that 'ما هو' asks about quiddity (ماهية), which is broader than existence.

Source Text

وهناك نظر، و هو أن أحد الأمرين لازم، إما اشتمال التعريف على أمر مستدرج، وإما أن لا يكون التعريف جامعاً : لأن المراد بـ «الكثيرين» إن كان مطلقاً - سواء كانوا موجودين في الخارج أو لم يكونوا - فيلزم أن يكون قوله المقول على واحد زائداً حشواً، لأن النوع الغير المتعدّد الأشخاص في الخارج مقول على كثيرين موجودين في الذهن، وإن كان المراد بـ «الكثيرين» الموجودين في الخارج : يخرج عن التعريف الأنواع التي لا وجود لها في الخارج أصلاً - كالعنقاء - فلايكون جامعاً.
والصواب أن يحذف من التعريف قوله «على واحد»، بل لفظ «الكلّي» أيضاً ، فإنّ «المقول على كثيرين» يغني عنه، ويقال «النوع هو المقول على كثيرين متفقين بالحقيقة في جواب ما هو »، وحينئذ يكون كل نوع مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً.
(۱) وذلك أن مفهوم «الكلي» هو مفهوم المقول على كثيرين بعينه، إلا أن لفظ «الكلي» يدل عليه إجمالا، ولفظ «المقول على كثيرين» تفصيلاً. لا يقال : مفهوم الكلّي هو الصالح لأن يقال بالفرض على كثيرين، ومفهوم المقول على كثيرين ما كان مقولاً على كثيرين بالفعل، فلايغني عنه، لأن دلالة «المقول بالفعل» على الصالح لأن يقال على كثيرين» التزام، ودلالة الالتزام ليست معتبرة في التعريفات. لأنا نقول : لم يرد بـ «المقول على كثيرين في تعريف الكليات إلا الصالح لأن يقال على كثيرين، إذ لو أريد به المقول بالفعل» لخرج عن تعريف الكليات مفهومات كلية ليس لها أفراد موجودة في الخارج ولا في الذهن، فإنّها لا تكون مقولة بالفعل بل بالصلاحية، فيكون المقول على كثيرين بمعنى الكلي، فيغني عنه (شريف).
والمصنّف لما اعتبر النوع في قوله « في جواب ما هو بحسب الخارج قسمه إلى ما يقال بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً، وإلى مايقال بحسب الخصوصية المحضة ؛ وهو خروج عن هذا الفن من وجهين :
أما أولاً : فلان نظر الفنّ عام يشمل المواد كلها، فالتخصيص بالنوع الخارجي ينافي ذلك).
(۱) فإن قلت : «ما هو » سؤال عن الحقيقة ولا حقيقة إلا للموجودات الخارجية ، فيلزم حينئذ التخصيص بـ«النوع الخارجي» قطعاً. قلت : «ماهو » سؤال عن الماهية ، و هي أعم من أن تكون موجودة في الخارج أم لا، وكيف يجوز التخصيص بالنوع الخارجي مع وجوب انحصار الكلّي في الخمسة ، فإن المفهومات التي لم يوجد شيء من أفرادها التي هي من تمام ماهيتها - كالعنقاء - مثلاً - لا يندرج في غير النوع قطعاً فلو أخرج عنه لم ينحصر الكلّي في الأقسام الخمسة، ولا يجوز أن يقال : «المعتبر في الكلي أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج ولو في ضمن فرد واحد» لأن ماسبق من مفهوم الكلي يتناول الموجود والمعدوم والممكن والممتنع، وسيأتي تقسيم الكلي بحسب الوجود في الخارج إلى هذه الأقسام.
.
نعم، المقصود الأصلي معرفة أحوال الموجودات، إذ لاكمال يعتد به في معرفة أحوال المعدومات ، إلا أن قواعد الفن شاملة لجميع المفهومات - معدومة كانت أو موجودة ممكنة كانت أو ممتنعة - والمقصود الأصلي من هذا الفن أن تستعمل في معرفة أحوال الموجودات الحقيقية، وقد تستعمل في معرفة المفهومات الاعتبارية وبيان أحوالها، فإن هذه المعرفة يحتاج إليها في معرفة أحوال الموجودات الحقيقية، ولذلك قيل : «لولا الاعتبارات لبطلت الحكمة» (شريف).
And there is a critique, which is that one of two things is necessary: either the definition includes a redundant element, or it is not comprehensive. Because if by 'the many' is meant absolutely—whether they exist externally or not—then his statement 'said of one' becomes redundant, since the species that does not have multiple individuals externally is said of many existing in the mind. And if by 'the many' is meant those existing externally, then species that have no existence externally at all—like the phoenix—would be excluded from the definition, so it would not be comprehensive.
The correct thing is to delete from the definition his statement 'of one', and even the word 'the universal', for 'said of many' suffices instead of it. And it is said: 'The species is what is said of many that agree in reality in answer to "what is it?"'. Then every species is said in answer to 'what is it?' according to both commonality and specificity.
(1) That is because the concept of 'the universal' is precisely the concept of 'said of many', except that the word 'universal' indicates it summarily, and the word 'said of many' indicates it in detail. It cannot be said: the concept of the universal is what is fit to be said by hypothesis of many, and the concept of 'said of many' is what is actually said of many, so it does not suffice, because the implication of 'actually said' upon 'fit to be said of many' is an implication, and implication is not considered in definitions. For we say: By 'said of many' in the definition of universals is intended only what is fit to be said of many, because if 'actually said' were intended, then universal concepts that have no individuals existing externally or in the mind would be excluded from the definition of universals, for they are not actually said but only by fitness. Thus 'said of many' has the meaning of 'universal', so it suffices instead of it. (Sharif)
And the author, when he considered the species in his statement 'in answer to "what is it?" according to external existence', divided it into what is said according to commonality and specificity together, and into what is said according to pure specificity. This is a departure from this art in two respects:
As for the first: because the concern of the art is general, covering all matters, so particularization to the external species contradicts that.
(1) For if you say: 'What is it?' is a question about reality, and there is no reality except for external existents, so then particularization to the 'external species' necessarily follows. I say: 'What is it?' is a question about quiddity, and it is more general than being existent externally or not. And how is particularization to the external species permissible while the universal must be confined to the five? For concepts that have none of their individuals existing—which are part of their complete quiddity—like the phoenix, for example—are certainly not included in anything other than the species. So if they were excluded, the universal would not be confined to the five divisions. And it cannot be said: 'The relevant condition in the universal is that it exists externally even within a single individual', because what preceded about the concept of the universal includes the existent and the non-existent, the possible and the impossible. Later will come the division of the universal according to external existence into these divisions.
Yes, the primary aim is knowledge of the states of existents, since there is no perfection to be relied upon in knowing the states of non-existents. However, the rules of the art encompass all concepts—whether non-existent or existent, possible or impossible. And the primary aim of this art is that it be used in knowing the states of true existents, but it may be used in knowing conceptual matters and explaining their states, for this knowledge is needed in knowing the states of true existents. That is why it is said: 'Were it not for conceptual considerations, wisdom would be invalid'. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the standard definition of 'species' (النوع) as a universal. The commentator (Sharif) first points out a dilemma in the existing definition that includes both 'said of one' and 'said of many'. If 'many' refers to any many (including mental), then 'said of one' is redundant because a species with few individuals (like the sun) is still said of many in the mind. If 'many' means only externally existing individuals, then species like the phoenix (which has no external individuals) are excluded, making the definition non-comprehensive. The commentator proposes deleting 'said of one' and even 'the universal' from the definition, since 'said of many' alone suffices. The definition then becomes: 'The species is what is said of many that agree in reality in answer to "what is it?"'.

Then a side issue is addressed: the concept of 'universal' is identical to 'said of many', but 'universal' indicates it concisely and 'said of many' in detail. An objector might argue that 'universal' is potential (fit to be said) while 'said of many' is actual, so they differ. The reply is that in the definition of universals, 'said of many' is intended to mean 'fit to be said', not 'actually said'; otherwise, universal concepts with no individuals (e.g., 'Anqa) would be excluded. Thus 'said of many' carries the same meaning as 'universal', and no extra word is needed.

The commentator then criticizes the original author (المصنف) for restricting species to those answering 'what is it?' according to external existence. This restriction is a departure from the art of logic in two ways: (1) logic considers all matters, not just external species; (2) it would exclude non-existent species, breaking the doctrine that universals are confined to five. An objection that 'what is it?' asks about reality and reality is only external is refuted: 'what is it?' asks about quiddity (ماهية), which includes both existent and non-existent quiddities. Non-existent concepts like the phoenix must be included in species; otherwise, they would not fit any of the five universals, contradicting their exhaustive classification. Finally, the commentator clarifies that while the primary aim of logic is knowledge of existents, its rules apply to all concepts, and such conceptual considerations are necessary for wisdom.

المقول على كثيرين
ما هو
الماهية

The Generality of Logic and the Scope of Species
First critique: restricting species to external existence violates the generality of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why restricting species to external existents contradicts the universal scope of logic.

Core Idea
Logic covers all concepts, existent or not; species must include non-existents like the phoenix.

The author attempts to divide species based on external existence, but the commentator argues this violates the generality of logic. This lesson unpacks the first critique: logic's subject matter is universal, encompassing both existent and non-existent quiddities. Understanding this ensures the completeness of the five universals classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's division
Find the author's statement dividing species into two types based on external existence: one said by commonality and specificity (genus+differentia) and one by pure specificity (differentia alone).
The division is given in the first line of the Arabic passage.
2
Locate the first objection
The commentator states that this specification to external existence 'is a departure from this art in two respects: first, because the scope of the art is general, encompassing all subject matters.'
The objection is that restricting to external species contradicts the generality of logic.
3
Examine the hypothetical objection and reply
An objector claims 'what is it?' asks about reality, which only applies to external existents. The reply: 'what is it?' asks about quiddity, which is broader than external existence.
This reply establishes that non-existent quiddities are included in the scope of 'what is it?'.
4
Analyze the phoenix example
The commentator uses the phoenix (al-'anqā') as an example of a concept with no external individuals. If such concepts were not considered species, the universal would not be confined to the five universals.
This demonstrates that species must include non-existent concepts to maintain the exclusivity of the five universals.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The universal's concept includes existent, non-existent, possible, and impossible. Therefore, restricting species to external existence is invalid.
The primary aim may be knowledge of existents, but the rules of logic apply to all concepts.

وَالمُصَنِّفُ لَمَّا اعْتَبَرَ النَّوْعَ فِي قَوْلِهِ « فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْخَارِجِ قَسَمَهُ إِلَى مَا يُقَالُ بِحَسَبِ الشَّرِكَةِ وَالْخُصُوصِيَّةِ مَعًا، وَإِلَى مَا يُقَالُ بِحَسَبِ الْخُصُوصِيَّةِ الْمَحْضَةِ » وَهُوَ خُرُوجٌ عَنْ هَذَا الْفَنِّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ: أَمَّا أَوَّلًا: فَلَانَّ نَظَرَ الْفَنِّ عَامٌّ يَشْمَلُ الْمَوَادَّ كُلَّهَا، فَالتَّخْصِيصُ بِالنَّوْعِ الْخَارِجِيِّ يُنَافِي ذَلِكَ. (١) فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: «مَا هُوَ » سُؤَالٌ عَنِ الْحَقِيقَةِ وَلَا حَقِيقَةَ إِلَّا لِلْمَوْجُودَاتِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ ، فَيَلْزَمُ حِينَئِذٍ التَّخْصِيصُ بِـ«النَّوْعِ الْخَارِجِيِّ» قَطْعًا. قُلْتُ: «مَاهُوَ » سُؤَالٌ عَنِ الْمَاهَِيَّةِ ، وَ هِيَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَوْجُودَةً فِي الْخَارِجِ أَمْ لَا، وَكَيْفَ يَجُوزُ التَّخْصِيصُ بِالنَّوْعِ الْخَارِجِيِّ مَعَ وُجُوبِ انْحِصَارِ الْكُلِّيِّ فِي الْخَمْسَةِ ، فَإِنَّ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الَّتِي لَمْ يُوجَدْ شَيْءٌ مِنْ أَفْرَادِهَا الَّتِي هِيَ مِنْ تَمَامِ مَاهِيَّتِهَا - كَالْعَنْقَاءِ - مَثَلًا - لَا تَنْدَرِجُ فِي غَيْرِ النَّوْعِ قَطْعًا فَلَوْ أُخْرِجَ عَنْهُ لَمْ يَنْحَصِرِ الْكُلِّيُّ فِي الْأَقْسَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «الْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْكُلِّيِّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْجُودًا فِي الْخَارِجِ وَلَوْ فِي ضِمْنِ فَرْدٍ وَاحِدٍ» لِأَنَّ مَاسَبَقَ مِنْ مَفْهُومِ الْكُلِّيِّ يَتَنَاوَلُ الْمَوْجُودَ وَالْمَعْدُومَ وَالْمُمْكِنَ وَالْمُمْتَنِعَ، وَسَيَأْتِي تَقْسِيمُ الْكُلِّيِّ بِحَسَبِ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْخَارِجِ إِلَى هَذِهِ الْأَقْسَامِ. نَعَمْ، الْمَقْصُودُ الْأَصْلِيُّ مَعْرِفَةُ أَحْوَالِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ، إِذْ لَاكَمَالَ يُعْتَدُّ بِهِ فِي مَعْرِفَةِ أَحْوَالِ الْمَعْدُومَاتِ ، إِلَّا أَنَّ قَواعِدَ الْفَنِّ شَامِلَةٌ لِجَمِيعِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ - مَعْدُومَةً كَانَتْ أَوْ مَوْجُودَةً مُمكنَةً كَانَتْ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةً - وَالْمَقْصُودُ الْأَصْلِيُّ مِنْ هَذَا الْفَنِّ أَنْ تُسْتَعْمَلَ فِي مَعْرِفَةِ أَحْوَالِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ، وَقَدْ تُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي مَعْرِفَةِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الِاعْتِبَارِيَّةِ وَبَيَانِ أَحْوَالِهَا، فَإِنَّ هَذِهِ الْمَعْرِفَةَ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا فِي مَعْرِفَةِ أَحْوَالِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ، وَلِذَلِكَ قِيلَ : «لَوْلَا الِاعْتِبَارَاتُ لَبَطَلَتِ الْحِكْمَةُ» (شَرِيف). وأما ثَانِيًا فَلَأَنَّ الْمَقُولَ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ » بِحَسَبِ الْخُصُوصِيَّةِ الْمَحْضَةِ عِنْدَهُمْ هُوَ «الْحَدُّ» بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْمَحْدُودِ، وَقَدْ جَعَلَهُ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ النَّوْعِ.
The author, when he considered the species in his statement 'in answer to 'what is it' according to external existence, he divided it into what is said in terms of commonality and specificity together, and into what is said in terms of pure specificity.' And this is a departure from this art in two respects: First, because the scope of the art is general, encompassing all subject matters, so specifying it to external species contradicts that. (1) If you say: 'What is it?' is a question about reality, and there is no reality except for external existents, then necessarily there would be specification to the external species. I say: 'What is it?' is a question about quiddity, and it is more general than whether it exists externally or not. How can specification to external species be allowed given the necessity that the universal is confined to the five? For concepts of which none of their individuals that are part of their complete quiddity exist — like the phoenix, for example — definitely do not fall under anything other than the species. So if it were excluded from it, the universal would not be confined to the five divisions. And it is not allowed to say: 'What is considered in the universal is that it exists externally, even if within a single individual,' because the earlier concept of the universal includes the existent, the non-existent, the possible, and the impossible, and soon the division of the universal according to external existence into these divisions will come. Yes, the primary purpose is knowledge of the states of existents, since there is no perfection worthy of consideration in knowing the states of non-existents. However, the rules of the art encompass all concepts — whether non-existent or existent, possible or impossible — and the primary purpose of this art is to be used in knowing the states of true existents, and it may be used in knowing conceptual mental considerations and explaining their states, for this knowledge is needed in knowing the states of true existents. Therefore it was said: 'Were it not for mental considerations, wisdom would be invalidated' (Sharif). And secondly, because what is said in answer to 'what is it' according to pure specificity is, according to them, the definition relative to the defined, and yet he made it [one] of the divisions of species.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage critiques the author's (al-Qutb al-Tahtani) classification of species into two types: one said in terms of both commonality and specificity (i.e., referring to a share of both genus and differentia) and one said in terms of pure specificity (i.e., pure differentia). The commentator argues this is a departure from the art of logic in two ways. First, logic's subject matter is general, covering all material, so restricting species to external existence contradicts that generality. A hypothetical objector raises that 'what is it?' asks about reality, which only applies to external existents, thus necessitating restriction to external species. The response: 'what is it?' asks about quiddity, which is broader than external existence. Non-existent concepts like the phoenix must still be classified as species; otherwise, the universal would not be confined to the five universals. Additionally, the concept of universal already covers existent, non-existent, possible, and impossible. The commentator then concedes that the primary aim is knowledge of existents, but the rules of logic cover all concepts, and even mental considerations are needed for that primary aim. Second, the author mistakenly classified 'said according to pure specificity' as a species, but actually it is the definition (hadd) relative to the defined, not a species. Thus the author's division is flawed.

الخارج
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must non-existent concepts like the phoenix be considered species?
A) Because external species do not exist in the mind.
B) Because the universal must include non-existent concepts like the phoenix.
C) Because the author's division is based on a false premise.
D) Because logic does not study external existents.

Key Takeaway
The universal must include non-existent concepts; species cannot be restricted to external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a non-existent concept in Islamic theology (e.g., the phoenix, or a hypothetical person) and explain why it must be classified as a species under the five universals. Write a brief argument supporting this using the critique in the passage.

Differentiating Species from Definition
Second critique: confusing 'pure specificity' with a species when it is actually a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between species and definition (hadd) when answering 'what is it?' in pure specificity.

Core Idea
Pure specificity answers the 'what is it' as a definition, not a species.

The author's second error is classifying 'what is said in pure specificity' as a species. The commentator clarifies that, in logic, such an answer is the definition (hadd), which gives the essence of the defined. This lesson examines why this misclassification undermines the proper understanding of the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the author's division
The author divides species into two: one said by commonality and specificity (genus+differentia) and one said by pure specificity (differentia alone).
The second type is the focus of this critique.
2
Understand the second objection
The commentator states: 'what is said in answer to 'what is it' according to pure specificity is, according to them, the definition relative to the defined.'
Thus, the author mistakenly treated the definition as a species.
3
Recognize the difference
A species is a universal predicated of multiple individuals in answer to 'what is it?'. A definition (hadd) is a statement revealing the essence through genus and differentia. Pure specificity (differentia alone) is not a species but part of a definition.
The definition often combines genus and differentia; pure specificity alone is the differentia, which is not a complete species.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'qiyas' is 'applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared 'illah'. This is a hadd, not a species. If someone called it a species of qiyas, they would confuse the classification.
This shows the practical importance of accurate classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
