The Five Universals
intermediate
8 min
Defining Genus: The Complete Common Part
Author defines genus as the complete common part in answer to 'what is it'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus of a quiddity by verifying that its part is the complete common part shared with another species.

Core Idea
Genus is the complete common part beyond which no further commonality exists.

In the five universals, the genus is the foundation of definition. But not every shared feature counts as a 'genus'—only the part that is completely common and answers the question 'what is it?' The logicians carefully specify that the genus must be the 'complete common part' (tamām al-juz' al-mushtarak), distinguishing it from the differentia and establishing the basis for proximate and remote genera.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the parts of the quiddity
Identify the universal parts that constitute the quiddity (the 'whatness'). The author states that every universal part is either a genus or a differentia. Determine which part you are examining.
2
Check commonality with another species
Ask whether this part is shared between the quiddity and at least one other species. If yes, it may be a genus. If not, it is likely a differentia.
3
Verify it is the complete common part
Confirm that there is no further common part beyond this one shared with the other species. The 'complete common part' means you cannot go higher while still including the same species. This is the defining condition for genus.
4
Classify as proximate or remote genus
If the part is common with only one other species (or with multiple but is the highest common part), it is a 'proximate genus' (jins qarīb). If it is common with one species but there is a higher genus that also includes it, it is a 'remote genus' (jins ba'īd). The absolute genus condition just requires being the complete common part with some species.
The text gives examples: if the part is shared with only one other species and is the complete part, it is proximate; if shared with many but is complete only relative to some, it may be remote.

He said: And if it is the second [type of part], and if it is the complete common part between it [the quiddity] and another species, then it is what is said in answer to 'what is it' in terms of pure commonality, and it is called 'genus', and they described it as 'a universal predicated of many differing in realities in answer to what is it'. I say: The universal that is a part of the quiddity is confined to the genus of the quiddity and its differentia, because either it is the complete common part between the quiddity and another species, or it is not. And what is meant by the complete common part between the quiddity and another species is the common part beyond which there is no common part between them, i.e., a common part (this much — I mean the part being the complete common part between the quiddity and another species only — is sufficient for it to be a genus. For if the part is common between the quiddity and another species only and is the complete common part between them, it is a proximate genus for it. And if the part is common between the quiddity and two other species or other species and is the complete common part between the quiddity and the two other species or the other species, it is also a proximate genus for the quiddity. But if it is the complete common part between it and one of the two species or species, it is a remote genus for it. So what is considered in the absolute genus is that it is the complete common part between the quiddity and another species, whether it is the complete common part in relation to everything that shares with the quiddity in that genus, or not. And soon you will know this meaning. So his statement 'or it is not' means that the part is not the complete common part between the quiddity and any species at all (Sharif).
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines the genus (jins) as one of the five universals. It begins by stating that if the part of the quiddity under consideration is the complete common part shared with another species, then it is the genus. The genus is described as a universal predicated of many things differing in reality (i.e., by their species) in answer to 'what is it'. The author then explains that the universal which is a part of the quiddity can only be either a genus or a differentia. The key condition for being a genus is that it is the complete common part (tamām al-juz' al-mushtarak): that is, a common part beyond which there is no further common part between the quiddity and another species. The footnote (by Sharif) elaborates: if the part is common only with one other species and is the complete common part, it is a proximate genus (jins qarīb). If it is common with two or more other species and is the complete common part among them, it is still a proximate genus (relative to those species). If it is the complete common part with only one of those species, it is a remote genus (jins ba'īd). The absolute condition for being a genus is simply being the complete common part between the quiddity and some (at least one) other species, regardless of how many species share it. The phrase 'or it is not' in the matn refers to the case where the part is not the complete common part with any species, which would be the differentia.

تمام الجزء المشترك
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد

Key Takeaway
The genus is defined not merely by commonality but by being the 'complete common part' shared with another species, a condition that distinguishes it from the differentia and determines whether it is proximate or remote.

Near and Distant Genus: Definitions and Hierarchy
Definition of near and distant genus with examples and rule for degrees of distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between near and distant genus and calculate the degree of distance using the logicians' rule.

Core Idea
Nearness and distance of genus depend on whether the answer to 'what is it?' is the same for all participants or not.

In defining any essence, logicians ask 'what is it?' The answer reveals the genus. But not all genera are equally close—some are immediate, others remote. This lesson unpacks the criteria for near and distant genus, using the classic hierarchy for the species 'man' as a concrete example. You will learn to calculate exactly how many levels distant a genus is, a skill essential for precise definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the 'what is it?' question
The passage begins by asserting that the answer to 'what is it?' excludes the remaining universals (property, differentia, common accident). This sets the stage: genus is that which answers 'what is it?'.
Focus on the exclusion: differentiae answer 'which kind?' not 'what?'.
2
Define near genus (jins qarīb)
A genus is near if the answer about the quiddity and some participants is identical to the answer about the quiddity and all participants. Example: 'animal' for man—the same answer covers man and horse.
The key is that the genus fully captures the common essence across all species sharing it.
3
Define distant genus (jins baʿīd)
A genus is distant if the answer about the quiddity and some participants differs from the answer about it and other participants. Example: 'growing body' for man—works for man and plant, but not for stone.
Multiple answers exist at the same level; each different answer indicates a degree of distance.
4
Trace the hierarchical ordering for man
The logicians arranged: man (species), animal (near genus), growing body, absolute body, substance. Each higher genus is more distant.
Notice that growing body is near genus for animal but distant for man—relativity of near/distant.
5
Calculate the degree of distance
Count the number of distinct answers that cover all participants of the species, then subtract one. The remainder is the degree. With respect to realities in answer to 'what is it?', and in answer to 'what is it?' the remaining universals are excluded, namely the property, the differentia, and the common accident. He said: It is 'near' if the answer about the quiddity and about some things that share it is the same as the answer about it and about everything that shares it, like 'animal' with respect to man. And 'distant' if the answer about it and about some things that share it is different from the answer about it and about some others, and there are two answers if it is distant by one rank, like 'growing body' with respect to man, three answers if distant by two ranks, like 'body', and four answers if distant by three ranks, like 'substance' — and analogously. I say: The scholars have arranged the universals so that exemplification becomes easy for the beginner learner. So they placed: man, then animal, then growing body, then absolute body, then substance. (1) By it are also excluded the differentiae of species and their properties, but the last restriction — namely 'in answer to what is it' — excludes differentiae and properties absolutely; therefore the exclusion is attributed to it. As for common accident, it is not excluded except by the last restriction (Sharif). (2) It is not hidden from you that universal principles are not clear to the beginner except through particular examples, (2) therefore you see the books of these scholars filled with examples to facilitate learning for the beginner. The masters of this art mentioned in their investigations particular examples for facilitation; they presented in the investigations of universals examples from the specific universals, and in the arrangement of species and genera, specific universals are arranged as he explained (Sharif). Commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: Man is a species — as you know — and animal is a genus for it because it is the complete common quiddity between man and horse. Similarly, growing body is a genus for man and plants because it is the complete common part between man and plants, such that if one asks about both 'what are they?' the answer is 'growing body'. Likewise, absolute body is a genus for it because it is the complete common part between it and, e.g., stone. Likewise, substance is a genus for it because it is the complete common quiddity between it and intellect. So it is evident that it is possible for a single quiddity to have different genera, some above others. And once this is engraved on the tablet of the mind, we say: Genus is either near or distant, because if the answer about the quiddity and about (1) You know that genus must be the complete commonality between the quiddity and other things. Either it is the complete commonality with respect to everything that shares the quiddity or not. The first must be an answer about the quiddity and all its participants, so the answer about the quiddity and some of its participants is the same as the answer about it and all participants, and this is called 'near genus'. The second — that which is not the complete commonality except with respect to some participants — occurs as an answer about the quiddity and some participants but not others, so the answer about the quiddity and some participants is different from the answer about it and the others, and this is called 'distant genus'. The rule for knowing degrees of distance is to consider the number of answers encompassing all participants, subtract one, and the remainder is the degree of distance. Know that growing body is a distant genus for man by one degree, and a near genus for animal because it is a relative species composed of the near genus which is growing body
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the division of genus into near (qarib) and distant (ba'id). It begins by establishing that when answering 'what is it?' (ma huwa), the remaining universals (property, differentia, common accident) are excluded. The definition of near genus: the answer about the quiddity and about some of its participants is the same as the answer about the quiddity and all participants. Example: 'animal' is near genus for man because whether you ask 'what is man?' or 'what is horse?' the answer 'animal' applies equally. Distant genus: the answer about the quiddity and some participants is different from the answer about the quiddity and other participants. Example: 'growing body' for man: if asked about man and horse, the answer 'growing body' works for man but not horse (horse is not vegetable); for man and tree, 'growing body' works. So one answer for some, another for others. The text then gives a series of increasingly distant genera: man's genera are animal, growing body, absolute body, substance. Each step up adds a broader answer. The commentator (Sharif) adds that the restriction 'in answer to what is it?' excludes differentiae and properties because they answer 'which one is it?' not 'what is it?'. Common accident is excluded only by the last restriction. The author then emphasizes that logicians use particular examples like man, animal, etc., to make universal concepts clear to beginners. The passage continues by explaining that one quiddity (like man) can have multiple genera stacked in hierarchy. The final part provides a precise rule for determining degree of distance: count the number of distinct answers encompassing all participants, subtract one, and the result is the degree. For man: answer 'substance' covers all, 'body' covers all but not plants? Actually the rule applied to man: participants are man, horse, plant, stone, intellect. The number of answers that cover all? The text seems incomplete but illustrates with growing body as distant by one degree for man, but near genus for animal (since animal's participants are only man and horse). This section solidifies the concept with a concrete hierarchy.

The nearness or distance of a genus is determined by whether the same answer to 'what is it?' applies to all participants of that species; the degree of distance is the number of distinct answers minus one.

Proximate vs. Remote Genus: Unity of Answer
Author defines proximate and remote genus with hierarchy and number of answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proximate genus from remote genus by analyzing whether the answer to 'what is it?' is identical for all species sharing that genus.

Core Idea
Proximate genus gives one answer for all; remote genus demands more than one.

In the five universals, genus is divided into proximate and remote based on how many answers the question 'what is it?' requires. This distinction is vital for constructing correct definitions (hadd) and understanding hierarchical classification. The author defines both concepts with clear examples: animal as proximate genus for human and horse, and growing body as remote for human.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus under examination
Start with a genus term, e.g., 'animal' or 'growing body'. Note its scope.
2
List the species that share that genus
For 'animal', include human and horse. For 'growing body', include human, horse, and plants.
3
Check if the answer to 'what is it?' is the same for all those species
If the answer 'animal' works identically for human and horse, it is a proximate genus. If 'growing body' works for human and plants but not for horse (which needs 'animal'), then it is remote.
4
Classify the genus and explain why
Based on the check, conclude whether the genus is proximate or remote, and state the reason using the author's criterion.

الكليات الخمس
بعض ما يشاركها في ذلك الجنس عين الجواب عنها وعن جميع مشاركاتها فيه فهو القريب»، كالحيوان فإنه جواب عن السؤال عن الإنسان والفرس بـ «ماهما وهو الجواب بعينه عنه وعن جميع الأنواع المشاركة للإنسان في الحيوانية.
وإن كان الجواب عن الماهية وعن بعض مشاركاتها في ذلك الجنس غير الجواب عنها وعن بعض الآخر، فهو «البعيد» كـ«الجسم النامي»، فإن النباتات والحيوانات تشارك الإنسان فيه، وهو الجواب عنه وعن المشاركات النباتية - لا المشاركات الحيوانية - بل الجواب عنه وعن المشاركات الحيوانية «الحيوان»، ويكون هناك جوابان إن كان الجنس بعيداً بمرتبة واحدة - كالجسم النامي بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، فإن الحيوان جواب و هوجواب آخر - وثلاثة أجوبة إن كان بعيداً بمرتبتين - كالجسم المطلق بالقياس إليه فإن الحيوان والجسم النامي جوابان وهو جواب ثالث- وأربعة أجوبة إن كان بعيداً بثلاث مراتب كالجوهر، فإن الحيوان
والجسم النامي والجسم المطلق أجوبة ثلاثة وهو جواب رابع؛ وعلى هذا القياس؛ فكلما يزيد البعد يزيد عدد الأجوبة ويكون عدد الأجوبة زائداً على عدد مراتب البعد بواحد، لأنّ الجنس القريب جواب، ولكل مرتبة من مراتب البعد جواب آخر.
واعلم أيضاً أن ترتيب الأجناس مما لا يجب، بل يجوز أن تتركب ماهية من جنس قريب لا يكون فوقه جنس ولا تحته جنس - كما سيأتي عن قريب هذه المعاني مفصلة (شريف).
The five universals. Some of what shares with it in that genus is the same answer for it and for all its participants in it, so it is the 'proximate', like 'animal' for it is the answer to the question about human and horse 'what are they?' and it is the same answer for it and for all the types sharing with human in animality. And if the answer about the quiddity and about some of its participants in that genus is not the same answer as for some others, then it is the 'remote', like 'growing body', for plants and animals share with human in it, and it is the answer for it and for the plant participants - not the animal participants - rather the answer for it and for the animal participants is 'animal', and there are two answers if the genus is remote by one degree - like 'growing body' relative to human, for 'animal' is one answer and it is another answer - and three answers if remote by two degrees - like 'absolute body' relative to it, for 'animal' and 'growing body' are two answers and it is a third - and four answers if remote by three degrees like 'substance', for 'animal', 'growing body', and 'absolute body' are three answers and it is a fourth; and on this analogy; the more the remoteness increases, the number of answers increases, and the number of answers is one more than the number of degrees of remoteness, because the proximate genus is one answer, and for each degree of remoteness there is another answer. Know also that the ordering of genera is not necessary; rather it is permissible that a quiddity be composed of a proximate genus that has no genus above it and no genus below it - as these meanings will come in detail soon (Sharif).
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of genus: proximate (qarīb) and remote (baʿīd). The author begins by explaining that a genus is called 'proximate' when it is the single answer to the question 'what is it?' for a species and for all other species sharing that genus. For example, 'animal' is the proximate genus of both human and horse because it answers the question for both identically. In contrast, a 'remote' genus is one where the answer differs for some participants. The example given is 'growing body' (jism nāmī): it is the answer for human and plants (which are both growing bodies), but for animals (which are also growing bodies), the answer 'animal' is used instead. The author then quantifies the number of answers (ajwiba) based on the degrees of remoteness. If the genus is remote by one degree (e.g., growing body for human), there are two answers: the proximate genus (animal) and the remote genus (growing body). If remote by two degrees (e.g., absolute body for human), there are three answers: animal, growing body, and absolute body. If remote by three degrees (e.g., substance for human), there are four answers: animal, growing body, absolute body, and substance. The rule: the number of answers equals the number of remoteness degrees plus one, because the proximate genus counts as one answer and each additional remote level adds one. Finally, the author notes that this hierarchical arrangement is not obligatory; it is possible for a quiddity to have a proximate genus with no genera above or below it.

الجنس البعيد

A) جوابان
B) ثلاثة أجوبة
C) أربعة أجوبة
D) جواب واحد

Key Takeaway
The proximate genus provides a single answer for all species; remote genus requires additional answers for different subgroups.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Using the definition of 'substance' (jawhar) in kalam, identify whether it is a proximate or remote genus for 'human' and state how many answers would be required.

Counting Answers by Degrees of Remoteness
Author defines proximate and remote genus with hierarchy and number of answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of answers needed to define a quiddity given the degrees of remoteness of its genus, and explain the rule: number of answers = degrees of remoteness + 1.

Core Idea
Each degree of remoteness adds one more answer: number of answers = remoteness + 1.

The author provides a clear quantitative rule: the number of distinct definitions (answers to 'what is it?') equals the number of degrees the genus is removed from the species plus one. This rule is illustrated with examples from human: one degree gives two answers (animal and growing body), two degrees give three (including absolute body), three degrees give four (including substance). Understanding this helps in parsing complex definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quiddity and its remotest genus
Choose a species (e.g., human) and identify the most remote genus mentioned (e.g., substance).
2
Determine the degrees of remoteness
Count how many genera lie between the species and that remote genus. For human: animal (proximate), growing body (first remote), absolute body (second remote), substance (third remote). So 3 degrees.
The proximate genus does not count as remote.
3
Apply the formula: answers = degrees + 1
Add 1 to the number of degrees. For 3 degrees, answers = 4. Those answers are: animal, growing body, absolute body, substance.
4
Verify with the author's examples
Check the passage: one degree gives two answers, two degrees give three, etc. Confirm consistency.
5
Note the exception: ordering is not necessary
The author says it is permissible for a quiddity to have a proximate genus with no genus above or below, meaning the hierarchy is not fixed for all quiddities.

Types of Differentia: Unshared and Specific Shared Part
Author defines differentia as a part of the quiddity that is either unshared or partially shared but specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of differentia (unshared vs. a specific part of a shared attribute) and distinguish them from genus in any given definition.

Core Idea
The differentia distinguishes the species either by being unprecedented or by being a specific form of a common trait.

We now move from genus to its counterpart, the differentia. The author explains that when a part of a quiddity is not the entire commonality shared with another type, it must be either completely unshared (like rationality for humans) or a specific part of a shared attribute (like sentience for animals as a specific kind of sensation). The commentary unpacks the logical necessity of this disjunction, ensuring we can correctly classify parts of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the part of the quiddity under examination
Look for a constituent component of the thing being defined, such as 'rational' in human or 'sentient' in animal.
The part must be a real attribute, not accidental.
2
Check if this part is the entirety of what is shared (tamam al-mushtarak) with another type
If the part equals the entire common attribute between the quiddity and another type, it is a genus, not a differentia. For example, 'body' is shared between human and animal, but if it were the entire commonality (which it is not, because there are other attributes), it might be genus.
In the text, 'tamam al-mushtarak' is the full set of shared attributes.
3
If not the entire commonality, determine whether it is completely unshared or a specific part of a shared attribute
If the part is not found in any other type at all, it is an unshared differentia (like 'rational'). If it is a specific instance of a broader shared attribute (e.g., 'sentient' is a specific form of 'sensation' shared by all animals), then it is a differentia that is a part of the shared attribute.
The text gives 'sentient' (al-hassas) as an example of the second type.
4
Confirm it distinguishes the quiddity from its participants in genus or existence
In both cases, the part serves to single out the quiddity from others that share a genus (like other animals) or just exist (like inanimate objects). This is the defining function of a differentia.
The author says 'fa kana faslan' - so it is a differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن لم يكن تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر، فلابد إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين نوع آخر أصلاً - كالناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان - أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له - كالحساس - وإلا لكان مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين نوع آخر؛ ولا يجوز أن يكون تمام المشترك بالنسبة إلى ذلك النوع - لأن المقدر خلافه - بل بعضه، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى مايساويه فيكون فصل جنس»؛ وكيفما كان يميز الماهية عن مشاركيها في جنس أو في وجود فكان فصلاً.
القول : هذا بيان للشق الثاني من الترديد، وهو أن جزء الماهية إن لم يكن تمام الجزء المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر يكون «فصلاً»، وذلك لأنّ أحد الأمرين لازم على ذلك التقدير، وهو أن ذلك الجزء إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلا بين الماهية ونوع آخر، أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له، وأيا ما كان يكون «فصلاً».
أما لزوم أحد الأمرين : فلأن الجزء إن لم يكن تمام المشترك، فإما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلاً - كالنطق - وهو الأمر الأول، أو يكون مشتركاً ولا يكون تمام المشترك - بل بعضه - وهو الأمر الثاني ; فذلك البعض إما
He said: And if it is not the entirety of what is shared between it and another type, then necessarily either it is not shared at all between the quiddity and another type - like 'rational' with respect to human - or it is a part of the entirety of what is shared that is equal to it - like 'sentient' - otherwise it would be shared between the quiddity and another type; and it is not permissible that it be the entirety of what is shared with respect to that type - because the supposition is contrary - but rather a part of it, and it does not regress infinitely but ends at what is equal to it, so it is the differentia of a genus; and however it is, it distinguishes the quiddity from its participants in a genus or in existence, so it is a differentia. The commentary: This is an explanation of the second branch of the disjunction, namely that a part of the quiddity, if it is not the entirety of the shared part between it and another type, is a 'differentia', because one of two things is necessarily entailed on that supposition, namely that that part either is not at all shared between the quiddity and another type, or is a part of the entirety of what is shared that is equal to it, and whichever it is, it is a 'differentia'. As for the necessity of one of the two: because the part, if it is not the entirety of what is shared, then either it is not shared at all - like 'speech' (natiq) - and that is the first case, or it is shared but not the entirety of what is shared - rather a part of it - and that is the second case; then that part is either...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines 'differentia' (al-faṣl). The author begins by stating the condition: if a part of a quiddity is not the entire commonality (tamām al-mushtarak) between that quiddity and another type, then necessarily one of two things holds: either that part is not shared at all with any other type (like 'rational' for human), or it is a part of the commonality that is equal to it (like 'sentient'). If it were the entire commonality, it would be a genus, not a differentia. The author also explains that the differentia must be specific enough to distinguish the quiddity from others that share a genus or existence. The commentary (القول) clarifies this is the second branch of a disjunction (the first being that the part is the entire commonality, which is genus). It restates that the part, if not the entire commonality, must fall into one of the two categories, and in either case it is a differentia. The text ends while explaining the necessity of one of the two cases, but the core definition and the two types of differentia are clear: a differentia is either completely unique (like rational) or a specific part of a shared attribute (like sentient, which is a specific kind of sensation).

Conditions for a part to be a differentia
Author defines differentia and rules out impossible relations between the part and the commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a proposed part of a quiddity can serve as a differentia by applying the conditions of being unshared or co-extensive with the commonality.

Core Idea
A differentia must be either exclusive to the species or exactly co-extensive with the genus.

This passage specifies the exact conditions under which a part of the quiddity functions as a differentia (fasl). By analyzing the relation between the part and the commonality (genus), we learn to distinguish valid differentiae from impossible ones. This is essential for constructing correct definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the candidate part and the commonality
Locate the part of the quiddity being considered as a differentia and the commonality (e.g., the genus) that it shares with another species. For example, 'rational' as a part of 'man' and 'animal' as the commonality.
2
Check if the part is entirely unshared
If the part does not apply to any other species (e.g., 'rational' is only for man among animals), then it is a differentia. This is the first permissible case.
3
If shared, check if it is a co-extensive part of the commonality
If the part is shared but applies to exactly the same things as the commonality (e.g., 'sentient' for animal), then it is also a differentia. This is the second permissible case.
4
Eliminate impossible relations
The text rules out three relations: mutually exclusive (impossible for a predicable part), more specific (would allow the whole to exist without the part), and more general (would cause the part to apply to another species without the commonality). Only equality remains.
Study the reasoning for each impossibility carefully.
5
Confirm validity
If the candidate part satisfies either the unshared or co-extensive condition, it is a valid differentia. Otherwise, it cannot serve a defining role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢١- الفصل] قال : وإن لم يكن تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر، فلابد إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين نوع آخر أصلاً - كالناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان - أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له - كالحساس - وإلا لكان مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين نوع آخر؛ ولا يجوز أن يكون تمام المشترك بالنسبة إلى ذلك النوع - لأن المقدر خلافه - بل بعضه، ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى مايساويه فيكون فصل جنس»؛ وكيفما كان يميز الماهية عن مشاركيها في جنس أو في وجود فكان فصلاً.
القول : هذا بيان للشق الثاني من الترديد، وهو أن جزء الماهية إن لم يكن تمام الجزء المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر يكون «فصلاً»، وذلك لأنّ أحد الأمرين لازم على ذلك التقدير، وهو أن ذلك الجزء إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلا بين الماهية ونوع آخر، أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له، وأيا ما كان يكون «فصلاً».

١٤٣
أن يكون مبايناً لتمام المشترك أو أخص منه أو أعم منه أو مساوياً له : لا جائز أن يكون مبايناً له، لأن الكلام في الأجزاء المحمولة، ومن المحال أن يكون المحمول على الشيء مبايناً له.
ولا أخص ، لوجود الأعم بدون الأخص، فيلزم وجود الكل بدون الجزء، وإنه محال.
ولا أعمّ ، لأن بعض تمام المشترك بين الماهية وبين نوع آخر لوكان أعم من تمام المشترك لكان موجوداً في نوع آخر بدون تمام المشترك تحقيقاً لمعنى العموم ، فيكون مشتركا بين الماهية وذلك النوع الذي هو بإزاء
(۲
(١) أي لا أخص مطلقاً ولا من وجه والالجاز وجود تمام المشترك الذي هو الكل بدون جزئه الذي هو أخص منه مطلقاً أو من وجه، وإذا لم يكن أخص من وجه لم يكن أعم من وجه أيضاً. ولك أن تقول ولا أخص» أي مطلقاً، وتجعل ولا أعم» متناولاً للأعم مطلقاً ومن وجه أيضاً.
والحاصل أن الأخص من وجه له خصوص باعتبار وعموم باعتبار، فإن شئت لاحظت خصوصه وأدرجته فيما لزم من الأخص مطلقاً وهو جواز وجود الكل بدون الجزء، وإن شئت اعتبرت عمومه وجعلته مشاركاً للأعم مطلقاً فيما لزمه من وجوده بدون تمام المشترك (شريف). (۲) قيل عليه تحقيق معنى العموم لا يتوقف على أن يكون تمام المشترك موجوداً في النوع الآخر الذي هو بإزائه، الجواز أن يكون تمام المشترك موجوداً أيضاً في هذا النوع ويكون بعض تمام المشترك أعم منه لصدقه على تمام المشترك وعلى هذا النوع، فيكون له فردان. وأما تمام المشترك فلا يصدق على نفسه إذ لا يكون الشيء فرداً لنفسه، بل يصدق على هذا النوع فيكون له فرد واحد فيكون
أخص
[Chapter 21] He said: If it is not the entirety of the commonality between it and another species, then it must either not be shared between the quiddity and another species at all - like the rational with respect to man - or be a part of the entirety of the commonality that is co-extensive with it - like the sentient - otherwise it would be shared between the quiddity and another species; and it is not permissible for it to be the entirety of the commonality with respect to that species - because the assumed is contrary - but rather a part of it, and there is no infinite regress; rather it ends at what is equal to it, so it becomes the differentia of the genus; and however it is, it distinguishes the quiddity from its partners in genus or existence, so it becomes a differentia. 
The explanation: This is an exposition of the second branch of the disjunction, which is that if a part of the quiddity is not the entirety of the common part between it and another species, it becomes a 'differentia'. And that is because one of two things is necessary upon that assumption: either that part is not shared at all between the quiddity and another species, or it is a part of the entirety of the commonality that is co-extensive with it; whichever it is, it becomes a 'differentia'. 
As for the necessity of one of the two: because if the part is not the entirety of the commonality, then either it is not shared at all - like speech (rationality) - which is the first case, or it is shared but not the entirety of the commonality - rather a part of it - which is the second case; then that part is either 
[PAGE_143] 
The Five Universals 
143 
mutually exclusive with the entirety of the commonality, or more specific than it, or more general than it, or co-extensive with it: it is not possible for it to be mutually exclusive, because we are speaking of predicable parts, and it is impossible for what is predicated of a thing to be mutually exclusive with it. 
Nor more specific, because the more general exists without the more specific, so the existence of the whole without the part would follow, and that is impossible. 
Nor more general, because if a part of the entirety of the commonality between the quiddity and another species were more general than the entirety of the commonality, then it would exist in another species without the entirety of the commonality, in realization of the meaning of generality, so it would be shared between the quiddity and that species which is opposite to it 
(2 
(1) Meaning: not absolutely more specific nor particular in some respect, because the existence of the entirety of the commonality which is the whole without its part which is more specific than it absolutely or in some respect is possible. And if it is not more specific in some respect, then it is also not more general in some respect. And you may say: 'nor more specific' i.e. absolutely, and make 'nor more general' encompassing the absolutely more general and the more general in some respect also. 
The outcome: that the more specific in some respect has particularity in one regard and generality in another regard. If you wish, consider its particularity and include it in what follows from the absolutely more specific, which is the possibility of the existence of the whole without the part. And if you wish, consider its generality and make it share with the absolutely more general in what follows from it, i.e. its existence without the entirety of the commonality (al-Sharif). (2) It is said against it: the realization of the meaning of generality does not require that the entirety of the commonality exists in the other species which is opposite to it; it is possible that the entirety of the commonality also exists in this species, and that some part of the entirety of the commonality is more general than it, because it applies to the entirety of the commonality and to this species, so it has two individuals. As for the entirety of the commonality, it does not apply to itself since a thing is not an individual of itself; rather it applies to this species, so it has one individual, so it is more specific.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl) and systematically eliminates impossible relations. The author begins by stating the two permissible cases: either the part (which could be a differentia) is not shared at all between the quiddity and any other species (like 'rational' for man, which is exclusive to man among animals), or it is a part of the commonality (the genus) that is co-extensive with it (like 'sentient' for animal, which applies to all animals and is equal to the genus 'animal' in extension). The key point is that the part must not be entirely shared with another species; otherwise it would be a common property, not a differentia. The commentary then expands on the second branch, explaining that if the part is not the whole commonality (i.e., it is a part of the genus), then it must be either not shared at all or a co-extensive part. The text then examines four possibilities for the part relative to the commonality: mutually exclusive, more specific, more general, or equal. It rules out the first three: mutually exclusive is impossible because we are dealing with predicable parts (so they must apply to the quiddity); more specific is impossible because then the more general (the commonality) could exist without it, meaning the whole exists without its part (absurd); more general is impossible because then the part would apply to another species without the commonality, making it shared across genera (undermining the definition). Only equality remains. The marginal notes by al-Sharif clarify nuances about 'more specific in some respect' and a counterargument about generality. This rigorous elimination shows that the differentia must be exactly co-extensive with the specific differentia (like 'rational' is co-extensive with 'man' only).

Key Terms
فصل
تمام المشترك
الأجزاء المحمولة

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for a differentia to be more general than the commonality (genus)?
A) Because then it would not distinguish the species
B) Because the more general exists without the commonality, making the definition invalid
C) Because it would then be a genus itself
D) Because it would be shared with another species without the genus

Key Takeaway
The differentia must be either entirely exclusive to the species or exactly co-extensive with the genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the candidate differentia 'having wings' for the species 'bird' sharing the commonality 'animal'. Using the conditions from this lesson, determine whether 'having wings' can serve as a differentia for birds. Explain your reasoning by checking the two permissible cases and eliminating impossible relations.
?
In the definition of 'salah' (prayer), one might propose 'specific actions' (ruku', sujud) as the differentia. Is this a valid differentia if 'specific actions' are co-extensive with the genus 'acts of worship'? Apply the condition of co-extensiveness.

The Impossible Relations of a Part to the Whole Common
Author refutes four impossible relations of a part to the whole common.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a part of quiddity cannot be disparate, more specific, more general, or equal to the whole common.

Core Idea
A part of quiddity must be either genus or difference.

Before defining genus and difference directly, the author sweeps away incorrect possibilities to clarify that the part of a quiddity cannot be just any relation to the common predicate shared with another species. This negative preparation sets the stage for the positive classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole common (tamām al-mushtarak)
The whole common is the entire predicate shared between the quiddity and another species. For example, 'animal' is the whole common between 'human' and 'horse'.
2
Understand the four possible relations
The author lists: disparate (mubāyin), more specific (akhass), more general (aʿamm), or equal (musāwin). He refutes each.
3
Follow the refutation of each
For disparate: impossible because something predicated must share something. For more specific: whole would exist without part, impossible. For more general: would become common to a species without the whole common, contradiction. For equal: would be the whole common itself, not a part.
4
Appreciate the method
This negative approach ensures no ambiguity remains before the positive division into genus and difference.

Source Text

أَنْ يَكُونَ مُبَايِنًا لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ أَوْ أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ أَوْ أَعَمَّ مِنْهُ أَوْ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ. لَا جَائِزَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ، لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِي الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمَحْمُولَةِ، وَمِنَ الْمُحَالِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ. وَلَا أَخَصَّ، لِوُجُودِ الْأَعَمِّ بِدُونِ الْأَخَصِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ وُجُودُ الْكُلِّ بِدُونِ الْجُزْءِ، وَإِنَّهُ مُحَالٌ. وَلَا أَعَمَّ، لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ لَوْ كَانَ أَعَمَّ مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ لَكَانَ مَوْجُودًا فِي نَوْعٍ آخَرَ بِدُونِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ تَحْقِيقًا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُومِ، فَيَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَذَلِكَ النَّوْعِ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ. وَأُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّا نُقَرِّرُ الْكَلَامَ هَكَذَا: جُزْءُ الْمَاهِيَّةِ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مَا مِنَ الْأَنْوَاعِ الْمُبَايِنَةِ لَهَا، أَوْ لَا؛ وَالْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْجِنْسُ. وَالثَّانِي إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا أَصْلًا بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ مُبَايِنٍ لَهَا، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ مُمَيِّزًا لَهَا عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمُبَايِنَاتِ. وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ مُبَايِنٍ لَهَا، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الْمُقَدَّرِ، بَلْ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَهَنَاكَ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ هُوَ بَعْضُهُ، وَجُزْؤُهُ، فَهَذَا الْبَعْضُ إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ، أَوْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا، فَالْأَوَّلُ يَكُونَ مُمَيِّزًا لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ الْمُبَايِنَةِ لَهُ، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِجِنْسِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الَّذِي هُوَ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ. وَالثَّانِي - أَعْنِي مَا يَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مَا مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ - لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَذَلِكَ النَّوْعِ الْمُبَايِنِ لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ - وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ جِنْسًا دَاخِلًا فِي الْقِسْمِ الْأَوَّلِ، لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ النَّوْعَ مُبَايِنٌ لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ أَيْضًا - فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَهَاهُنَا تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ ثَانٍ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ هُوَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الْأَوَّلِ، لِأَنَّ هَذَا النَّوْعَ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ مُبَايِنٌ لَهُ، فَلَوْ وُجِدَ فِيهِ لَكَانَ مَحْمُولًا عَلَيْهِ، لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِي الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمَحْمُولَةِ فَلَا يَكُونُ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ، فَانْدَفَعَ بِذَلِكَ كَوْنُ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي بِعَيْنِهِ هُوَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الْأَوَّلِ. لَكِنْ إِذَا قِيلَ: أَنَّ بَعْضَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ - الَّذِي كَلَامُنَا فِيهِ – إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ أَوْ لَا؛ فَالثَّانِي يَكُونُ فَصْلًا لِلْجِنْسِ الَّذِي هُوَ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي، وَالْأَوَّلُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ هَذَا النَّوْعِ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي، وَهُوَ خِلَافُ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَمَا عَرَفْتَ، وَإِمَّا يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، فَهَنَاكَ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ ثَالِثٍ...
It is either disparate from the whole common, or more specific than it, or more general than it, or equal to it. It is not possible that it is disparate from it, because the discussion is about the predicative parts, and it is impossible for something predicated on a thing to be disparate from it. Nor more specific, because the more general exists without the more specific, so the existence of the whole without the part would follow, and that is impossible. Nor more general, because if some part of the whole common between the quiddity and another species were more general than the whole common, it would exist in another species without the whole common, to realize the meaning of generality, so it would be common between the quiddity and that species which is opposite the whole common. And it was answered: We determine the discussion thus: The part of the quiddity is either the whole common between it and some species that is disparate from it, or not; the first is genus. The second is either not common at all between it and another species disparate from it, so it would be a differentia for the quiddity distinguishing it from all disparate things. Or it is common between it and another species disparate from it; then it cannot be the whole common between them because that contradicts the assumption, but it must be a part of the whole common between them, so there is a whole common which is its part and portion. Then this part either is not common between the whole common and a species disparate from it, or it is common. The first would be distinguishing the whole common from all disparate quiddities, so it would be a differentia for the genus of the quiddity which is the whole common, thus it would be a differentia for the quiddity overall. The second – I mean what is common between the whole common and some species disparate from it – cannot be the whole common between the quiddity and that species disparate from the whole common – otherwise it would be a genus included in the first division, because that species is also disparate from the quiddity – so it must be a part of the whole common between them. So here there is a second whole common, and it cannot be identical to the first whole common, because this species which is opposite the whole common is disparate from it; if it existed in it, it would be predicated on it, because the discussion is about predicative parts, so it would not be disparate from it. Thus it is repelled that the second whole common is exactly the first whole common. But if it is said: That some part of the whole common – which we are discussing – either is common between the second whole common and a species disparate from it, or not; the second is a differentia for the genus which is the second whole common; the first either is the whole common between the quiddity and this species which is opposite the second whole common, which is contrary to what is assumed as you know, or it is a part of the whole common, so there is a third whole common...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary on the Shamsiyya is a definitional and dialectical analysis of the parts of a quiddity (māhiyya) in terms of genus and difference. The author begins by refuting four impossible relations that a part of a quiddity might have to the 'whole common' (tamām al-mushtarak) – the common predicate that includes the quiddity and some other species. The part cannot be disparate, because it is predicated and thus must share something with the quiddity. It cannot be more specific, otherwise the whole common would exist without it, which is impossible since the whole includes the part. It cannot be more general, because then it would exist in a different species without the whole common, contradicting its commonality. It cannot be equal, because that would make it the whole common itself, which is not a part. After refuting these, the author presents the positive division in response to an implicit objection. The part of a quiddity is either the whole common (genus) or not. If not, it is either not common at all (difference) or common. If common, it must be a part of another whole common, and this leads to a chain. The key insight is that at some level, a part will be a difference for the genus above it, and thus indirectly a difference for the original quiddity. The passage demonstrates the hierarchical structure of genus and difference, showing that infinite regress is avoided because each step moves to a more specific whole common. The author uses the example of a quiddity and a disparate species to illustrate how parts are shared or not. This is essential for understanding how definitions are constructed from genus and difference.

C) Because it would be equal to the whole common

Key Takeaway
A part of quiddity cannot be disparate, more specific, more general, or equal to the whole common; it must be either genus or difference.

The Division of Parts into Genus and Difference
Author divides parts of quiddity into genus and difference, handling intermediate cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any part of a quiddity as either genus (jins) or difference (fasl) based on its commonality with other species.

Core Idea
Genus is the whole common between a quiddity and another species; difference is what distinguishes it from all disparate species.

After refuting impossible relations, the author presents a positive classification that breaks down the parts of a quiddity into genus and difference. The genus is the entire common predicate shared with another species; the difference is a part not shared with any disparate species. But what about parts that are shared but not the whole common? The author resolves this by showing they become differences for higher genera, ultimately contributing to the quiddity's definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole common (genus)
If the part is exactly the common predicate shared between the quiddity and a disparate species, it is the genus (jins). Example: 'animal' is genus for 'human'.
2
Identify a part not common at all (difference)
If the part is not found in any other disparate species, it is the difference (fasl). Example: 'rational' distinguishes human.
3
Handle parts that are common but not the whole common
These parts are common with another species but not the whole common. The passage shows they must be parts of another whole common, and this chain eventually stops at a difference that distinguishes that highest genus.
4
Trace the hierarchical chain
Each common part that is not the whole common becomes a part of a higher whole common, and at the top, a part that is not common with any disparate species serves as difference for that genus, indirectly contributing to the quiddity's definition.

أَنْ يَكُونَ مُبَايِنًا لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ أَوْ أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ أَوْ أَعَمَّ مِنْهُ أَوْ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ. لَا جَائِزَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ، لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِي الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمَحْمُولَةِ، وَمِنَ الْمُحَالِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ. وَلَا أَخَصَّ، لِوُجُودِ الْأَعَمِّ بِدُونِ الْأَخَصِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ وُجُودُ الْكُلِّ بِدُونِ الْجُزْءِ، وَإِنَّهُ مُحَالٌ. وَلَا أَعَمَّ، لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ لَوْ كَانَ أَعَمَّ مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ لَكَانَ مَوْجُودًا فِي نَوْعٍ آخَرَ بِدُونِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ تَحْقِيقًا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُومِ، فَيَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَذَلِكَ النَّوْعِ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ. وَأُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّا نُقَرِّرُ الْكَلَامَ هَكَذَا: جُزْءُ الْمَاهِيَّةِ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مَا مِنَ الْأَنْوَاعِ الْمُبَايِنَةِ لَهَا، أَوْ لَا؛ وَالْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْجِنْسُ. وَالثَّانِي إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا أَصْلًا بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ مُبَايِنٍ لَهَا، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ مُمَيِّزًا لَهَا عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمُبَايِنَاتِ. وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ آخَرَ مُبَايِنٍ لَهَا، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا لِأَنَّهُ خِلَافُ الْمُقَدَّرِ، بَلْ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَهَنَاكَ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ هُوَ بَعْضُهُ، وَجُزْؤُهُ، فَهَذَا الْبَعْضُ إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ، أَوْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا، فَالْأَوَّلُ يَكُونَ مُمَيِّزًا لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ الْمُبَايِنَةِ لَهُ، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِجِنْسِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الَّذِي هُوَ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، فَيَكُونَ فَصْلًا لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ. وَالثَّانِي - أَعْنِي مَا يَكُونُ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مَا مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ - لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَذَلِكَ النَّوْعِ الْمُبَايِنِ لِتَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ - وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ جِنْسًا دَاخِلًا فِي الْقِسْمِ الْأَوَّلِ، لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ النَّوْعَ مُبَايِنٌ لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ أَيْضًا - فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا، فَهَاهُنَا تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ ثَانٍ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ هُوَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الْأَوَّلِ، لِأَنَّ هَذَا النَّوْعَ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ مُبَايِنٌ لَهُ، فَلَوْ وُجِدَ فِيهِ لَكَانَ مَحْمُولًا عَلَيْهِ، لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِي الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمَحْمُولَةِ فَلَا يَكُونُ مُبَايِنًا لَهُ، فَانْدَفَعَ بِذَلِكَ كَوْنُ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي بِعَيْنِهِ هُوَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الْأَوَّلِ. لَكِنْ إِذَا قِيلَ: أَنَّ بَعْضَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ - الَّذِي كَلَامُنَا فِيهِ – إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مُبَايِنٍ لَهُ أَوْ لَا؛ فَالثَّانِي يَكُونُ فَصْلًا لِلْجِنْسِ الَّذِي هُوَ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي، وَالْأَوَّلُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ هَذَا النَّوْعِ الَّذِي هُوَ بِإِزَاءِ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ الثَّانِي، وَهُوَ خِلَافُ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَمَا عَرَفْتَ، وَإِمَّا يَكُونَ بَعْضًا مِنْ تَمَامِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، فَهَنَاكَ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكٍ ثَالِثٍ...
It is either disparate from the whole common, or more specific than it, or more general than it, or equal to it. It is not possible that it is disparate from it, because the discussion is about the predicative parts, and it is impossible for something predicated on a thing to be disparate from it. Nor more specific, because the more general exists without the more specific, so the existence of the whole without the part would follow, and that is impossible. Nor more general, because if some part of the whole common between the quiddity and another species were more general than the whole common, it would exist in another species without the whole common, to realize the meaning of generality, so it would be common between the quiddity and that species which is opposite the whole common. And it was answered: We determine the discussion thus: The part of the quiddity is either the whole common between it and some species that is disparate from it, or not; the first is genus. The second is either not common at all between it and another species disparate from it, so it would be a differentia for the quiddity distinguishing it from all disparate things. Or it is common between it and another species disparate from it; then it cannot be the whole common between them because that contradicts the assumption, but it must be a part of the whole common between them, so there is a whole common which is its part and portion. Then this part either is not common between the whole common and a species disparate from it, or it is common. The first would be distinguishing the whole common from all disparate quiddities, so it would be a differentia for the genus of the quiddity which is the whole common, thus it would be a differentia for the quiddity overall. The second – I mean what is common between the whole common and some species disparate from it – cannot be the whole common between the quiddity and that species disparate from the whole common – otherwise it would be a genus included in the first division, because that species is also disparate from the quiddity – so it must be a part of the whole common between them. So here there is a second whole common, and it cannot be identical to the first whole common, because this species which is opposite the whole common is disparate from it; if it existed in it, it would be predicated on it, because the discussion is about predicative parts, so it would not be disparate from it. Thus it is repelled that the second whole common is exactly the first whole common. But if it is said: That some part of the whole common – which we are discussing – either is common between the second whole common and a species disparate from it, or not; the second is a differentia for the genus which is the second whole common; the first either is the whole common between the quiddity and this species which is opposite the second whole common, which is contrary to what is assumed as you know, or it is a part of the whole common, so there is a third whole common...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary on the Shamsiyya is a definitional and dialectical analysis of the parts of a quiddity (māhiyya) in terms of genus and difference. The author begins by refuting four impossible relations that a part of a quiddity might have to the 'whole common' (tamām al-mushtarak) – the common predicate that includes the quiddity and some other species. The part cannot be disparate, because it is predicated and thus must share something with the quiddity. It cannot be more specific, otherwise the whole common would exist without it, which is impossible since the whole includes the part. It cannot be more general, because then it would exist in a different species without the whole common, contradicting its commonality. It cannot be equal, because that would make it the whole common itself, which is not a part. After refuting these, the author presents the positive division in response to an implicit objection. The part of a quiddity is either the whole common (genus) or not. If not, it is either not common at all (difference) or common. If common, it must be a part of another whole common, and this leads to a chain. The key insight is that at some level, a part will be a difference for the genus above it, and thus indirectly a difference for the original quiddity. The passage demonstrates the hierarchical structure of genus and difference, showing that infinite regress is avoided because each step moves to a more specific whole common. The author uses the example of a quiddity and a disparate species to illustrate how parts are shared or not. This is essential for understanding how definitions are constructed from genus and difference.

Key Takeaway
Every part of a quiddity is either the whole common (genus) or a difference, either directly or indirectly through a hierarchy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'zakat'. Identify its genus (e.g., 'obligatory charity') and difference that distinguishes it from other charitable acts. Use the author's method to check if any part of the definition is shared with another disparate species and resolve it.

The Five Universals
advanced
10 min
Proof and Failure of Genus-Differentia Classification
Author presents a proof that quiddity parts are genus or differentia, then admits the proof fails against an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that every part of quiddity is genus or differentia, and identify the point at which it fails against the objection of multiple unrelated genera.

Core Idea
Every part of quiddity must be genus or differentia—but proving it is harder than it looks.

This passage from a Mantiq commentary attempts to rigorously prove that any constituent of a quiddity (juz' al-māhiyya) must be either a genus or a differentia. The author lays out a detailed classification based on whether the part is the entire commonality (tamām al-mushtarak) between the quiddity and another species, or only a partial commonality. However, after a long analysis, the author admits that the proof fails unless a certain premise is established—which it is not. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of that proof and the reason for its abandonment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The author splits parts of quiddity into two: those that are the entire commonality with another species (genus) and those that are not. The latter are further subdivided into unshared (differentia) and shared (but not entirely). Note that the shared-but-not-entire case leads to a regress.
2
Follow the regress
If a part is shared but not entire, it must be part of a higher entire commonality (a higher genus). Then apply the same division recursively to that part: is it shared or not? This creates a chain of higher genera.
3
Locate the objection
An objection suggests that the later genera could be identical to earlier ones, breaking the regress. The author examines whether the second entire commonality could be the same as the first. He argues it cannot because that would contradict the assumption of distinctness between the quiddity and the species.
The objection is implicit in the author's own analysis; he considers and refutes the possibility of overlap.
4
See the unresolved issue
The only way to stop the regress is to assert that a quiddity cannot have two genera that are not part of each other. Since this premise is not established, the proof fails. The author concludes that another argument must be sought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجيب : بأنا نقرر الكلام هكذا : جزء الماهية إما أن يكون تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع ما من الأنواع المباينة لها، أو لا؛ والأول هو الجنس. والثاني إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلاً بينها وبين نوع آخر مباين لها، فيكون فصلاً للماهية مميّزاً لها عن جميع المباينات. وإما أن يكون مشتركاً بينها وبين نوع آخر مباين لها، وحينئذ لا يجوز أن يكون تمام المشترك بينهما لأنه خلاف المقدّر ، بل لابد أن يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك بينهما، فهناك تمام مشترك هو بعضه ،وجزؤه، فهذا البعض إما أن لا يكون مشتركاً بين تمام المشترك وبين نوع مباين له، أو يكون مشتركاً، فالأول يكون مميزاً لتمام المشترك عن جميع الماهيات المباينه له، فيكون فصلاً لجنس الماهية الذي هو تمام المشترك، فيكون فصلاً للماهية في الجملة. والثاني - أعني ما يكون مشتركاً بين ممام المشترك وبين نوع ما مباين له - لا يجوز أن يكون تمام المشترك بين الماهية وذلك النوع المباين لتمام المشترك - وإلا لكان جنساً داخلاً في القسم الأول، لأن ذلك النوع مباين للماهية أيضاً - فلابد أن يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك بينهما، فهاهنا تمام مشترك ثان، و لا يجوز أن يكون هو تمام المشترك الأول، لأن هذا النوع الذي هو بإزاء تمام المشترك مباين له، فلو وجد فيه لكان محمولاً عليه، لأن الكلام في الأجزاء المحمولة فلايكون مبايناً له، فاندفع بذلك كون تمام المشترك الثاني بعينه هو تمام المشترك الأول. لكن إذا قيل : أن بعض تمام المشترك - الذي كلامنا فيه – إما أن يكون مشتركاً بين تمام المشترك الثاني وبين نوع مباين له أو لا ؛ فالثاني يكون فصلاً للجنس الذي هو تمام المشترك الثاني، والأوّل إما أن يكون تمام المشترك بين الماهية وبين هذا النوع الذي هو بإزاء تمام المشترك الثاني، وهو خلاف المفروض كما عرفت، وإما يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك، فهناك تمام مشترك ثالث تجه[؟] أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا الثالث بعينه هو الأول، بأن يكون بإزاء تمام المشترك لوجوده فيهما ، فإما أن يكون تمام المشترك بينهما - وهو محال لأن المقدّر أن الجزء ليس تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع مـا مـن الأنواع - وإما أن لا يكون تمام المشترك - بل بعضاً منه – فيكون للماهية تماما مشترك : أحدهما تمام المشترك بين الماهية وبين النوع الذي بإزائها، والثاني تمام المشترك بينها وبين النوع الثاني الذي هو بإزاء تمام المشترك الماهية نوعان متباينان ومباينان للماهية أيضاً يشاركها كل منهما في تمام المشترك بين الماهية وذلك النوع، ولا يوجد ذلك - أي تمام المشترك المذكور – في النوع الآخر، ويكون الجزء الذي هو بعض تمام المشترك موجوداً في كل من النوعين وأعم من كل واحد من تمام المشترك فلايكون فصل جنس (ن : فصلا لجنس) ؟ وهذا الاعتراض تما لا مدفع له إلا إذا ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون لماهية واحدة جنسان لايكون أحدهما جزء للآخر، ولم يثبت هاهنا، فلابد من ترك هذا الدليل والتمسك بدليل آخر.
It was answered: We present the discussion as follows: The part of quiddity either is the entire commonality between it and some species distinct from it, or not; the first is the genus. The second either is not shared at all between it and another species distinct from it, so it is a differentia for the quiddity distinguishing it from all distinct things. Or it is shared between it and another species distinct from it, and then it is not permissible to be the entire commonality between them because that contradicts what is assumed, but rather it must be a part of the entire commonality between them. So there is an entire commonality that is its part and part of it. This part either is not shared between the entire commonality and a distinct species, or is shared. The first distinguishes the entire commonality from all distinct quiddities, so it is a differentia for the genus of the quiddity which is the entire commonality, thus it is a differentia for the quiddity in some respect. The second—meaning what is shared between the entire commonality and some species distinct from it—is not permissible to be the entire commonality between the quiddity and that species distinct from the entire commonality—otherwise it would be a genus included in the first division, because that species is also distinct from the quiddity—so it must be a part of the entire commonality between them. So here there is a second entire commonality, and it is not permissible for it to be the first entire commonality, because this species which corresponds to the entire commonality is distinct from it; if it were found in it, it would be predicated on it, because we are dealing with predicated parts, so it would not be distinct from it. Thus it is refuted that the second entire commonality is identical to the first. But if it is said: That part of the entire commonality—which we are discussing—either is shared between the second entire commonality and a distinct species or not; the second is a differentia for the genus which is the second entire commonality, and the first either is the entire commonality between the quiddity and this species which corresponds to the second entire commonality—which contradicts what is assumed as you know—or is a part of the entire commonality, so there is a third entire commonality. Then it can be said: Why is it not permissible for this third to be identical to the first, by corresponding to the entire commonality due to its existence in both? So either it is the entire commonality between them—which is absurd because the assumption is that the part is not the entire commonality between the quiddity and some species—or it is not the entire commonality, but a part of it. Then the quiddity has two entire commonalities: one entire commonality between the quiddity and the species corresponding to it, and the second entire commonality between it and the second species which corresponds to the entire commonality. The quiddity has two distinct species also distinct from the quiddity, each sharing with it in the entire commonality between the quiddity and that species, and that mentioned entire commonality is not found in the other species. And the part which is a part of the entire commonality exists in each of the two species and is more general than each of the entire commonalities, so it is not a differentia for the genus. This objection has no way to be repelled unless it is established that it is not permissible for a single quiddity to have two genera where one is not part of the other, and this is not established here. Therefore we must abandon this argument and rely on another argument.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement within a commentary on the Shamsiyya. The author responds to an objection by presenting a detailed proof that every part of quiddity (جزء الماهية) must be either a genus (جنس) or a differentia (فصل). He begins by dividing the parts: if a part is the entire commonality (تمام المشترك) between the quiddity and another species, it is a genus; if not, it either is completely unshared (making it a differentia) or is shared but not as the entire commonality. In the latter case, a regress arises because the shared part must be a part of a higher genus, leading to an infinite chain of intermediate genera. An objection is raised: could the later genera be the same as earlier ones? The author analyses the possibilities and shows that the objection can only be refuted if one proves that a single quiddity cannot have two genera that are not part of each other—a premise not established. Hence the author abandons this line of reasoning and moves to a simpler proof.

تمام المشترك

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only way to rescue the proof from the regress objection?
A) Assume each quiddity has only one genus that is not part of another genus.
B) Assume all parts of quiddity are unshared.
C) Assume no two species share any commonality at all.
D) Assume the differentia is always the entire commonality.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to prove that every part of quiddity is genus or differentia fails because it cannot rule out multiple unrelated genera without a further premise.

Refining the Differentia: The Condition of Entire Commonality
Author provides a streamlined proof that every part of quiddity is either genus or differentia, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the author's refined condition to determine whether a part of quiddity qualifies as a genuine differentia (fasl) rather than a mere distinguishing trait.

Core Idea
Not every distinguishing part is a differentia; it must be the entire commonalty with another species.

The author presents a tidy proof that every part of quiddity is either a genus or a differentia. But an objector spots a problem: the proof seems to make every part a differentia. The author's response tightens the definition of differentia, introducing a crucial condition. Understanding this refinement is essential for correctly analyzing definitions in the five universals framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial disjunction
The author splits the case where a part of quiddity is not the entire commonality with a distinct species. There are two possibilities: either the part is not shared at all with any distinct species, or it is shared but not as the entire commonality.
Focus on the key phrase 'تمام المشترك' (entire commonality).
2
Follow the first branch: unshared part—differentia
If the part is not shared with any distinct species, it distinguishes the quiddity from all other distinct things. This is a clear case of a differentia.
3
Follow the second branch: shared but not entire—also a differentia?
If the part is shared with some species but not as the entire commonality, the author argues it still distinguishes the quiddity from those species that lack it. Because some quiddities are simple (بسيطة) and have no parts, this part cannot be common to everything else, so it acts as a differentia relative to those lacking it.
The author's use of simple quiddities (e.g., prime matter, intellect) is key to the reasoning.
4
Identify the objection
The objector says: 'On this basis, all parts of quiddity would be differentiae, because no part is shared by everything else.' This is a reductio ad absurdum of the proof.
5
Analyze the author's response
The author replies that mere distinguishing is insufficient. For a part to be a genuine differentia, it must be the entire commonality (تمام المشترك) between the quiddity and another species. This condition raises the bar: a part that is only a partial commonality or a unique trait does not qualify.
The response refines the definition of differentia, ensuring that the part in question serves as the complete shared basis between two species under a higher genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أن يقال : جزء الماهية إذا لم يكن تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع ما من الأنواع المباينة لها : فإما أن لا يكون مشتركاً بينها وبين نوع مباين لها كان مميزاً لها عن جميع المباينات، وإما أن يكون مشتركاً بينها وبين غيرها، لكن لا يكون تمام المشترك بينهما ؛ فهذا الجزء لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين جميع ما عداها - إذ من جملة الماهيات ماهيّة بسيطة لاجزء لها - فيكون هذا الجزء مميزاً للماهية عن الماهيات التي لاتشاركها في هذا الجزء، فيكون فصلاً للماهية. فإن قلت : فعلى هذا ينحصر أجزاء الماهية في الفصل وحده، لأن جزء الماهية لا يجوز أن يكون جزء الجميع ما عداها كما ذكرتم، فيكون مميزاً للماهية عما لا يشاركها فيه، فيكون فصلا لها. قلت : لا يكفي في كون الجزء فصلاً للماهية مجرد تميزه لها في الجملة، بل لابد أن يكون تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر.
And it is to be said: The part of quiddity, if it is not the entire commonality between it and some species distinct from it: either it is not shared between it and any distinct species, so it distinguishes it from all distinct things; or it is shared between it and others, but it is not the entire commonality between them. Then this part cannot be common between the quiddity and everything else — since among quiddities there are simple quiddities with no parts — so this part distinguishes the quiddity from those quiddities that do not share this part, thus it is a differentia for the quiddity. If you say: On this basis, the parts of quiddity are limited to the differentia alone, because a part of quiddity cannot be a part of everything else as you mentioned, so it distinguishes the quiddity from what does not share it, so it is a differentia. I say: It is not sufficient for a part to be a differentia for the quiddity merely that it distinguishes it in some respect; rather it must be the entire commonality between it and another species.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a simpler proof that every part of quiddity (جزء الماهية) is either a genus or a differentia. The author divides the case where a part is not the entire commonality (تمام المشترك) with any species. If it is not shared at all with a distinct species, it acts as a differentia distinguishing the quiddity from all others. If it is shared but not as the entire commonality, the part cannot be common to everything else because some quiddities are simple (بسيطة) with no parts; thus it still distinguishes the quiddity from those that lack this part, making it a differentia in a relative sense. An objector argues that this would make every part a differentia, since no part is common to all other quiddities. The author responds that a differentia must not merely distinguish in some respect but must be the entire commonality (تمام المشترك) between the quiddity and another species. In other words, for a part to be a differentia, it must be the complete set of predicates shared with a contrasting species, not just any distinguishing feature. This refines the definition of differentia.

تمام المشترك
فصل
ماهية بسيطة

A genuine differentia is not merely any distinguishing part of quiddity; it must be the entire commonalty between the quiddity and a contrasting species.
