Conditions for a Part to Be a Differentia and Refuting Infinite Regress
Author presents an alternative proof and critiques infinite regress claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's alternative proof for when a part of a quiddity becomes a differentia and evaluate his critique of the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
A part of a quiddity is a differentia only if it is the complete common with another species.

In this passage, the author refines the conditions under which a part of a quiddity qualifies as a differentia (faṣl). He dismisses a previous proof as ungrounded and presents an alternative argument. He also corrects a misconception about infinite regress, clarifying that his proof does not entail an infinite chain of quiddity parts. By following his reasoning, you will learn how to identify a genuine differentia and avoid a common fallacy in definition theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the discarded proof and its flaw
Find the initial objection the author rejects: the assumption that one quiddity cannot have two genera without one being part of the other. Note why the author says this assumption is unproven and thus the proof must be abandoned.
The author says 'this objection has no repulsion' unless that assumption is established, which it is not.
2
Extract the alternative proof's two cases
The author presents two cases for a part that is not the complete common (tamām al-mushtarak) between the quiddity and a separate species. First, if it is not shared at all, it distinguishes the quiddity. Second, if it is shared but not as the complete common, it still distinguishes from simple quiddities that lack that part.
The proof relies on the existence of simple quiddities with no parts.
3
Examine the objection about parts being solely differentiae
A potential objector claims that this reasoning would make every part of a quiddity a differentia, since no part can be shared with everything else. Understand the author's response: mere distinguishing is insufficient; the part must also be the complete common between the quiddity and another species.
The author emphasizes the condition of 'complete common' (tamām al-mushtarak).
4
Trace the infinite regress argument and its critique
Follow the scenario where a part is a portion of the complete common but more general than it, leading to a potential infinite regress of complete commons. Note the author's critique: true infinite regress (tasalsul) would require that each complete common is a part of the previous one, which is not necessary here. The proof does not force an infinite chain of quiddity parts.
The author says the statement 'it does not regress infinitely' is incorrect as presented.
5
Synthesize the two conditions for a differentia
The author concludes that a part is a differentia in two cases: (1) it is not shared at all with any other species, so it is specific to the quiddity; (2) it is equal to the complete common, in which case it becomes a differentia of the genus (the complete common) and thus a differentia of the quiddity.
The passage ends mid-explanation but the logical closure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاعتراض تما لا مدفع له إلا إذا ثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون لماهية واحدة جنسان لايكون أحدهما جزء للآخر، ولم يثبت هاهنا، فلابد من ترك هذا الدليل والتمسك بدليل آخر.
وهو أن يقال : جزء الماهية إذا لم يكن تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع ما من الأنواع المباينة لها : فإما أن لا يكون مشتركاً بينها وبين نوع مباين لها كان مميزاً لها عن جميع المباينات، وإما أن يكون مشتركاً بينها وبين غيرها، لكن لا يكون تمام المشترك بينهما ؛ فهذا الجزء لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وبين جميع ما عداها - إذ من جملة الماهيات ماهيّة بسيطة لاجزء لها - فيكون هذا الجزء مميزاً للماهية عن الماهيات التي لاتشاركها في هذا الجزء، فيكون فصلاً للماهية. فإن قلت : فعلى هذا ينحصر أجزاء الماهية في الفصل وحده، لأن جزء الماهية لا يجوز أن يكون جزء الجميع ما عداها كما ذكرتم، فيكون مميزاً للماهية عما لا يشاركها فيه، فيكون فصلا لها. قلت : لا يكفي في كون الجزء فصلاً للماهية مجرد تميزه لها في الجملة، بل لابد أن يكون تمام المشترك بينها وبين نوع آخر (شریف).

الأول، وحينئذ لو كان بعض تمام المشترك بين الماهية والنوع الثاني أعم منه لكان موجوداً في نوع آخر بدون تمام المشترك الثاني، فيكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وذلك النوع الثالث الذي هو بإزاء تمام المشترك الثاني - وليس تمام المشترك بينهما بل بعضه - فيحصل تمام مشترك ثالث، وهلم جراً، فإما أن يوجد تمام المشتركات إلى غير النهاية أو ينتهي إلى بعض تمام مشترك مساو له ؛ والأول محال ، وإلا لتركبت الماهية من أجزاء غير متناهية.
فقوله : «ولا يتسلسل» ليس على ما ينبغي، لأن التسلسل هو ترتب أمور غير متناهية، ولم يلزم من الدليل ترتب أجزاء الماهية، وإنما يلزم أن لو كان تمام المشترك الثاني جزء من تمام المشترك الأول، وهو غير لازم - ولعله أراد بالتسلسل وجود أمور غير متناهية في الماهية، لكنه خلاف المتعارف.
وإذا بطلت الأقسام الثلاثة تعين أن يكون بعض تمام المشترك مساوياً له وهو الأمر الثاني.
وأما أن الجزء فصل على تقدير كل واحد من الأمرين فلأنه إن لم يكن مشتركاً أصلاً يكون مختصاً بها، فيكون مميزاً للماهية عن غيرها، وإن كان بعض تمام المشترك مساوياً له يكون فصلا لتمام المشترك لاختصاصه به وتمام المشترك ،جنس فيكون فصل ،جنس فيكون فصلاً للماهية، لأنه
And this objection has no repulsion at all unless it is established that it is not permissible for one quiddity to have two genera such that one is not a part of the other, and that is not established here, so we must abandon this proof and adhere to another proof. And it is that it is said: A part of the quiddity, when it is not the complete common between it and some species from among the species that are separate from it: then either it is not common between it and any species separate from it, then it would be distinguishing for it from all what separates it; or it is common between it and others, but not the complete common between them; so this part cannot be common between the quiddity and all that is other than it – since among quiddities there is a simple quiddity that has no part – so this part becomes distinguishing for the quiddity from the quiddities that do not share this part with it, so it becomes a differentia for the quiddity. If you say: Thus the parts of the quiddity are confined to the differentia alone, because a part of the quiddity cannot be a part of everything other than it as you mentioned, so it would be distinguishing for the quiddity from what does not share it, so it would be a differentia for it. I say: Mere distinguishing for it in general is not sufficient for a part to be a differentia, rather it must be the complete common between it and another species (al-Sharif). First, and then if some part of the complete common between the quiddity and the second species is more general than it, it would exist in another species without the second complete common, so it would be common between the quiddity and that third species which is opposite to the second complete common – and it is not the complete common between them but some of it – so a third complete common obtains, and so on ad infinitum, so either the complete commons extend to infinity or it ends at some part of the complete common that is equal to it; the first is impossible, otherwise the quiddity would be composed of infinite parts. So his statement: 'And it does not regress infinitely [tasalṣul]' is not as it should be, because tasalṣul is the arrangement of infinite things, and the proof does not necessitate the arrangement of the parts of the quiddity; rather it would necessitate if the second complete common were a part of the first complete common, which is not necessary – perhaps he meant by tasalṣul the existence of infinite things in the quiddity, but that is contrary to convention. And when the three divisions are invalid, it is determined that some part of the complete common is equal to it, which is the second matter. And as for the part being a differentia on the assumption of each of the two matters: because if it is not common at all, it is specific to it, so it is distinguishing for the quiddity from others; and if it is some part of the complete common equal to it, it becomes a differentia of the complete common due to its specification to it, and the complete common is a genus, so it becomes a differentia of the genus, and thus it becomes a differentia for the quiddity, because...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a prior argument concerning the conditions for a part of a quiddity (māhiyya) to be a differentia (faṣl). The author first dismisses an earlier objection that assumed a quiddity cannot have two genera without one being part of the other—since this assumption is unproven, the previous proof must be abandoned. He then provides an alternative proof: if a part of a quiddity is not the complete common (tamām al-mushtarak) between that quiddity and a separate species, then either it is not shared at all (hence distinguishing, making it a differentia) or it is shared but not as the complete common. In the latter case, it cannot be common to all other things (since some quiddities are simple and have no parts), so it distinguishes the quiddity from those that do not share it, again making it a differentia. An objection is raised: wouldn't this make every part of a quiddity a differentia, since it cannot be a part of everything else? The author responds that mere distinguishing is insufficient; the part must also be the complete common between the quiddity and another species. Then he addresses the scenario where the part is a portion of the complete common but more general than it—this leads to an infinite regress of complete commons unless it ends at a part equal to the complete common. He criticizes a statement that 'it does not regress infinitely' (lā yatasalsal), clarifying that true infinite regress (tasalsul) involves an infinite chain of things, but here the supposed regress would only occur if the second complete common were part of the first, which is not necessary. He concludes that the only remaining possibility is that the part is equal to the complete common (the second matter). Finally, he shows that on either assumption (part not shared at all, or part equal to the complete common), the part becomes a differentia: either because it is specific to the quiddity, or because it is a differentia of the genus (the complete common), which makes it a differentia of the quiddity. The passage ends mid-sentence, continuing the explanation.

Key Terms
تمام المشترك (tamām al-mushtarak): The complete commonality or shared attribute between two things; the entire set of characteristics that two quiddities share, as opposed to a part of that commonality. [Note: In earlier logic texts, 'mushtarak' often refers to a common term (homonym), but here it is used technically for the shared essential content between species and genus.]
فصل (faṣl): Differentia; a part of the quiddity that distinguishes it from other species under the same genus, and which is not shared with those species. [Note: Stable across the tradition; the term consistently means the differentia in the Porphyrian tree.]
جزء (juz’): A part of the quiddity; an essential component such as genus or differentia. [Note: No significant shift; always means a constituent element of the definition.]
ماهية (māhiyya): Quiddity; the essence or whatness of a thing, comprising its genus and differentia. [Note: In falsafah, synonymous with dhāt (essence); in later kalām, sometimes contrasted with haqīqa.]
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress; an endless chain of causes or parts. The author criticizes its use here, arguing that the proof does not actually entail a regress of quiddity parts. [Note: In kalām, tasalsul is a famous argument against infinite causal chains; here it is used more technically in logic.]

B) It must be the complete common between the quiddity and another species.
C) It must be a part shared with all other quiddities.
D) It must be a part of the genus.

Key Takeaway
A part of a quiddity becomes a differentia only if it is either not shared at all with other species, or it is equal to the complete common between the quiddity and another species; the alleged infinite regress does not invalidate this proof.

Parts of the Complete Common as Differentia
Author explains how a part of the complete common can be a differentia and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given part of a quiddity qualifies as a differentia by distinguishing simple parts from composite parts and applying the condition of being a distinguishing part of the complete common.

Core Idea
A simple part of the complete common can be a differentia even without a genus.

How can a part of what is shared become a distinguishing mark? This passage from al-Qutb al-Tahtani explains that a simple part of the complete common (e.g., a genus) can serve as a differentia, and clarifies that composite parts like 'rational animal' are not at issue. The argument uses a careful analysis of sharing and specificity, and addresses a potential objection. We will dissect this logical move step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the author's thesis: that a part of the complete common can be a differentia. Note how he defines differentia broadly as 'that which distinguishes the quiddity in a general sense'.
2
Trace the proof structure
The author gives two versions of the proof. In the first, he shows that if a part is not the complete common, it distinguishes the quiddity from some others. In the second (abbreviated), he considers whether the part is shared with another species or not.
Focus on the second version: if the part is not shared, it is specific to the common; if shared, it becomes a part of a higher common.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objector says: 'rational animal' and 'sensitive animal' are parts of man but are not genus or differentia. The author replies that the discussion is about simple parts, not composite ones like 'rational animal'.
Understand why 'rational animal' is composite (it combines genus and differentia) and thus not a simple part.
4
Distinguish simple vs composite parts
Identify the key distinction: simple parts (juz' mufrad) are indivisible constituents of the quiddity, while composite parts (like 'rational animal') are composed of simpler elements. The rule about parts of tamam al-mushtarak applies only to simple parts.
This distinction protects against the false dichotomy that all parts must be primitive genera or differentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما ميز الجنس عن أغياره - وجميع أغيار الجنس بعض أغيار الماهية - فيكون مميزاً للماهية عن بعض أغيارها، ولا نعني بالفصل إلا مميز الماهية في الجملة، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله : وكيف ما كان» أي سواء لم يكن الجزء مشتركاً أصلاً أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له، فهو مميز للماهية عن مشاركيها في جنس لها أو وجود، فيكون فصلاً. وإنما قال «في جنس أو وجود» لأن اللازم من الدليل ليس إلا أنّ الجزء إذا لم يكن تمام المشترك يكون مميزاً لها في الجملة، وهو الفصل. وأما أنه يكون مميزاً عن المشاركات الجنسية حتى إذا كان للماهية فصل وجب أن يكون لها جنس : فلايلزم من الدليل. فالماهية إن كان لها جنس كان فصلها مميّزاً لها عن المشاركات الجنسية، وإن لم يكن لها فلا أقل من أن يكون لها مشاركات في الوجود والشيئية، وحينئذ يكون فصلها مميزاً لها عنها. ويمكن اختصار الدليل بحذف النسب الأربع، بأن يقال بعض تمام المشترك إن لم يكن مشتركاً بين تمام المشترك و بين نوع آخر يكون مختصاً بتمام المشترك، فيكون فصلاً له، فيكون فصلاً للماهية، وإن كان مشتركاً بينهما يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وذلك النوع، فلم يكن تمام المشترك بينهما، فيكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك بين الماهية والنوع الثاني وهكذا. لا يقال: حصر جزء الماهية في الجنس والفصل باطل لأن الجوهر الناطق والجوهر الحسّاس - مثلاً - جزء لماهية الإنسان، مع أنه ليس بجنس ولا فصل. لانا نقول : الكلام في الأجزاء المفردة لا في مطلق الأجزاء، وهذا ما وعدناه في صدر البحث.
When the genus is distinguished from its others—and all the others of the genus are some of the others of the quiddity—then it distinguishes the quiddity from some of its others. By 'differentia' we mean only that which distinguishes the quiddity in a general sense. This is what he referred to by his saying 'however it may be', i.e., whether the part is not shared at all or it is a part of the complete common that is equal to it, it distinguishes the quiddity from its partners in a genus it has or in existence, so it is a differentia. He said 'in a genus or existence' because what follows from the proof is only that if the part is not the complete common, it distinguishes it in a general sense, and that is the differentia. As for it distinguishing from generic partners such that if the quiddity has a differentia it must have a genus—that does not follow from the proof. So if the quiddity has a genus, its differentia distinguishes it from generic partners; if it does not, then at least it has partners in existence and thingness, and then its differentia distinguishes it from them. The proof can be abbreviated by removing the four relations, by saying: a part of the complete common, if it is not shared between the complete common and another species, is specific to the complete common, so it is a differentia for it, thus a differentia for the quiddity. If it is shared between them, then it is shared between the quiddity and that species, so it is not the complete common between them, but a part of the complete common between the quiddity and the second species, and so on. Do not say: confining the part of the quiddity to genus and differentia is false because 'rational animal' and 'sensitive animal'—for example—are parts of the quiddity of man, yet they are neither genus nor differentia. For we say: the discussion is about simple parts, not about parts in general, and this is what we promised at the beginning of the investigation.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of differentia (fasl). The author explains that a part of the quiddity (mahiyya) that serves to distinguish it from other things is a differentia, even if that part is not a genus. The key point is that a part of the complete common (tamam al-mushtarak) can be a differentia. If the part is not shared with another species, it is a differentia for the complete common, and hence for the quiddity. If it is shared, it reduces to a part of another common. The author also addresses an objection: how can 'rational' and 'sensitive' be parts of man but not genus or differentia? The answer: the discussion concerns simple parts, not composite ones like 'rational animal' (jawhar natiq). This clarification protects against misunderstanding that all parts of a quiddity must be primitive genera or differentiae.

Key Terms
فصل
تمام المشترك
جزء مفرد
الجوهر الناطق
الجوهر الحسّاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He concedes that the definition of differentia is incomplete.
B) He says the discussion is about simple parts, not all parts.
C) He argues that 'rational animal' is actually a genus.
D) He says that 'rational animal' is a differentia in a broader sense.

Key Takeaway
A differentia can be a simple part of the complete common; composite parts like 'rational animal' are not considered because they are not simple.

Rasm of Differentia: Any Universal That Distinguishes
Author presents the definition of differentia via rasm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of differentia (fasl) as a rasm and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A differentia is anything that distinguishes a quiddity in answer to 'what is it in its substance?' — even if accidental.

This passage presents the definition of differentia (fasl) through rasm — a non-essential definition. Unlike the hadd definition that restricts to essential differentia, the rasm definition allows any universal that distinguishes the quiddity when asked 'what is it in its substance?' This broader conception is important in Islamic logic and theology, where multiple features can be used to differentiate entities.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of fasl
Find the sentence in the Arabic where the author states the rasm definition. It begins with 'رسموه بأنه كلّي يُحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره؟»'.
This is the core claim.
2
Understand the question 'what is it in its substance?'
The question seeks what distinguishes the thing in a general sense. It does not demand the nearest differentia or essential property.
The Arabic phrase is 'أي شيء هو في جوهره'.
3
Analyze the examples: 'rational' and 'sensitive' for man
The author gives 'rational' and 'sensitive' as valid answers for man. Both are universals that distinguish man from other animals, even though 'sensitive' is shared with animals.
This shows that multiple differentiae can apply.
4
Note the footnote's expansion
The footnote clarifies that any differentia—near or far, essential or accidental—is acceptable as long as it distinguishes the thing in some way.
This prevents the misconception that only the nearest differentia is valid.
5
Compare with hadd definition
A hadd definition would require the essential differentia that constitutes the quiddity. This rasm definition is broader and pragmatic for identification purposes.
Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing definitions in Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقال : ورسموه بأنه كلّي يُحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره؟»، فعلى هذا لو تركبت حقيقة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كان كلّ منها فصلاً لها، لأنه يميزها عن مشاركيها في الوجود. أقول : رسموا الفصل بأنه كلّي يحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره كالناطق والحساس، فإنّه إذا سئل عن «الإنسان» أو عن زيد بـ«أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ فالجواب أنه ناطق أو حساس، لأن السؤال بـ «أي شيء هو ؟» إنما يُطلب به ما يميز الشيء في الجملة، فكل (١) قد يناقش حينئذ في أنه كيف يعد «الجسم النامي» من الأجزاء المفردة مع كونه مركباً ؟ (٢) إذا سئل عن الإنسان بـ «أي شيء هو ؟ كان المطلوب ما يميزه في الجملة، سواء ميزه عن جميع ما عداه أو عن بعضه، وسواء ميزه تمييزاً ذاتياً أو عرَضيّاً، فيصح أن يجاب بأي فصل أريد - قريباً كان أو بعيداً، كالناطق والحسّاس والنامي
He said: And they defined it by saying: a universal that is predicated of a thing in answer to 'what is it in its substance?'. According to this, if a reality is composed of two equal things or equal matters, each of them is a differentia for it, because it distinguishes it from its partners in existence. I say: They defined the differentia as a universal that is predicated of a thing in answer to 'what is it in its substance?', like 'rational' and 'sensitive'. For if one asks about 'man' or about Zayd, 'what is it in its substance?', the answer is that he is rational or sensitive. Because the question 'what is it?' only seeks what distinguishes the thing in general. So every [part]... (Footnote 2: If one asks about man 'what is it?', what is sought is what distinguishes him in general, whether it distinguishes him from all others or from some of them, and whether it distinguishes him essentially or accidentally, so it is permissible to answer with any differentia one intends—near or far—like 'rational', 'sensitive', and 'growing'.)
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage gives the definition (rasm) of differentia (fasl) as a universal that is predicated in answer to the question 'what is it in its substance?' (ayyu shay'in huwa fi jawharihi). For example, if you ask about a human 'what is he in his substance?', the answer can be 'rational' or 'sensitive' because both distinguish the human in a general sense. The author explains that when a quiddity is composed of multiple equal properties, each can be a differentia since they all distinguish the quiddity. The footnote clarifies that the answer can be any differentia, near or far, essential or accidental, as long as it provides distinction. This guards against the mistake that only the nearest differentia (fasl qarib) is acceptable; rather, any distinguishing predicate may serve.

Key Takeaway
The rasm of differentia allows any universal that distinguishes the quiddity in answer to the substance question, even non-essential features, making multiple differentiae possible.

Defining the Differentia (Fasl) – Core Definition and Key Terms
Author defines fasl and analyzes its logical definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differentia in a definition and distinguish it from genus, species, property, and common accident.

Core Idea
The differentia answers 'what thing is it in its essence?' to distinguish a species from its co-species.

In this lesson, we dissect the definition of the differentia (fasl) as one of the five universals. The passage gives a precise logical formulation: a universal predicated of a thing in answer to the question 'What thing is it in its essence?' (أي شيء هو في جوهره). Understanding this definition is crucial for constructing correct definitions (hadd) in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The definiendum is 'differentia' (fasl). Note that it is one of the five universals, specifically the one that distinguishes a species within a genus.
The passage begins with 'They defined it as...'.
2
Parse the definition's components
The definition has three parts: 'universal' (جنس), 'predicated of a thing in answer to What thing is it' (يخرج النوع والجنس والعرض العام), and 'in its essence' (يخرج الخاصة). These components exclude the other four universals.
Compare with the definition of genus (جنس) which answers 'What is it?' (ما هو).
3
Examine the exclusion of species and genus
Species and genus answer 'What is it?' not 'What thing is it?'. The question 'What thing is it?' seeks a distinguisher, not just the general category.
For example, 'animal' answers 'What is it?' but not 'What thing is it in its essence?'.
4
Examine the exclusion of property and common accident
Property (خاصة) answers 'What thing is it?' but not 'in its essence' – it is accidental. Common accident (عرض عام) does not answer any such question at all.
Laughing (ضاحك) is a property of man, but not differentia because it is not essential.
5
Test the definition with an objection
An objector suggests that if the question only seeks a distinguisher in general, genus would also qualify. The response: genus does not distinguish in essence; the phrase 'in its essence' blocks this.
This shows the definition's precision: distinguishing must be essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ورسموه بأنه كلّي يُحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره؟»، فعلى هذا لو تركتب حقيقة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كان كلّ منها فصلاً لها، لأنه يميزها عن مشاركيها في الوجود. أقول: رسموا الفصل بأنه كلّي يحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره كالناطق والحساس، فإنّه إذا سئل عن «الإنسان» أو عن زيد بـ«أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ فالجواب أنه ناطق أو حساس، لأن السؤال بـ «أي شيء هو ؟» إنما يُطلب به ما يميز الشيء في الجملة، فكل ما يميزه يصلح للجواب ؛ ثم إن طلب المميز الجوهري يكون الجواب بالفصل، وإن طلب المميّز العرضي يكون الجواب بالخاصة. فـ«الكلي» جنس يشمل سائر الكليات، ويقولنا «يحمل على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو يخرج النوع والجنس والعرض العام، لأن النوع والجنس يقالان في جواب «ماهو ؟ لا في جواب «أي شيء هو ؟» والعرض العام لا يقال في الجواب أصلاً. ويقولنا في جوهره يخرج الخاصة لأنها وإن كانت مميزة للشيء، لكن لا في جوهره وذاته، بل في عرضه. فإن قلت: السائل بـ«أي شيء هو ؟» إن طلب مميز الشيء عن جميع الأغيار، لا يكون مثل «الحساس» فصلاً للإنسان - لأنه لا يميزه عن جميع - وإن طلب المميز في الجملة - سواء كان عن جميع الأغيار أو عن بعضها - فالجنس مميز للشيء عن بعضها، فيجب أن يكون صالحاً للجواب، فلا يخرج عن الحد. وقابل الأبعاد - وأن يجاب بالخاصة أيضاً. وإذا قيل: «أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ » لم يصح الجواب بالخاصة، وصح بالفصول المذكورة كلها، وكذا إذا قيل: «أي جوهر هو في ذاته ؟ » صح الجواب بجميع تلك الفصول. وأما إذا قيل: «أي جسم هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب إلا بما عدا «القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة»، وإذا قيل: أي جسم نام هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب بـ «القابل للأبعاد» و «النامي» أيضاً، وإذا قيل: «أي حيوان هو في ذاته ؟» تعين «الناطق» للجواب (شريف).
He said: They defined it (the differentia) as a universal predicated of a thing in answer to 'What thing is it in its essence?'. Accordingly, if a reality is composed of two equal matters or equal matters, each of them would be a differentia for it, because it distinguishes it from its participants in existence. I say: They defined the differentia as a universal predicated of a thing in answer to 'What thing is it in its essence?' like 'rational' and 'sensitive', for if one asks about 'human' or Zayd 'What thing is it in its essence?' the answer is that it is rational or sensitive, because the question 'What thing is it?' only seeks what distinguishes the thing in general, so whatever distinguishes it is suitable for the answer; then if the essential distinguisher is sought, the answer is by the differentia, and if the accidental distinguisher is sought, the answer is by the property. So 'universal' is a genus encompassing all universals, and our saying 'predicated of a thing in answer to What thing is it' excludes species, genus, and common accident, because species and genus are said in answer to 'What is it?' not in answer to 'What thing is it?', and common accident is not said in the answer at all. And our saying 'in its essence' excludes the property because although it distinguishes the thing, it does not do so in its essence and substance, but in its accident. If you say: The questioner by 'What thing is it?' if he seeks the distinguisher of the thing from all others, then like 'sensitive' would not be a differentia for man – because it does not distinguish him from all – and if he seeks the distinguisher in general – whether from all others or from some – then the genus distinguishes the thing from some, so it must be suitable for the answer, thus it would not be excluded from the definition. And 'possessor of dimensions' – and that the answer could also be by the property. And if one says: 'What thing is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer by the property, but it is correct by all the mentioned differentiae; likewise if one says: 'What substance is it in its essence?' it is correct to answer by all those differentiae. As for if one says: 'What body is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer except by what excludes 'possessor of three dimensions'; and if one says: 'What growing body is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer by 'possessor of dimensions' and 'growing' as well; and if one says: 'What animal is it in its essence?' 'rational' is specified for the answer (Sharif).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl), one of the five universals. The author begins by stating the definition: a differentia is a universal predicated of a thing in answer to the question 'What thing is it in its essence?' (أي شيء هو في جوهره؟). He then explains that if a reality is composed of two or more equal constituents (like human from rational and sensitive?), each can serve as a differentia because it distinguishes the species from others that share the same genus. The commentary clarifies the definition by giving examples: 'rational' (الناطق) and 'sensitive' (الحساس) are differentiae of humans. The question seeks what distinguishes the thing in general, so any distinguisher is acceptable. If the essential distinguisher is sought, the answer is a differentia; if accidental, a property (خاصة). The author then analyzes the definition's parts: 'universal' includes all universals; 'predicated in answer to What thing is it' excludes species and genus (which answer 'What is it?') and common accident (which cannot answer at all); 'in its essence' excludes the property (which is accidental). An objection is raised: If the question seeks distinction from all others, then 'sensitive' fails (since plants are also sensitive), so it wouldn't be a differentia. If it seeks distinction from some, then genus also distinguishes (e.g., 'body' distinguishes human from angels), so genus would also be a valid answer, contradicting its exclusion. The answer relies on the phrase 'in its essence'—the genus does not distinguish in essence but in a more general way. The passage further illustrates with nested questions: if one asks 'What thing is it in its essence?', all differentiae (from broad to specific) are valid. But if the question specifies a higher genus, like 'What body is it?', then the differentia must exclude that genus's own differentiae. For example, when asking 'What animal is it?', only 'rational' (the specific differentia of humans) is the correct answer. This hierarchical precision shows that the differentia's role depends on the context of the question, but the core definition remains that it is predicated essentially.

Key Terms
الفصل
الخاصة
الجنس
النوع
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT excluded by the phrase 'in its essence' in the definition of fasl?
A) Property (خاصة)
B) Species (نوع)
C) Genus (جنس)
D) Common accident (عرض عام)

Key Takeaway
The differentia is an essential universal that answers 'What thing is it in its essence?', distinguishing a species from its co-species within a genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the definition of 'wudu' (ablution) as 'washing specified parts with water with intention', identify the genus and differentia. Which part answers 'What thing is it?' and which answers 'What thing is it in its essence'?
?

Applying the Differentia: Hierarchical Questions and Conditions
Author illustrates hierarchical application of fasl questions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which differentia is appropriate for a given hierarchical question and explain why different questions yield different answers.

Core Idea
The appropriate differentia depends on the level of the genus specified in the question.

This lesson explores the practical application of the differentia through a series of nested questions. The passage shows that when the question specifies a higher genus (e.g., 'What body is it?'), the differentia must be one that distinguishes within that genus. This hierarchical precision is essential for defining things correctly in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nested question scenario
The passage presents a sequence: 'What thing is it in its essence?' → 'What substance is it?' → 'What body is it?' → 'What growing body is it?' → 'What animal is it?'. Each question limits the genus further.
The answers at each level are differentiae relevant to that genus.
2
Observe the pattern of answers
For the broadest question ('What thing is it?'), all differentiae from remote to close are valid. As the genus narrows, the range of valid differentiae shrinks until only the specific differentia (e.g., rational) remains.
For 'What animal is it?', only 'rational' is valid because 'sensitive' is already included in the genus 'animal'.
3
Apply to a real Islamic example
Consider defining 'human' in Kalam: if asked 'What substance is it?', 'rational' and 'sensitive' are valid. But if asked 'What animal is it?', only 'rational' is correct.
This mirrors the passage's examples: when asking 'What body?', 'possessor of dimensions' is excluded.
4
Recognize the condition of the questioner's intent
The passage notes that if the questioner seeks distinction from all others, only the specific differentia works. If seeking in general, all differentiae work. But the definition of fasl fixes the intent to essential distinguishing.
The objection earlier is resolved by the phrase 'in its essence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ورسموه بأنه كلّي يُحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره؟»، فعلى هذا لو تركتب حقيقة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كان كلّ منها فصلاً لها، لأنه يميزها عن مشاركيها في الوجود. أقول: رسموا الفصل بأنه كلّي يحمل على الشيء في جواب «أي شيء هو في جوهره كالناطق والحساس، فإنّه إذا سئل عن «الإنسان» أو عن زيد بـ«أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ فالجواب أنه ناطق أو حساس، لأن السؤال بـ «أي شيء هو ؟» إنما يُطلب به ما يميز الشيء في الجملة، فكل ما يميزه يصلح للجواب ؛ ثم إن طلب المميز الجوهري يكون الجواب بالفصل، وإن طلب المميّز العرضي يكون الجواب بالخاصة. فـ«الكلي» جنس يشمل سائر الكليات، ويقولنا «يحمل على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو يخرج النوع والجنس والعرض العام، لأن النوع والجنس يقالان في جواب «ماهو ؟ لا في جواب «أي شيء هو ؟» والعرض العام لا يقال في الجواب أصلاً. ويقولنا في جوهره يخرج الخاصة لأنها وإن كانت مميزة للشيء، لكن لا في جوهره وذاته، بل في عرضه. فإن قلت: السائل بـ«أي شيء هو ؟» إن طلب مميز الشيء عن جميع الأغيار، لا يكون مثل «الحساس» فصلاً للإنسان - لأنه لا يميزه عن جميع - وإن طلب المميز في الجملة - سواء كان عن جميع الأغيار أو عن بعضها - فالجنس مميز للشيء عن بعضها، فيجب أن يكون صالحاً للجواب، فلا يخرج عن الحد. وقابل الأبعاد - وأن يجاب بالخاصة أيضاً. وإذا قيل: «أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ » لم يصح الجواب بالخاصة، وصح بالفصول المذكورة كلها، وكذا إذا قيل: «أي جوهر هو في ذاته ؟ » صح الجواب بجميع تلك الفصول. وأما إذا قيل: «أي جسم هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب إلا بما عدا «القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة»، وإذا قيل: أي جسم نام هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب بـ «القابل للأبعاد» و «النامي» أيضاً، وإذا قيل: «أي حيوان هو في ذاته ؟» تعين «الناطق» للجواب (شريف).
He said: They defined it (the differentia) as a universal predicated of a thing in answer to 'What thing is it in its essence?'. Accordingly, if a reality is composed of two equal matters or equal matters, each of them would be a differentia for it, because it distinguishes it from its participants in existence. I say: They defined the differentia as a universal predicated of a thing in answer to 'What thing is it in its essence?' like 'rational' and 'sensitive', for if one asks about 'human' or Zayd 'What thing is it in its essence?' the answer is that it is rational or sensitive, because the question 'What thing is it?' only seeks what distinguishes the thing in general, so whatever distinguishes it is suitable for the answer; then if the essential distinguisher is sought, the answer is by the differentia, and if the accidental distinguisher is sought, the answer is by the property. So 'universal' is a genus encompassing all universals, and our saying 'predicated of a thing in answer to What thing is it' excludes species, genus, and common accident, because species and genus are said in answer to 'What is it?' not in answer to 'What thing is it?', and common accident is not said in the answer at all. And our saying 'in its essence' excludes the property because although it distinguishes the thing, it does not do so in its essence and substance, but in its accident. If you say: The questioner by 'What thing is it?' if he seeks the distinguisher of the thing from all others, then like 'sensitive' would not be a differentia for man – because it does not distinguish him from all – and if he seeks the distinguisher in general – whether from all others or from some – then the genus distinguishes the thing from some, so it must be suitable for the answer, thus it would not be excluded from the definition. And 'possessor of dimensions' – and that the answer could also be by the property. And if one says: 'What thing is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer by the property, but it is correct by all the mentioned differentiae; likewise if one says: 'What substance is it in its essence?' it is correct to answer by all those differentiae. As for if one says: 'What body is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer except by what excludes 'possessor of three dimensions'; and if one says: 'What growing body is it in its essence?' it is not correct to answer by 'possessor of dimensions' and 'growing' as well; and if one says: 'What animal is it in its essence?' 'rational' is specified for the answer (Sharif).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl), one of the five universals. The author begins by stating the definition: a differentia is a universal predicated of a thing in answer to the question 'What thing is it in its essence?' (أي شيء هو في جوهره؟). He then explains that if a reality is composed of two or more equal constituents (like human from rational and sensitive?), each can serve as a differentia because it distinguishes the species from others that share the same genus. The commentary clarifies the definition by giving examples: 'rational' (الناطق) and 'sensitive' (الحساس) are differentiae of humans. The question seeks what distinguishes the thing in general, so any distinguisher is acceptable. If the essential distinguisher is sought, the answer is a differentia; if accidental, a property (خاصة). The author then analyzes the definition's parts: 'universal' includes all universals; 'predicated in answer to What thing is it' excludes species and genus (which answer 'What is it?') and common accident (which cannot answer at all); 'in its essence' excludes the property (which is accidental). An objection is raised: If the question seeks distinction from all others, then 'sensitive' fails (since plants are also sensitive), so it wouldn't be a differentia. If it seeks distinction from some, then genus also distinguishes (e.g., 'body' distinguishes human from angels), so genus would also be a valid answer, contradicting its exclusion. The answer relies on the phrase 'in its essence'—the genus does not distinguish in essence but in a more general way. The passage further illustrates with nested questions: if one asks 'What thing is it in its essence?', all differentiae (from broad to specific) are valid. But if the question specifies a higher genus, like 'What body is it?', then the differentia must exclude that genus's own differentiae. For example, when asking 'What animal is it?', only 'rational' (the specific differentia of humans) is the correct answer. This hierarchical precision shows that the differentia's role depends on the context of the question, but the core definition remains that it is predicated essentially.

Key Terms

الخاصة
الذات
الجوهر
الجسم

The differentia that answers a hierarchical question depends on the genus level; only the most specific essential distinguisher is valid at the lowest level.

Definition of Fasl and Critique of Genus Requirement
Author critiques the view that every essence with a differentia must have a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a correct differentia (fasl) and evaluate the argument that a differentia does not always require a genus.

Core Idea
A differentia can exist without a genus if the essence is composed of equal parts.

In this passage, the author defines the differentia (fasl) and then challenges a longstanding view among logicians—including Ibn Sina—that every essence with a differentia must have a genus. The discussion refines our understanding of the five universals and shows that definitions in Islamic sciences must be constructed with precision, avoiding unnecessary constraints.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of fasl
Find in the Arabic where the author states that fasl is 'a universal essential that is not predicated in answer to "What is it?" and distinguishes the thing in general.' Note the two conditions: not answering 'What is it?' and providing general distinction.
This definition excludes genus, which answers 'What is it?'.
2
Understand the exclusion of full commonality
The author adds that a proper differentia must not be the 'full commonality between the thing and another species.' That full commonality is the genus, which is thus excluded from the definition of fasl.
This prevents confusing genus and differentia.
3
Analyze the hypothetical of equal essences
The author imagines an essence composed of two equal parts, like the highest genus and the ultimate differentia (e.g., 'rational'). In such a case, each part could serve as a differentia because each distinguishes the essence essentially from other existents, without requiring a separate genus.
This shows a case where a differentia exists without a genus.
4
Evaluate the critique of the genus requirement
The author points out that demonstration does not support the claim that every differentia must have a genus. Two arguments are given: (1) sharing in existence does not necessitate a genus, and (2) the possibility of equal essences as a counterexample.
The critique uses logical reasoning to reject a widely held view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : لا يكتفى في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟» بالتمييز في الجملة، بل لابد معه أن لا يكون تمام المشترك بين الشيء ونوع آخر، فالجنس خارج عن التعريف ؛ ولما كان محصله أن الفصل كلّي ذاتي لا يكون مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» ويكون مميزاً للشيء في الجملة، فلو فرضنا ماهيّة مركبة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كماهية الجنس العالي والفصل الأخير - كالناطق – كان كل منهما فصلاً لها، لأنه يميز الماهية تمييزاً جوهرياً عما يشاركها في الوجود ويحمل عليها في جواب «أي موجود هو ؟». واعلم أن قدماء المنطقيين زعموا أن كل ماهية لها فصل وجب أن يكون لها جنس، حتّى أنّ الشيخ تبعهم في الشفاء" وحد الفصل بأنه كلّي مقول على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره من جنسه»، وإذ لم يساعده البرهان على ذلك تبه المصنف على ضعفه بالمشاركة في الوجود أولاً، وبإيراد هذا الاحتمال ثانياً.
So we say: In answering 'What is it in its essence?', mere distinction in general is not sufficient; rather, along with it, it must not be the full commonality between the thing and another species. Thus the genus is excluded from the definition. And since the gist of it is that the differentia is a universal essential that is not predicated in answer to 'What is it?' and it distinguishes the thing in general, so if we suppose an essence composed of two equal matters or equal matters like the essence of the highest genus and the ultimate differentia—such as 'rational'—then each of them would be a differentia for it, because it distinguishes the essence essentially from what shares existence with it and is predicated of it in answer to 'Which existent is it?'. And know that the ancient logicians claimed that every essence having a differentia necessarily has a genus, such that the Shaykh followed them in al-Shifa' and defined the differentia as 'a universal predicated of the thing in answer to "What is it in its essence" from its genus.' And since demonstration did not support him on that, the author pointed out its weakness: first by sharing in existence, and second by introducing this possibility.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the definition of differentia (fasl) and a refutation of the claim that every differentia must have a genus. The author begins by clarifying the criteria for a proper differentia: it must distinguish an essence in general but must not be the full commonality between that thing and another species, which is the role of the genus. Thus genus is excluded from the definition of differentia. The author then summarizes the standard view: differentia is a universal essential that is not predicated in answer to 'What is it?' and distinguishes the thing in general. A hypothetical example is given: if an essence is composed of two equal constituents, like the highest genus and the ultimate differentia (e.g., 'rational'), then each would be a differentia because each distinguishes the essence essentially from other existents. The author then critiques the ancient logicians, including Ibn Sina (the Shaykh), who held that every essence with a differentia must have a genus, leading them to define differentia as 'a universal predicated of the thing in answer to "What is it in its essence" from its genus.' The author points out that this is not supported by demonstration; the weakness is shown first by the fact that sharing in existence does not require a genus, and second by the possibility of essences composed of equal parts without a genus.

Proximate vs Remote Differentia
Definition of proximate and remote differentiae with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a differentia as proximate or remote based on the genus it distinguishes within.

Core Idea
The proximity of a differentia is determined by the genus it distinguishes, not by its own nature.

In the five universals, the differentia (fasl) is what distinguishes a species from other species within the same genus. But not all differentiae operate at the same level: some distinguish within the immediate genus, others within a more remote genus higher up. This lesson will teach you to identify which is which, a crucial skill for evaluating definitions in logic and Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the differentia
Find the attribute that distinguishes the species from others. In the passage, 'rational' and 'sentient' are differentiae for human.
The differentia is always a quality that sets the species apart.
2
Identify the genus being used for distinction
Determine what broader category the differentia separates the species from. For 'rational', it separates human from other animals; for 'sentient', it separates human from plants and inanimate objects.
The genus is the category within which the differentia operates.
3
Check if that genus is immediate or higher
In the hierarchy, the immediate genus is the one directly above the species. For human, the immediate genus is 'animal'. A higher remote genus is 'growing body' (above animal).
Hierarchy: species → genus → higher genus → ...
4
Classify the differentia
If the differentia distinguishes within the immediate genus, it is proximate (e.g., rational). If it distinguishes within a higher genus, it is remote (e.g., sentient).
The same species can have both proximate and remote differentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والفصل المميز للنوع عن مشاركه في الجنس «قريب» إن ميزه عنه في جنس قريب كالناطق للإنسان، و «بعيد» إن ميزه عنه في جنس بعيد كالحساس للإنسان. أقول : الفصل إمّا مميز عن المشارك الجنسي أوعن المشارك الوجودي، فإن كان مميزاً عن المشارك الجنسي فهو إما قريب أو بعيد، لأنه إن ميزه عن مشاركاته في الجنس القريب فهو فصل قريب كالناطق للإنسان، فإنّه يميزه عن مشاركاته في الحيوان وإن ميزه عن مشاركاته في الجنس البعيد فهو «فصل بعيد» كالحساس للإنسان، فإنّه يميّزه عن مشاركاته في الجسم النامي. (۱
He said: The differentia that distinguishes the species from its partner in the genus is 'proximate' if it distinguishes it from it in a proximate genus, like 'rational' for human; and 'remote' if it distinguishes it from it in a remote genus, like 'sentient' for human. I say: The differentia either distinguishes from a generic partner or from an existential partner. If it distinguishes from a generic partner, then it is either proximate or remote. Because if it distinguishes it from its fellows in the proximate genus, it is a proximate differentia, like 'rational' for human, for it distinguishes him from his fellows in 'animal'; and if it distinguishes it from its fellows in the remote genus, it is a 'remote differentia', like 'sentient' for human, for it distinguishes him from his fellows in 'growing body'.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the division of differentia (fasl) into proximate (qarib) and remote (ba'id). The matn states that a differentia distinguishing a species from others sharing its genus is called 'proximate' if it distinguishes within a proximate genus (e.g., 'rational' for human within the genus 'animal'), and 'remote' if it distinguishes within a remote genus (e.g., 'sentient' for human within 'growing body'). The sharh expands: differentia can either distinguish from a generic partner (sharing a genus) or from an existential partner (sharing existence without a common genus). The distinction between proximate and remote only applies to the first kind. If the differentia distinguishes the species from others in the immediate genus, it is proximate; if from a more distant genus higher up, it is remote. Examples are given: 'rational' distinguishes humans from other animals (proximate genus), while 'sentient' distinguishes humans from plants and inanimate objects (remote genus, 'growing body' being a subset of 'body'). This classification aids in analyzing definitions and understanding how differentiae function at various levels of generality.

Key Terms
الفصل القريب
الفصل البعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A differentia is proximate if it distinguishes a species within its immediate genus, and remote if it distinguishes within a higher, more remote genus.

Definition of Necessary and Separable Accident
Author defines the two divisions of accident: necessary and separable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and separable accidents using the criterion of inseparability from the essence.

Core Idea
Accident is either necessary or separable based on whether it can leave the essence without destroying it.

In the five universals, after substance and differentia comes accident (al-'arad). Accidents are attributes that inhere in a substance. But not all accidents are equal: some are inseparable from the essence, others can be separated. This lesson teaches you how to tell them apart.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term 'arad in the Arabic text. Note that it is introduced as 'the third' (al-thalith) of the five universals. The author is about to define its division.
2
Extract the division criterion
The key phrase is 'if its separation from the reality (mahiyya) is impossible' versus 'otherwise'. Identify this condition as the sole distinguishing factor.
The phrase 'imtana'a infikakuhu 'an al-mahiyya' is the critical test.
3
Test with examples
Apply the condition to known accidents. For man, the capacity to laugh is inseparable (if man exists, he can potentially laugh), so it is a necessary accident. For a body, whiteness can be removed (e.g., painting it black), so it is a separable accident.
Use Islamic examples: the capacity to speak is a necessary accident for humans; the whiteness of snow is separable (snow can be dirty).
4
Check against the five universals schema
Remember that necessary accident is non-constitutive but inseparable, whereas differentia is constitutive. Do not confuse the two. Necessary accident is still an accident, not part of the essence.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس ٢٤ - العرض اللازم والمفارق وأقسامها ] قال، وأما الثالث فإن امتنع انفكاكه عن الماهية فهو «اللازم» وإلا فهو «العرض المفارق».
The five universals. 24 - The necessary accident and the separable accident and their divisions. He said: As for the third [of the five universals], if its separation from the reality is impossible, then it is the 'necessary'; otherwise it is the 'separable accident'.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a definitional excerpt from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya, introducing the third of the five universals: accident ('arad). The text states that if an accident cannot be separated from the essence (mahiyya), it is called 'necessary accident' (al-'arad al-lazim); if it can be separated, it is called 'separable accident' (al-'arad al-mufariq). This classification is foundational in logic for distinguishing essential properties from accidental attributes.

Key Terms

عرض مفارق
ماهية

Key Takeaway
The dividing line between necessary and separable accident is whether the attribute can be conceptually or physically separated from the essence.

Identifying the Two Types of Accident: Inseparable and Separable
Author defines the two types of 'arad and responds to objections about the distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-'arad al-lazim and al-'arad al-mufariq and explain the logical responses to objections regarding their relationship to the essence.

Core Idea
Accidents are external to essence but may be conceptually inseparable or separable.

In this passage, the author defines the third division of the universal – the accident – into two kinds: that which cannot be separated from the essence (lazim) and that which can (mufariq). He then addresses objections concerning the proofs that distinguish them, clarifying that the accident need not be entirely external and that mental parts are distinct only in the mind. This lesson unpacks the definitions and the subtle reasoning behind the objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Identify the criterion for dividing 'arad into lazim and mufariq from the first sentence: if separation from the essence is impossible, it is lazim; otherwise it is mufariq.
2
Understand the subtlety
Note the author's comment that this is a slippery topic where even the intelligent may slip. This indicates that the distinction is not merely concrete but involves conceptual analysis.
The author warns that misapplying the criterion can lead to errors.
3
Follow the first objection
Trace the argument that not all parts of the essence necessarily depend on each other. The response allows for mutual need from different aspects, avoiding circularity.
This objection targets a proof about the parts of the mahiyya.
4
Follow the second objection
Understand that the accident need not be entirely external; it can be partially inherent. The author distinguishes between two meanings of 'arad: external to the essence vs. subsisting in it.
The example is 'rational' for man: external but not entirely so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال، وأما الثالث فإن امتنع انفكاكه عن الماهية فهو «اللازم» وإلا فهو «العرض المفارق». مما يلقيه الأذكياء فيما بينهم ويطرحون عليه أفكارهم، أي هو من المباحث الدقيقة التي يعتني بها الأذكياء ويتعرضون لتقويتها أودفعها، أو يعني أنه مما يطرح فيه الأذكياء وتوقع في الغلط، كأنه مزلقة تزل (ن : يتزلق) فيها أقدام أذهانهم ؛ والمقصود منه الإشارة إلى ما في الدليلين من الأنظار. أما في الأول فبأن يقال : لانسلم وجوب احتياج بعض أجزاء الماهية الحقيقية إلى البعض مطلقاً، بل إنما يجب ذلك في الأجزاء الخارجية المتمايزة في الوجود العيني، وأما في الأجزاء المحمولة الذهنية فلأنها أجزاء ذهنية لاتمايز بينها في الوجود الخارجي قطعاً. وأن يقال : جاز احتياج كل منهما إلى الآخر من جهتين مختلفتين، فلا يلزم الدور، وجاز أن يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر دون العكس ولا محذور، إذ لا يلزم من التساوى في الصدق التساوى في الحقيقة، فجاز أن يكونا متخالفين بالماهية فلا يلزم من الاحتياج من أحد الطرفين دون الآخر ترجيح من غير مرجّح. وأما في الدليل الثاني فبأن يقال : إنا نختار أن أحد الجزأين يصدق عليه الجوهر، و أن الجوهر خارج عنه. أما قولك : «فلايكون العارض بتمامه عارضاً وأنه محال» قلنا : استحالته ممنوعة، فإن العارض للشيء بمعنى الخارج عنه لا يجب أن يكون خارجاً عنه بجميع أجزائه، فإن الإنسان إذا قيس إلى الناطق لم يكن عينه ولاجزؤه، بـل خارجاً . عنه، وليس بتمامه خارجاً عنه ؛ نعم ، العارض للشيء بمعنى القائم به لا يجوز أن لا يكون بتمامه عارضا ،له وبين المعنيين بون بعيد (شريف).
He said: As for the third [division of the universal], if its separation from the essence is impossible, it is the 'inseparable accident'; otherwise it is the 'separable accident'. This is something that the intelligent discuss among themselves and upon which they test their thoughts. That is, it is among the subtle investigations that the intelligent pay attention to, and they attempt to strengthen or defend them. Or it means that it is something in which the intelligent slip and fall into error, as if it is a slippery place in which the feet of their minds slip (variant: slip). The purpose of it is to indicate the considerations in the two proofs. As for the first [proof], it is said: We do not concede the necessity of some parts of the true essence needing others absolutely. Rather, that is necessary only for external parts that are distinct in concrete existence. As for mental predicated parts, they are mental parts that are certainly not distinct in external existence. And it is said: It is possible for each of the two [parts] to need the other from two different aspects, so circularity does not follow. And it is possible for one of them to need the other without the reverse, with no problem, since equality in truth does not imply equality in reality. So they can be different in essence, and thus need from one side without the other does not imply preponderance without a preponderator. As for the second proof, it is said: We choose that one of the two parts is predicated of substance, and that substance is external to it. As for your saying: 'Then the accident would not be entirely an accident, and that is impossible', we say: Its impossibility is not conceded. For the accident of a thing in the sense of that which is external to it does not have to be entirely external to it. For when man is compared to 'rational', it is not identical to it nor a part of it, but is external to it, yet not entirely external to it. Yes, the accident of a thing in the sense of that which subsists in it does not allow that it not be entirely an accident to it, and between the two meanings there is a great distance (Sharif).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines two types of 'arad (accident) that are among the five universals. The author, in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya, defines the third division (the universal that is external to the essence) as either inseparable (lazim) or separable (mufariq). He then comments that this is a subtle topic where even the intelligent may slip, referring to two proofs (from the original text) that are being discussed. The remainder of the passage presents objections and responses regarding the first and second proofs. The objection to the first proof argues that not all parts of the essence necessarily depend on each other; only external parts in concrete existence do, while mental parts (like genus and differentia) are distinct only in the mind. The response allows for mutual need from different aspects, avoiding circularity. For the second proof, the objection is that the accident need not be entirely external; it can be partially inherent, as 'rational' is external to 'man' but not entirely so. The author distinguishes between two meanings of 'accident' (external vs. inherent) and concludes that the impossibility only applies to the latter. The gloss by Sharif notes the distance between the two meanings.

Key Terms
العرض اللازم
العرض المفارق
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what distinguishes 'arad lazim from 'arad mufariq?
A) By whether the attribute is permanent or temporary in existence.
B) By whether the attribute can be conceptually separated from the essence.
C) By whether the attribute is essential or accidental to the universal.
D) By whether the attribute is mental or concrete.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between inseparable and separable accident hinges on conceptual separability from the essence, not on concrete reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the attribute 'being written' for the Quran is a 'arad lazim or 'arad mufariq, and explain why using the criterion from the passage.
?
In the definition of 'iman (faith), scholars include 'belief in the heart' as essential. Is 'belief' a 'arad lazim or a 'arad mufariq for the essence of 'iman'? Defend your answer using the conceptual inseparability criterion.

Lazim al-Wujud vs Lazim al-Mahiyya
Classification of accidental necessary into existence-related and essence-related.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a property that is inseparable in existence (lazim al-wujud) and one that is inseparable in essence (lazim al-mahiyya), using examples from Islamic ontology.

Core Idea
Not every inseparable accident is essential; some cling to existence, others to quiddity.

In the classification of the five universals, the accident is divided into necessary and separable. Among the necessary, a further distinction is drawn: some accidents are inseparable from the very existence of the individual, while others are inseparable from the essence itself. Mastering this distinction sharpens your ability to analyze definitions and properties in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of lazim
From the passage, note that al-lazim is divided into lazim al-wujud and lazim al-mahiyya. Understand that lazim al-wujud accompanies the individual's existence but is not part of its essence; lazim al-mahiyya is conceptually necessary for the essence.
2
Examine the example of blackness for a black person
Blackness (sawad) is not part of the essence of being human; it is an accident that the black person happens to have. Yet it is inseparable from his existence – he cannot exist without it while being black. This illustrates lazim al-wujud.
The text uses 'حبشي' (Habashi) as the example.
3
Examine the example of evenness for four
Evenness (zawjiyya) is inseparable from the essence of four. You cannot conceive of four without it being even; evenness is a necessary property of the essence of four. This is lazim al-mahiyya.
4
Apply to new cases
Consider the property 'being created' for a contingent being. Is it a lazim al-wujud or lazim al-mahiyya? Use the criterion: is the property conceptually necessary for the essence, or merely attached to its existence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللازم قديكون لازما للوجود كالسواد للحبشي، وقد يكون لازماً للماهية كالزوجية للأربعة. وهو إما بين، وهو الذي يكون تصوّره مع تصوّر ملزومه كافياً في جزم الذهن باللزوم بينهما، كالانقسام بمتساويين للأربعة، وإماغيربين وهو الذي يفتقر جزم الذهن باللزوم بينهما إلى وسط كتساوي الزوايا الثلاث لقائمتين للمثلث. وقد يقال «البين» على اللازم الذي يلزم من تصوّر ملزومه تصوّره والأوّل أعم. والعرض المفارق: إما سريع الزوال كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل، وإما بطيئه كالشيب والشباب. أقول : الثالث من أقسام الكلّي ما يكون خارجاً عن الماهية، وهو إما أن يمتنع انفكاكه عن الماهية أو يمكن انفكاكه : والأوّل العرض اللازم كالفرديّة للثلاثة. والثاني «العرض المفارق» كالكتابة بالفعل للإنسان. واللازم : إما لازم للوجود كالسواد للحبشي، فإنّه لازم لوجوده (۱) قوله كالفردية للثلاثة وقوله : كالكتابة بالفعل للإنسان» و قوله : «كالسواد للزنجي، هذه من المساحات المشهورة في عباراتهم والأمثلة المطابقة هي الفرد والكاتب بالفعل والأسود، لأن الكلام في الكلّي الخارج عن ماهية أفراده، فلابد أن يكون محمولاً على تلك الماهية وأفرادها، لكنهم تسامحوا فذكروا مبدء المحمول بدله اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم من سياق الكلام ماهو المقصود منه، وقس على ماذكرنا سائر ماتسامحوا فيها من أمثلة الكليات (شريف).
The inseparable accident may be inseparable in existence, like blackness for an Abyssinian, and may be inseparable in essence, like evenness for four. It is either evident, which is that its conception along with the conception of its subject is sufficient for the mind to be certain of the inseparability between them, like division into two equal halves for four; or non-evident, which is that the mind's certainty of inseparability between them requires a middle term, like the equality of the three angles to two right angles for a triangle. And 'evident' may be said of the inseparable accident whose conception follows from the conception of its subject; the first meaning is more general. The separable accident: either quickly passing, like the redness of shyness and the paleness of fear, or slowly passing, like gray hair and youth. I say: The third of the divisions of the universal is that which is external to the essence. It is either impossible to separate from the essence or possible to separate. The first is the inseparable accident, like oddness for three. The second is the separable accident, like actual writing for man. And the inseparable accident is either inseparable in existence, like blackness for a black person, for it is inseparable in his existence. (1) His statement: 'like oddness for three', and his statement: 'like actual writing for man', and his statement: 'like blackness for a Zanjī', these are from the well-known slip-ups in their expressions. The appropriate examples are 'odd', 'actually writing', and 'black', because the discussion concerns the universal that is external to the essence of its individuals, so it must be predicated of that essence and its individuals. But they were lenient and mentioned the source of the predicate instead, relying on the learner's understanding from the context of what is intended. And measure by what we have mentioned the rest of the leniencies in the examples of the universals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of the third universal. First, the inseparable accident (al-lazim) is divided into: (1) that which is inseparable in existence, like blackness for a black person (this is accidental but accompanies the individual's existence), and (2) that which is inseparable in essence, like evenness for four (it cannot be conceived without the number). Then it splits the inseparable accident into clear (bayyin) and unclear (ghayr bayyin). A clear inseparability is one where the mind immediately grasps the connection, e.g., four is divisible into equal halves. An unclear one needs a proof, e.g., triangle's angles equal two right angles. There is also a second definition of 'clear' as that whose conception follows from the subject alone, but the first definition is broader. Next, the separable accident (al-mufariq) is divided into quickly passing (like blushing) and slowly passing (like gray hair). The author then summarizes the third universal: it is external to the essence, and either inseparable or separable, with examples (oddness, writing, blackness). A marginal note by Sharif criticizes the examples: the correct predications should be 'odd', 'actually writing', and 'black', not the abstract forms, but the authors were lenient for pedagogical ease.

لازم الماهية

D) Oddness for three.

Key Takeaway
An inseparable accident may be tied to existence (lazim al-wujud) or to essence (lazim al-mahiyya); this distinction refines our understanding of necessary properties.

Bayyin and Ghayr Bayyin: Degrees of Evident Inseparability
Subdivision of lazim into evident and non-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an inseparable accident is evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin) based on whether a middle term is needed to affirm the inseparability.

Core Idea
Not all inseparable connections are immediately obvious; some require proof.

After dividing the inseparable accident into two types, the passage further divides it based on how easily the mind grasps the inseparability. A connection is evident (bayyin) if merely conceiving the subject and the property is enough to affirm the connection. If a middle term (proof) is needed, it is non-evident (ghayr bayyin). This distinction helps you assess the epistemological status of a property.

Guided Path
1
Define bayyin and ghayr bayyin
The passage defines bayyin as a lazim whose conception together with the conception of its subject is sufficient for the mind to be certain of the inseparability. Ghayr bayyin requires a middle term (proof) to establish the connection.
There is a second definition: bayyin also can mean that conceiving the subject yields the conception of the lazim itself, but this is more restrictive.
2
Analyze the example of division into equal halves for four
Four is always divisible into two equal halves. The moment you conceive 'four', you grasp that it can be divided equally; no further proof is needed. This is bayyin.
3
Analyze the example of triangle angles equaling two right angles
It is not immediately obvious that a triangle's three angles sum to two right angles. You need a geometrical proof (e.g., drawing a parallel line) – a middle term. Thus it is ghayr bayyin.
4
Apply the criterion to other properties
Consider the inseparability of 'being created' from the world. Is it bayyin (immediately obvious without argument) or ghayr bayyin (requires proof like the argument from contingency)?

Sari' al-Zawal and Bati' al-Zawal: Types of Separable Accidents
Subdivision of separable accident into quick and slow passing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a separable accident that departs quickly (sari' al-zawal) and one that departs slowly (bati' al-zawal) using examples from everyday phenomena.

Core Idea
Separable accidents vary in duration; some flee quickly, others linger.

The passage completes the classification by turning to the separable accident (al-'arad al-mufariq). It divides it into two types based on how quickly it leaves: quickly passing (like the redness of embarrassment) and slowly passing (like gray hair). This simple but insightful division helps you recognize that accidents can differ in persistence, which is useful in descriptive definitions (rasm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mufariq
From the passage, al-'arad al-mufariq is either quick to disappear (sari' al-zawal) or slow to disappear (bati' al-zawal).
2
Analyze the example of fast departure
The redness of shyness (humar al-khajal) appears quickly and vanishes quickly when the emotion subsides. It is a separable accident that departs fast.
3
Analyze the example of slow departure
Gray hair (shayb) is an unavoidable accident of aging; it stays for a long time once it appears. It is slowly passing.
4
Apply to new cases
Think of the paleness of fear (sufra al-wajal). Is it sari' or bati'? The passage includes it as an example of fast departure: paleness from fear fades quickly when fear ends.
