Correct Predication of Universals: The Sharifian Correction
Marginal note correcting the predication of universal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mistake of using abstract nouns instead of adjectives when predicated universals on essences, and apply the correct predication as taught by Sharif.

Core Idea
Universals must be predicated as adjectives, not as abstract nouns, to properly refer to individuals.

The passage concludes with a marginal note by Sharif that criticizes a common leniency in examples. Many logicians used abstract nouns like 'blackness' instead of 'black' when illustrating universal accidents. Sharif points out that since the universal is external to the essence of its individuals, it should be predicated directly as an adjective (e.g., 'black') rather than its source (e.g., 'blackness'). This subtle correction prevents logical confusion between the accident and its source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples in the main text
The author uses examples like 'oddness for three', 'actual writing for man', and 'blackness for a black person'. Note that these are abstract nouns referring to the accident itself.
2
Understand Sharif's objection
Sharif says these are common leniencies (tasāmuhāt). The correct predication should be adjectives: 'odd', 'actually writing', and 'black' because the universal must be predicated of the essence and its individuals.
The universal is al-kullī al-khārij 'an māhiyyat afrādihi.
3
Apply the correction
Instead of saying 'Three has oddness', say 'Three is odd'. Instead of 'Man has writing', say 'Man is actually writing'. Instead of 'Zanjī has blackness', say 'Zanjī is black'. This aligns with the logical role of the universal.
4
Generalize to other examples
The passage tells us to measure other leniencies by these examples. Whenever you encounter a universal predicated as an abstract noun, convert it to an adjective form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللازم قديكون لازما للوجود كالسواد للحبشي، وقد يكون لازماً للماهية كالزوجية للأربعة. وهو إما بين، وهو الذي يكون تصوّره مع تصوّر ملزومه كافياً في جزم الذهن باللزوم بينهما، كالانقسام بمتساويين للأربعة، وإماغيربين وهو الذي يفتقر جزم الذهن باللزوم بينهما إلى وسط كتساوي الزوايا الثلاث لقائمتين للمثلث. وقد يقال «البين» على اللازم الذي يلزم من تصوّر ملزومه تصوّره والأوّل أعم. والعرض المفارق: إما سريع الزوال كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل، وإما بطيئه كالشيب والشباب. أقول : الثالث من أقسام الكلّي ما يكون خارجاً عن الماهية، وهو إما أن يمتنع انفكاكه عن الماهية أو يمكن انفكاكه : والأوّل العرض اللازم كالفرديّة للثلاثة. والثاني «العرض المفارق» كالكتابة بالفعل للإنسان. واللازم : إما لازم للوجود كالسواد للحبشي، فإنّه لازم لوجوده (۱) قوله كالفردية للثلاثة وقوله : كالكتابة بالفعل للإنسان» و قوله : «كالسواد للزنجي، هذه من المساحات المشهورة في عباراتهم والأمثلة المطابقة هي الفرد والكاتب بالفعل والأسود، لأن الكلام في الكلّي الخارج عن ماهية أفراده، فلابد أن يكون محمولاً على تلك الماهية وأفرادها، لكنهم تسامحوا فذكروا مبدء المحمول بدله اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم من سياق الكلام ماهو المقصود منه، وقس على ماذكرنا سائر ماتسامحوا فيها من أمثلة الكليات (شريف).
The inseparable accident may be inseparable in existence, like blackness for an Abyssinian, and may be inseparable in essence, like evenness for four. It is either evident, which is that its conception along with the conception of its subject is sufficient for the mind to be certain of the inseparability between them, like division into two equal halves for four; or non-evident, which is that the mind's certainty of inseparability between them requires a middle term, like the equality of the three angles to two right angles for a triangle. And 'evident' may be said of the inseparable accident whose conception follows from the conception of its subject; the first meaning is more general. The separable accident: either quickly passing, like the redness of shyness and the paleness of fear, or slowly passing, like gray hair and youth. I say: The third of the divisions of the universal is that which is external to the essence. It is either impossible to separate from the essence or possible to separate. The first is the inseparable accident, like oddness for three. The second is the separable accident, like actual writing for man. And the inseparable accident is either inseparable in existence, like blackness for a black person, for it is inseparable in his existence. (1) His statement: 'like oddness for three', and his statement: 'like actual writing for man', and his statement: 'like blackness for a Zanjī', these are from the well-known slip-ups in their expressions. The appropriate examples are 'odd', 'actually writing', and 'black', because the discussion concerns the universal that is external to the essence of its individuals, so it must be predicated of that essence and its individuals. But they were lenient and mentioned the source of the predicate instead, relying on the learner's understanding from the context of what is intended. And measure by what we have mentioned the rest of the leniencies in the examples of the universals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of the third universal. First, the inseparable accident (al-lazim) is divided into: (1) that which is inseparable in existence, like blackness for a black person (this is accidental but accompanies the individual's existence), and (2) that which is inseparable in essence, like evenness for four (it cannot be conceived without the number). Then it splits the inseparable accident into clear (bayyin) and unclear (ghayr bayyin). A clear inseparability is one where the mind immediately grasps the connection, e.g., four is divisible into equal halves. An unclear one needs a proof, e.g., triangle's angles equal two right angles. There is also a second definition of 'clear' as that whose conception follows from the subject alone, but the first definition is broader. Next, the separable accident (al-mufariq) is divided into quickly passing (like blushing) and slowly passing (like gray hair). The author then summarizes the third universal: it is external to the essence, and either inseparable or separable, with examples (oddness, writing, blackness). A marginal note by Sharif criticizes the examples: the correct predications should be 'odd', 'actually writing', and 'black', not the abstract forms, but the authors were lenient for pedagogical ease.

غير بين
سريع الزوال
بطيء الزوال
تسامح

C) Oddness is in three.
D) Three is oddness.

Key Takeaway
Universals must be predicated as adjectives, not abstract nouns, to maintain logical correctness.

Division of Lāzim into Dhihnī and Māhiyyī
Author defines two types of necessary concomitant and responds to an objection about their division into evident and non-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental concomitant (luzūm dhihnī) and essential concomitant (lāzim al-māhiyya) and explain why the essential concomitant can be non-evident.

Core Idea
Not all necessary concomitants are consciously perceived; essential concomitants may be present without awareness.

In the previous lesson, you learned that properties of a thing include necessary concomitants (lāzim). Now we delve deeper: a concomitant can be either mental, where mere conception of the subject yields the predicate, or essential, where the concomitant is inseparable from the essence. But does that mean every essential concomitant is immediately known? The following passage, from a commentary on al-Shamsiyya, clarifies this by responding to an objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of lāzim
Find the definitions of luzūm dhihnī and lāzim al-māhiyya in the Arabic. Luzūm dhihnī: the conception of the subject alone suffices to realize the predicate in the mind. Lāzim al-māhiyya: the concomitant is inseparable from the essence in all modes of existence.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that lāzim al-māhiyya must be evident in the more specific sense (bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass), meaning the conception of the subject alone yields the predicate. Why? Because if the essence exists in the mind, its concomitant must also exist there, thus automatically perceived.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author uses the triangle example: the essence of triangle carries the property of angle equality, but one may be unaware of it. Thus, presence in the mind does not entail conscious perception. This shows that lāzim al-māhiyya can be non-evident.
4
Distinguish the two senses of 'evident'
Bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-a‘amm: the conception of both subject and predicate together yields the judgment of necessity. Bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass: the conception of the subject alone yields the conception of the predicate (and the judgment).
The passage confirms the division of lāzim al-māhiyya into these two categories.
5
Evaluate the later objection
Al-Sayyid al-Sharīf objects that the first sense (a‘amm) is not necessarily more general. He proposes that if the second sense includes the judgment of necessity, then it is clearly more specific. This shows the importance of precise definitions.
This is a meta-commentary refining the classical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس ككون الاثنين ضعف الواحد، فإن من تصور الاثنين أدرك أنه ضعف الواحد، والمعني الأوّل أعم ، لأنه متى يكفي تصور الملزوم في اللزوم بدون إدراك الأول ويسمّى لزوماً ذهنياً ، وإما أن يكون بالنظر إلى الماهية من حيث هي هي، على معنى أنها يمتنع أن توجد بأحد الوجودين منفكة عن ذلك اللازم، بل أينما وجدت كانت معه موصوفة به، ويسمى هذا اللازم لازم الماهية». فإن قلت: لازم الماهية من حيث هي هي يجب أن يكون لازماً ذهنياً، لأن الماهية إذا وجدت في الذهن وجب أن يوجد ذلك اللازم فيه أيضاً، فيكون لازم الماهية لازماً ذهنياً قطعاً، فيكون بيناً بالمعنى الأخص، فلا يجوز انقسامه إلى اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم وغير البين. قلت: الواجب في لازم الماهية أن يكون بحيث إذا وجدت الماهية في الذهن كانت متصفة به ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون اللازم مدركاً مشعوراً به، فإن ماهية المثلث إذا وجدت في الذهن كانت موصوفة بكون زواياها الثلاث مساوية لقائمتين، ومع ذلك يمكن أن لا يكون للذهن شعور بمفهوم المساواة المذكورة – فضلا عن الجزم بثبوتها لماهية المثلث - فليس كل ماكان حاصلاً للماهية المدركة في الذهن يجب أن يكون مدركاً، فإن كون الماهية مدركة صفة حاصلة لها هناك، مع أنه لا يجب الشعور به، وإلا لزم من إدراك أمر واحد إدراك أمور غير متناهية، بل يجوز أن يكون لازم الماهية بحيث يلزم من تصورهما الجزم باللزوم بينهما، وأن لا يكون كذلك ؛ فصح الانقسام إلى البين بالمعنى الأعم وغير البين ؛ ويجوز أن يكون بحيث يلزم من تصوّر الملزوم - أي الماهية - تصوره فيكون بيناً بالمعنى الأخص، وأن لا يكون بهذه الحيثية (شريف). اعترض عليه بأن المعتبر في الأول هو كون تصورهما كافيين في الجزم باللزوم، والمعتبر في الثاني هو كون تصوّر الملزوم كافياً في تصوّر اللازم ؛ وبهذا يكفي تصوّر اللازم مع تصوّر الملزوم ، وليس كلّما يكفي التصوّر أن يكفي تصور واحد. القدر لم يتبين كون الأول أعم، إذ ربما كان تصوّر الملزوم كافياً في تصوّر اللازم ولا يكون التصوران معاً كافيين في الجزم باللزوم، فلابد لنفي ذلك من دليل. نعم لوفسر البين بالمعنى الثاني بـ «ما يكون تصور الملزوم كافياً في تصوّر اللازم مع الجزم باللزوم، كان المعنى الثاني أخص من الأول بلاشبهة، لكن لم يثبت هذا التفسير في كلامهم (شريف).
The five universals. Like the fact that two is double one, for whoever conceives two perceives that it is double one. The first meaning is more general, because whenever the conception of the necessitating condition suffices for the necessity without perceiving the first, it is called 'mental necessity'. And sometimes it is with respect to the essence as such, meaning that it is impossible for it to exist in either of the two modes of existence separated from that necessary concomitant; rather wherever it exists, it is with it qualified by it, and this necessary concomitant is called 'necessary concomitant of the essence'. If you say: The necessary concomitant of the essence as such must be a mental necessary concomitant, because if the essence exists in the mind, that necessary concomitant must also exist in it, so the necessary concomitant of the essence is definitely a mental necessary concomitant, thus it would be evident in the more specific sense, so its division into the evident in the more general sense and the non-evident would not be permissible. I say: The necessary requirement for the necessary concomitant of the essence is that it be such that when the essence exists in the mind, it is qualified by it, but it does not follow from that that the necessary concomitant is perceived and sensed. For the essence of triangle when it exists in the mind is qualified by the equality of its three angles to two right angles, and yet it is possible that the mind has no awareness of the concept of that equality—let alone judgment of its being established for the essence of triangle. So not everything that is present for the perceived essence in the mind must be perceived. For the fact that the essence is perceived is a quality present for it there, and yet awareness of it is not necessary; otherwise perceiving one thing would necessitate perceiving infinite things. Rather it is possible that the necessary concomitant of the essence is such that from the conception of both of them, the judgment of necessity between them follows, and that it is not so. Thus the division into the evident in the more general sense and the non-evident is correct. And it is possible that it is such that from the conception of the necessitating condition (i.e., the essence) its conception follows, so it is evident in the more specific sense, and that it is not in this respect. (Sharif). It was objected that what is considered in the first is that the conception of both is sufficient for the judgment of necessity, and what is considered in the second is that the conception of the necessitating condition is sufficient for the conception of the necessary concomitant; and with this, the conception of the necessary concomitant together with the conception of the necessitating condition suffices, and not every time the conception suffices does a single conception suffice. [The gloss says:] It is not established that the first is more general, for perhaps the conception of the necessitating condition is sufficient for the conception of the necessary concomitant and yet the two conceptions together are not sufficient for the judgment of necessity, so a proof is needed to negate that. Yes, if the evident in the second sense is interpreted as 'what the conception of the necessitating condition is sufficient for the conception of the necessary concomitant together with the judgment of necessity', then the second sense would be more specific than the first without doubt, but this interpretation is not established in their words. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of necessary concomitants (lāzim) within the framework of the five universals. The author first introduces a definitional example: two is the double of one—if you conceive two, you immediately perceive that it is double. This illustrates the first type of concomitant, where the conception of the subject alone suffices to know the predicate; this is called 'mental necessity' (luzūm dhihnī). The second type is considered from the essence itself (al-māhiyya min ḥaythu hiya hiya), where the concomitant is inseparable from the essence regardless of existence. This is called 'necessary concomitant of the essence' (lāzim al-māhiyya). An objector then claims that lāzim al-māhiyya must necessarily be mental, because if the essence exists in the mind, its concomitant must also exist there, making it evident in the more specific sense (bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass), which would prevent its division into evident and non-evident. The author responds by clarifying that while lāzim al-māhiyya is indeed present in the mind, it need not be consciously perceived. The example of a triangle: its essence in the mind carries the property that its angles equal two right angles, but one may be unaware of that equality. Thus, not every mental attribute is perceived—otherwise, perceiving one thing would require perceiving infinite things. Hence, lāzim al-māhiyya can be such that the conception of both subject and predicate together yields the judgment of necessity (evident in the more general sense, bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-a‘amm), or it can be such that the conception of the subject alone yields the conception of the predicate (evident in the more specific sense, bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass). The division is thus valid. A subsequent objection (by al-Sayyid al-Sharīf) challenges the claim that the first sense is more general, arguing that it is possible for the subject's conception to suffice for the predicate's conception without the two together being sufficient for the judgment of necessity. The response suggests that if the second sense is reinterpreted to include the judgment of necessity, then it would indeed be more specific, but such an interpretation is not found in the standard texts.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim): A necessary concomitant, either mental (where conception of subject suffices for knowledge of the predicate) or essential (where the concomitant is inseparable from the essence regardless of existence). [Note: Stable across the tradition; the division into dhihnī and māhiyyī is standard in post-Abharian logic.]
لزوم ذهني (luzūm dhihnī): A concomitant such that the conception of the subject alone is sufficient to realize the predicate in the mind, without requiring a separate act of inference. [Note: This term is used consistently in later commentaries on al-Shamsiyya.]
لازم الماهية (lāzim al-māhiyya): A concomitant that is inseparable from the essence, meaning the essence cannot exist without it in either mental or external existence. [Note: In earlier logic, the term 'lāzim' alone often sufficed; the addition 'al-māhiyya' is a precise specification.]
بين بالمعنى الأعم (bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-a‘amm): Evident in the more general sense: the two concepts together (subject and predicate) necessarily produce the judgment of their relation. [Note: This distinction between a‘amm and akhass is a refinement found in later commentaries, not in the matn of al-Shamsiyya itself.]
بين بالمعنى الأخص (bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass): Evident in the more specific sense: the conception of the subject alone automatically produces the conception of the predicate. [Note: Same as above.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about lāzim al-māhiyya?
A) It is always evident in the more specific sense (bayyin bi-l-ma‘nā al-akhass).
B) It is inseparable from the essence but may not be perceived.
C) It requires a separate act of inference to be known.
D) It is the same as luzūm dhihnī.

Key Takeaway
Lāzim al-māhiyya may be present in the mind without being consciously perceived, so it can be divided into evident (in the more general sense) and non-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Property and General Accident: The Accidental Universals
Author defines property and general accident and concludes the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an accidental universal is a property (khāṣṣa) or a general accident ('araḍ 'āmm) based on its scope of predication.

Core Idea
Property is unique to one species; general accident spans multiple species.

We have already studied the essential universals—genus, species, and differentia. Now we turn to the two accidental universals: property and general accident. This passage from al-Qutbī al-Taḥtānī defines each and provides examples to illustrate the difference. Understanding these two completes the set of five universals that logicians use to classify concepts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential from accidental predicates
Note that both property and general accident are predicated in an accidental manner (قول عرضي). This means they are not part of the essence of the subject.
2
Check the scope of the predicate
If the predicate applies only to individuals of a single essence (حقيقة واحدة), it is a property. If it applies to individuals of multiple essences, it is a general accident.
3
Apply to the examples given
Laughter is specific to humans, so it is a property. Walking applies to many species, so it is a general accident.
4
Understand the commentator's synthesis
The commentator clarifies that every universal outside the essence—whether necessary concomitant or separable—falls into one of these two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وكلّ واحد من اللازم والمفارق إن اختص بأفراد حقيقة واحدة فهو «الخاصة كالضاحك، وإلا فهو «العرض العام» كالماشي. وترسم الخاصة بأنها كلّيّة مقولة على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط، قولاً عرَضيّاً. والعرض العام بأنه كلّيّ مقول على أفراد حقيقة واحدة وغيرها، قولاً عرَضيّاً. فالكليات إذن خمس نوع وجنس وفصل وخاصة وعرض عام. أقول : الكلّي الخارج عن الماهية - سواء كان لازماً أو مفارقاً - إما خاصة أو عرض عام.
He said: And each one of the necessary concomitant and the separable—if it is specific to individuals of one essence, then it is 'property' (al-khāṣṣa), like laughter; otherwise it is 'general accident' (al-‘araḍ al-‘āmm), like walking. And property is defined as a universal predicated of what is under one essence only, in an accidental manner of speech. And general accident is that it is a universal predicated of individuals of one essence and of others, in an accidental manner of speech. So the universals are therefore five: species, genus, differentia, property, and general accident. I say: The universal that is outside the essence—whether necessary concomitant or separable—is either property or general accident.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage provides the definitions of two of the five universals: property (khāṣṣa) and general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm). The author quotes the matn statement: every necessary concomitant or separable accident, if it is specific to individuals of a single essence (ḥaqīqa wāḥida), it is a property (like the ability to laugh for humans); if not, it is a general accident (like walking, which applies to many species). Then he gives the formal definitions: property is a universal predicated of things under a single essence only, in an accidental manner (i.e., not part of the essence). General accident is a universal predicated of individuals of one essence and of others, also accidentally. The passage then concludes that the five universals are: species (naw‘), genus (jins), differentia (faṣl), property (khāṣṣa), and general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm). Finally, the commentator adds his own note: any universal outside the essence, whether necessary concomitant or separable, falls under either property or general accident.

Key Terms
خاصة
عرض عام
كليات خمس
قول عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Property is an accidental universal unique to one essence; general accident is an accidental universal common to multiple essences. Together with the three essential universals, they form the five universals.

Distinguishing Property and Common Accident
Commentator defines al-khassah and al-'ard al-'amm and explains their rasam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-khassah (property) from al-'ard al-'amm (common accident) and explain the rasam of each, including which universals each condition excludes.

Core Idea
Property is an accident unique to one reality; common accident is shared—both are accidental predicates.

In the five universals, two are accidental: property (khassah) and common accident ('ard 'amm). This passage defines them and their descriptions (rusum), showing how each part of the description excludes other universals. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for constructing precise definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of distinction
The passage divides accidental universals based on whether they are specific to one reality or shared. Note the examples: 'risible' (property of man) and 'walking' (common accident).
The accident can be necessary (lazim) or separable (mufariq).
2
Extract the rasam of property
The rasam of property includes three elements: universal, predicated only of individuals of one reality, and accidental. Note that 'universal' is redundant (since we are discussing universals). 'Only' excludes genus and common accident, which apply to multiple realities. 'Accidental' excludes species and differentia, which are essential.
The commentator emphasizes that these exclusions are deliberate.
3
Extract the rasam of common accident
The rasam of common accident includes: universal, predicated of individuals of one reality and others, and accidental. 'And others' excludes species, differentia, and property (which are restricted to one reality). 'Accidental' excludes genus (essential).
Also, differentiae of genera are excluded by the accidental condition.
4
Understand why these are rusum not hadd
The commentator concludes that these definitions are descriptions (rusum) rather than essential definitions (hadd), because they define the universals by accidental properties. The universals might have essential definitions that are not captured here.

(وكذا يخرج فصول الأجناس كالحسّاس ومافوقه، لكن القيد الأخير يخرج الفصول مطلقاً : أعني فصول الأنواع والأجناس، فلذلك أسند إخراج الفصول إليه (شريف).) He said: And each one of the necessary and separable [accident], if it is specific to individuals of one reality, then it is 'property' like 'risible', otherwise it is 'common accident' like 'walking'. And property is described as a universal predicated of what is under one reality only, in an accidental mode. And common accident is [described] as a universal predicated of individuals of one reality and others, in an accidental mode. Thus the universals are five: species, genus, differentia, property, and common accident. I say: The universal external to the essence – whether necessary or separable – is either a property or a common accident. Because if it is specific to individuals of one reality, then it is 'property' – like 'risible', for it is specific to the reality of man – and if it is not specific to it but encompasses it and others, then it is 'common accident' – like 'walking', for it includes man and others. And property is described as a universal predicated of individuals of one reality only, in an accidental mode. So 'universal' is redundant – as has passed more than once – and our saying 'only' excludes genus and common accident, because they are predicated of different realities, and our saying 'in an accidental mode' excludes species and differentia, because their predication of what is under them is essential, not accidental. And common accident is described as a universal predicated of individuals of one reality and others in an accidental mode. So by our saying 'and others' are excluded species, differentia, and property, because they are not said except of individuals of one reality only, and by our saying 'in an accidental mode' is excluded genus, because its predication is essential. (And likewise are excluded the differentiae of genera like 'sensitive' and what is above it, but the last restriction excludes differentiae absolutely: I mean the differentiae of species and genera, thus the exclusion of differentiae is attributed to it (Sharif).) (The exclusion of species by this restriction is without doubt, and likewise the exclusion of the differentia of species like 'rational'; as for the differentiae of genera – I mean the remote differentiae of species, for example – they are excluded by the last restriction (Sharif).) And these definitions are only descriptions (rusum) of the universals because it is possible that they have

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the five universals, specifically focusing on two accidental universals: al-khassah (property) and al-'ard al-'amm (common accident). The author begins by citing the matn: if a necessary or separable accident is specific to one reality, it is property; otherwise it is common accident. Examples: 'risible' (property of man) and 'walking' (common accident shared by man and other animals). Then the commentator (أقول) clarifies that any universal external to the essence falls into one of these two categories. The rasam (description) of property includes three elements: it is universal, predicated only of individuals of one reality, and in an accidental mode. Each element excludes other universals: 'universal' is a redundant reminder (since we are discussing universals), 'only' excludes genus and common accident (which apply to multiple realities), and 'accidental' excludes species and differentia (which are essential). Similarly, the description of common accident: 'universal' is redundant, 'and others' excludes species, differentia, and property (which are restricted to one reality), and 'accidental' excludes genus (essential). The commentator also notes that the differentiae of genera (e.g., 'sensitive') are excluded by the 'accidental' restriction because they are essential. The passage concludes that these definitions are rusum (descriptions) rather than hadd (essential definitions) because the universals might have essential definitions that are not captured by these accidental descriptions.

Differentiating Special Characteristic from Common Accident
Author defines the special characteristic and common accident with their distinguishing features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given universal as a special characteristic or common accident by checking its scope and mode of predication.

Core Idea
Scope and essentiality together define the five universals.

The author now defines two of the five universals: the special characteristic and the common accident. The key to telling them apart lies in two conditions: does the universal apply to only one reality or many, and is its predication essential or accidental? This lesson will walk you through the precise logical distinctions that separate these two types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals being defined
Locate the terms 'al-khassa' and 'al-'arad al-'amm' in the Arabic. Notice that both are defined by contrast: one is restricted to a single reality, the other spans multiple realities.
2
Focus on the scope condition: 'only' vs 'and others'
In the definition of al-khassa, the word 'فقط' (only) restricts it to individuals of one reality. In al-'arad al-'amm, the phrase 'وغيرها' (and others) explicitly includes other realities. This is the primary differentiator.
The author uses 'فقط' and 'وغيرها' as key qualifiers.
3
Focus on the mode of predication: accidental
Both definitions include the condition 'قولاً عرضياً' (accidentally predicated). This excludes essential predications like species and differentia. Compare: 'laughing' is accidental to humans, while 'rational' is essential.
4
Apply the definitions to examples
Test the examples given: 'laughing' is specific to humans and accidental → khassa. 'walking' applies to humans and other animals and accidental → 'arad 'amm. Explain why each condition is met.
Use the same reasoning to classify other universals from Islamic sciences.

And if it is not specific to it but rather encompasses it and others, then it is the 'common accident' - like 'walking', for it includes man and others. Commentary
This passage defines two of the five universals: the special characteristic (al-khassa) and the common accident (al-'arad al-'amm). The author begins by contrasting them: if a universal applies exclusively to individuals of a single reality (haqiqa), it is a special characteristic, exemplified by 'laughing' which applies only to humans. If it applies to individuals of multiple realities, it is a common accident, exemplified by 'walking' which applies to humans and other animals. Then formal definitions (rusum) are given: the special characteristic is a universal predicated accidentally on individuals of only one reality. The term 'universal' is noted as redundant, as it is common to all five. The condition 'only' excludes genus and common accident, which are predicated on multiple realities. The condition 'accidentally' excludes species and differentia, which are essential (dhati) predications. Similarly, the common accident is defined as a universal predicated accidentally on individuals of one reality and others. Here 'and others' excludes species, differentia, and special characteristic, which are limited to one reality. The accidental predication condition excludes genus, which is essential. This precision ensures each universal is uniquely identified by its scope and mode of predication.

Why the Five Universals Are Real Definitions (Hadd) Not Descriptions (Rasm)
Author defends definitions of universals as hadd and explains correct predication form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the claim that definitions of the five universals are genuine hadd (real definitions) and explain why derived nouns like "natiq" are used instead of abstract nouns like "nutq" in predication.

Core Idea
The five universals have no essence beyond their concepts; thus their definitions are hadd, not rasm.

In this passage, the commentator argues that the definitions of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are genuine hadd (real definitions) rather than mere rasm (descriptions). He then explains a key technical point: when presenting examples of these universals, we must use derived nouns (like "natiq") that allow predication by identity (haml al-muwata'a), rather than abstract nouns (like "nutq") which would only allow derivative predication (haml al-ishtiqaq). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly defining terms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author insists that definitions of the five universals are hadd (real definitions), not rasm (descriptions). Find the sentence where he states this.
2
Understand the basis for the claim
He argues that universals are "umur i'tibariyya" (mental considerations) whose concepts are immediately grasped and named; there are no separate essences behind them. Why does that make their definitions hadd?
3
Distinguish between haml al-muwata'a and haml al-ishtiqaq
The author explains that predicates like "natiq" (speaker) are predicated of individuals by identity, while abstract nouns like "nutq" (speech) cannot. How does this affect the choice of example terms?
4
Apply the distinction to examples
The author recommends using "natiq", "dahik", "mashi" instead of "nutq", "dahk", "mashy". Verify why the derived forms work for predication by identity.
5
Reflect on the threefold division of predication
Some scholars add a third type (composition), but the author merges it with derivation. Why is that better?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماهيات وراء تلك المفهومات ملزومات مساوية لها، فحيث لم يتحقق ذلك أطلق عليها اسم «الرسم». وهو بمعزل عن التحقيق، لأن الكليات أمور اعتبارية حصلت مفهوماتها أولاً ووضعت أسماؤها بإزائها، فليس لها معان غير تلك المفهومات، فتكون هي حدوداً لها ) ، على أن عدم العلم بأنها حدود أعم لا يوجب العلم بأنها رسوم ; فكان المناسب ذكر التعريف الذي هو من الحد والرسم. وفي تمثيل الكليات بالناطق والضاحك والماشي - لا بالنطق والضحك والمشي التي هي مبادئها - فائدة، وهي أن المعتبر في حمل الكلي على جزئياته حمل المواطأة - وهو حمل هو هـو – لاحمل الاشتقاق – وهو حمل ذوهو - والنطق والضحك والمشي لا يصدق على أفراد الإنسان بالمواطأة ، فلايقال «زيد نطق » بل «ذو نطق» أو «ناطق». بل النطق يصدق على أفراده - أعني نطق زيد ونطق عمرو ونطق خالد - بالمواطأة، فيكون كلّيّاً بالقياس إليها. وأما بالقياس إلى أفراد الإنسان فلا ; نعم إذا اشتق منه الناطق» أو ركب مع «ذو» كان ذلك المشتق أو المركب كليا بالقياس إلى أفراد الإنسان لحمله عليها بالمواطأة، وقس عليه الضحك والمشي ونظائرهما. وبعضهم جعل الحمل ثلاثة أقسام : حمل المواطأة وحمل الاشتقاق وحمل التركيب ; ولما كان مؤدّى الأخيرين واحداً كان جعلهما قسماً واحداً أولى (شریف).
Essences behind those concepts that are necessary and coextensive with them; when that is not realized, they are called 'rasm'. And he is far from verification, because the universals are mental matters whose concepts are first obtained and their names placed corresponding to them; they have no meanings other than those concepts, so they are definitions for them. Yet the ignorance that they are definitions being more general does not necessitate that they are descriptions. So it was appropriate to mention the definition which belongs to both hadd and rasm. And in representing the universals with 'natiq', 'dahik', and 'mashi' — not with 'nutq', 'dahk', and 'mashy' which are their principles — there is a benefit, namely that what is considered in predication of the universal upon its particulars is predication of identity (haml al-muwata'a), which is predication that it is it, not predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq), which is predication that it is of it. And 'nutq', 'dahk', and 'mashy' are not true of the individuals of man by identity; so one does not say 'Zayd is nutq' but rather 'possessor of nutq' or 'natiq'. Rather, 'nutq' is true of its individuals — namely, the nutq of Zayd, the nutq of 'Amr, and the nutq of Khalid — by identity, so it is a universal relative to them. But relative to the individuals of man, no. Yes, when 'natiq' is derived from it or combined with 'dhu', that derived or composite becomes a universal relative to the individuals of man because it is predicated of them by identity. And analogize for 'dahk', 'mashy', and their likes. And some made predication three divisions: predication of identity, predication of derivation, and predication of composition. And since the import of the last two is one, making them one division is better (Sharif).
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the five universals, specifically defending the view that their definitions are genuine hadd (definition) rather than rasm (description). The author argues that the universals are purely mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) whose concepts are the first objects and whose names are assigned to those concepts. Therefore, their definitions are real definitions (hadd haqiqi) because there is no extra reality behind the concept. He criticizes the alternative view that would make them rasm. He then explains a technical point: when representing the universals, it is better to use derived nouns like 'natiq' (speaking) rather than abstract nouns like 'nutq' (speech). This is because the universal is predicated of its particulars by predication of identity (haml al-muwata'a), where the predicate is identical to the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is a speaker'), not by derivative predication (haml al-ishtiqaq) where the predicate is something derived from the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is speaking' or 'Zayd has speech'). The abstract noun 'nutq' cannot be predicated of a person by identity; it is only predicated of particular instances of speech. He notes that some divide predication into three types (identity, derivation, and composition), but combining the last two is more appropriate. This passage thus clarifies the precise nature of the definitions of the universals and the correct way to express them in examples.

Key Takeaway
The definitions of the five universals are real definitions (hadd) because they are identical to their concepts, and examples of universals must use derived nouns to allow predication by identity.

The Five Universals: Classification and Definition
Author enumerates the five universals with their definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given universal predicate into one of the five universals by applying the three-way division (essence, internal, external).

Core Idea
The five universals are derived from the relation of a universal to the essence of its particulars.

This passage presents the foundational taxonomy of the five universals (al-kulliyat al-khams) that every logician must internalize. The author classifies every universal predicate based on whether it is identical to the essence of its particulars, part of the essence, or outside the essence. By mastering this division, you will be able to analyze any definition or description in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the three fundamental relations
The universal can relate to the essence of its particulars in one of three ways: it is either the very essence (identical), internal to the essence (a part), or external to the essence (an accident). Write these three categories down.
2
Identify the species (النوع)
If the universal is identical to the essence of its particulars, it is the species. For example, 'human' is the species of Zayd, because 'human' is exactly what Zayd is in his essence.
Species is the most specific universal in a hierarchy.
3
Distinguish genus (الجنس) from differentia (الفصل)
If the universal is internal to the essence, it is either shared with other species (genus) or unique to that species (differentia). For example, in 'human' the genus is 'animal' (shared with horse), and the differentia is 'rational' (not shared).
Genius and differentia together constitute the essence.
4
Separate property (الخاصة) from common accident (العرض العام)
If the universal is external to the essence, it is either specific to one species (property) or common to many (common accident). For example, 'risible' is a property of humans, while 'walking' is a common accident.
Property is unique but accidental; common accident is non-unique and accidental.
5
Apply the taxonomy to a new example
Take the concept 'horse'. Classify the following predicates: 'animal', 'neighing', 'four-legged', 'mammal'. Use the three-way division to determine which universal each is.
Practice with real examples solidifies the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ قد سمعت ماتلونا عليك ظهر لك أن الكليات منحصرة في خمس : نوع، وجنس، وفصل، وخاصة ، وعرض عام; لأن الكلي إما أن يكون نفس ماهية ماتحته من الجزئيات، أو داخلاً فيها، أو خارجاً عنها : فإن كان نفس ماهيّة ماتحته من الجزئيات فهو «النوع». وإن كان داخلاً فيها، فإما أن يكون تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر فهو «الجنس». وألا يكون فهو «الفصل». وإن كان خارجاً عنها، فإن اختص بحقيقة واحدة فهو «الخاصة». وإلا فهو «العرض العام».
And since you have heard what we recited to you, it becomes clear to you that the universals are confined to five: species, genus, differentia, property, and common accident. Because the universal is either the very essence of what is under it from the particulars, or internal to it, or external to it. If it is the very essence of what is under it from the particulars, then it is 'species'. If it is internal to it, then either it is the entirety of what is shared between the essence and another species, then it is 'genus'; or else it is not shared, then it is 'differentia'. And if it is external to it, then if it is specific to one nature, then it is 'property'; otherwise, it is 'common accident'.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage provides a classic enumeration and definition of the five universals (kulliyat khams). The author explains that the five universals are derived from the relation of a universal to its particulars. The universal can relate to the essence of its particulars in three ways: either it is identical to the essence, or it is part of the essence, or it is outside the essence. If it is identical to the essence, it is the species (naw'). If it is internal to (i.e., part of) the essence, then it is either shared with another species (i.e., a common part), which is the genus (jins), or it is not shared, which is the differentia (fasl). If it is external to the essence, then it is either specific to one species (i.e., a property, khassa) or not specific (i.e., a common accident, 'arad 'amm). This classification is fundamental in logic because it categorizes every predicate that can be said of a subject. For example, in 'Human is an animal', 'animal' is the genus (shared part); 'Human is rational', 'rational' is the differentia (non-shared part); 'Human is a risible being', 'risible' is a property (specific to human); 'Human is tall', 'tall' is a common accident. This passage should be memorized as it is the standard way to divide the five predicables (al-mafahim al-khamsa).

Key Terms
النوع
الجنس
الفصل
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a universal is external to the essence and not specific to one nature, it is called:
A) Property (خاصة)
B) Common accident (عرض عام)
C) Differentia (فصل)
D) Genus (جنس)

Key Takeaway
The five universals are an exhaustive classification based on the relation of a universal to the essence of its particulars: identical (species), internal shared (genus), internal unique (differentia), external unique (property), external non-unique (common accident).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept 'Zakat' (obligatory charity). Identify which of the five universals each of the following predicates represents: 'worship act', 'obligatory', 'purification of wealth', 'specific to Muslims', 'financial'. Use the essence/internal/external division to justify each classification.
?
Consider the definition 'Human is a rational animal'. Identify the genus, differentia, and species in this definition. Then, propose a property and a common accident for the species 'human'.

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Classifying Universals by Essence Relation
Author defines the five universals based on their relation to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal term as one of the five universals based on its relation to the essence of its particulars.

Core Idea
The five universals exhaustively categorize all universal terms by whether they are the essence, part of it, or outside it.

This passage from Qutbi's commentary lays the foundation for understanding definitions in Islamic logic. It begins by distinguishing two types of predication—homiletic and derivative—before presenting the three-fold classification that yields the five universals. Mastering this classification is the first step to constructing and evaluating definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish homiletic vs derivative predication
The passage explains that homiletic predication (حمل المواطأة) means the predicate is identical in meaning to the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is human'), while derivative predication (حمل الاشتقاق) uses a derived term (e.g., 'Zayd is speaking'). This distinction is crucial because only universals that are part of the essence can be predicated homiletically.
Remember: 'speech' is not predicated homiletically of Zayd; we say 'Zayd is speaking' not 'Zayd is speech'.
2
Grasp the three relations to essence
The author states that every universal is either: (a) the essence itself of its particulars, (b) inside the essence (a part of it), or (c) outside the essence. These three categories are mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive.
This is the logical backbone of the entire classification.
3
Identify species as the essence itself
If a universal is the full essence of its particulars, it is species (نوع). For example, 'human' is the species of Zayd because 'Zayd is human' is homiletic and human is Zayd's essence.
Species is the most specific universal that captures the complete quiddity.
4
Differentiate genus and differentia as inside the essence
If a universal is part of the essence and shared with another species, it is genus (جنس) — e.g., 'animal' shared by human and horse. If it is part of the essence but not shared (it distinguishes), it is differentia (فصل) — e.g., 'rational' for humans.
Genus is the common part; differentia is the unique part.
5
Distinguish property and general accident as outside the essence
If a universal is outside the essence and specific to one reality, it is property (خاصة) — e.g., 'laughing' for humans. If it is outside and not specific, it is general accident (عرض عام) — e.g., 'walking' that applies to humans and other animals.
These are non-essential but nevertheless universal predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكليات الخمس. على جزئياته حمل المواطأة - وهو حمل هو هو – لا حمل الاشتقاق – وهو حمل ذو هو - والنطق والضحك والمشي لا يصدق على أفراد الإنسان بالمواطأة، فلا يقال «زيد نطق» بل «ذو نطق» أو «ناطق». وإذ قد سمعت ما تلونا عليك ظهر لك أن الكليات منحصرة في خمس: نوع، وجنس، وفصل، وخاصة، وعرض عام؛ لأن الكلي إما أن يكون نفس ماهية ما تحته من الجزئيات، أو داخلاً فيها، أو خارجاً عنها: فإن كان نفس ماهية ما تحته من الجزئيات فهو «النوع». وإن كان داخلاً فيها، فإما أن يكون تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر فهو «الجنس». أو لا يكون فهو «الفصل». وإن كان خارجاً عنها، فإن اختص بحقيقة واحدة فهو «الخاصة». وإلا فهو «العرض العام».
The five universals. [It is explained that] the predication of being identical [homiletic predication] on its particulars is a predication that is 'it is it' – not derivative predication – and it is the predication of 'possessor of it'. And speech, laughter, and walking are not true of the individuals of human by homiletic predication, so one does not say 'Zayd is speech' but 'possessor of speech' or 'speaking'. And since you have heard what we recited to you, it becomes clear to you that the universals are confined to five: species, genus, differentia, property, and general accident. Because the universal is either the essence of what is under it from the particulars, or inside it, or outside it. If it is the essence of what is under it from the particulars, then it is 'species'. If it is inside it, then either it is the complete commonality between the essence and another species, so it is 'genus'. Or it is not, so it is 'differentia'. If it is outside it, then if it is specific to one reality, it is 'property'. Otherwise, it is 'general accident'.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines the five universals, which are the foundational classification of all universal terms used in definition and predication. The author begins by clarifying the two types of predication: homiletic (المواطأة) where the predicate is identical in meaning to the subject, like saying 'Zayd is human', and derivative (الاشتقاق) where the predicate is derived from a root meaning, like saying 'Zayd is walking' or 'possessor of speech'. The examples of 'speech', 'laughter', and 'walking' show that these cannot be predicated of human individuals homiletically; we say 'Zayd is speaking' not 'Zayd is speech'. This underscores the distinction between essence and accidental properties. The core of the passage then proceeds to define the five universals based on the universal's relation to the essence of the particulars under it: (1) Species (نوع) is the essence itself, e.g., 'human'. (2) Genus (جنس) is part of the essence shared with another species, e.g., 'animal' is common to human and horse. (3) Differentia (فصل) is part of the essence that distinguishes one species from another, e.g., 'rational'. (4) Property (خاصة) is outside the essence but specific to one reality, e.g., 'laughing' for humans. (5) General accident (عرض عام) is outside the essence and not specific, e.g., 'walking' applies to humans, animals, etc. The classification is exhaustive: every universal must be either the essence, within it, or outside it, leading exactly to these five. This is a standard logical doctrine, and the author presents it concisely, ready for memorization and parsing.

Checkpoint
From the passage, which of the following is an example of a universal that is part of the essence and shared with another species?
A) Human
B) Animal
C) Laughing
D) Walking

Key Takeaway
Every universal term is either the essence of its particulars, inside it, or outside it—leading to exactly five categories: species, genus, differentia, property, and general accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'الصلاة' (prayer) in fiqh. Which of the five universals is it? Identify its genus and differentia if applicable.
?
Find a definition from any Islamic science (e.g., from a textbook on Kalam, Nahw, or Tafsir) and classify its components into the five universals.
